1 of 275 DOCUMENTS


Winkler v. State


Opinion No. 27685


SUPREME COURT OF SOUTH CAROLINA


418 S.C. 643; 795 S.E.2d 686; 2016 S.C. LEXIS 392


April 13, 2016, Heard 
November 23, 2016, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Rehearing denied by Winkler v. State, 2017 S.C. LEXIS 34 (S.C., Feb. 9, 2017)
US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Winkler v. S.C., 2017 U.S. LEXIS 5482 (U.S., Oct. 2, 2017)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal From Horry County. James E. Lockemy, Trial Court Judge, Benjamin H. Culbertson, Post-Conviction Relief Judge. Appellate Case No. 2014-000904.
State v. Winkler, 388 S.C. 574, 698 S.E.2d 596, 2010 S.C. LEXIS 292 (S.C., Aug. 16, 2010)



DISPOSITION: REVERSED AND REMANDED.


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: ISSUE: Whether defendant was entitled to post-conviction relief. HOLDINGS: [1]-Defendant's trial counsel was not ineffective in the sentencing phase of defendant's trial for not objecting when the trial court did not answer the jury's questions about the consequences, pursuant to S.C. Code Ann. § 16-3-20(C) (2015) (amended), of a failure to reach a unanimous verdict; [2]-The post-conviction relief (PCR) court's denial, pursuant to S.C. Code Ann. § 17-27-160(C) (2014), of defendant's pretrial motions in the PCR action, in which defendant requested additional time to obtain and analyze evidence related to his alleged brain damage, was inappropriate because the denial of additional time deprived defendant of the opportunity to adequately develop his PCR claim that trial counsel was ineffective for failing to investigate the possibility of defendant's brain damage.


OUTCOME: Judgment reversed in part and vacated in part. Case remanded for further proceedings.

 

2 of 275 DOCUMENTS


Joseph v. S.C. Dep't of Labor, Licensing & Regulation


Opinion No. 27666


SUPREME COURT OF SOUTH CAROLINA


417 S.C. 436; 790 S.E.2d 763; 2016 S.C. LEXIS 271


February 19, 2015, Heard 
September 14, 2016, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Rehearing denied by Joseph v. S.C. Dep't of Labor Licensing & Regulation, 2016 S.C. LEXIS 406 (S.C., Dec. 7, 2016)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal From Richland County. The Honorable G. Thomas Cooper, Jr., Circuit Court Judge. Appellate Case No. 2014-001115.



DISPOSITION: REVERSED.


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: ISSUE: Whether the trial court erred in its summary judgment dismissal of appellants' suit challenging the Board of Physical Therapy's position statement that the act of a physical therapist (PT) or PT assistant (PTA) who provided services to a patient at the request of another PT or PTA employed by the same practice did not constitute a "referral" under S.C. Code Ann. § 40-45-110(A)(1). HOLDINGS: [1]-The high court overruled Sloan v. South Carolina Board of Physical Therapy Examiners as an unconstitutional interpretation of § 40-45-110(A)(1), which it construed as prohibiting only referral-for-pay situations rather than all employer-employee relationships between physicians and PTs; [2]-As the Board's position statement was a "binding norm" with the effect of a regulation under the APA, it violated the APA by adopting the position statement without promulgating it as a regulation.


OUTCOME: The decision was reversed.

 

3 of 275 DOCUMENTS


S.C. Public Interest Found. v. Lucas


Opinion No. 27638


SUPREME COURT OF SOUTH CAROLINA


416 S.C. 269; 786 S.E.2d 124; 2016 S.C. LEXIS 121


March 22, 2016, Heard 
May 18, 2016, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appellate Case No. 2015-001441.



DISPOSITION: PROVISO STRICKEN.


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: HOLDINGS: [1]-A constitutional challenge by a political action committee to the 2015-16 Appropriations Act had merit, as the inclusion of 2015 S.C. Acts 91, Part 1B, § 84, Proviso 84.18, violated the "one subject" requirement and the log-rolling prohibition of S.C. Const. art. III, § 17 because it related to the suspension of the appointment power and it was not reasonably and inherently related to the raising and spending of tax monies; [2]-As the provision of the General Appropriations Act was not germane to the purpose of that Act, and striking the Act in its entirety was a drastic remedy, the court modified its holding in Am. Petroleum Inst. v. South Carolina Dep't of Rev., 382 S.C. 572 (2009), such that it excised just the offending provision.


OUTCOME: Provision excised.

 

4 of 275 DOCUMENTS


State v. Stukes


Opinion No. 27633


SUPREME COURT OF SOUTH CAROLINA


416 S.C. 493; 787 S.E.2d 480; 2016 S.C. LEXIS 100


January 13, 2016, Heard 
May 4, 2016, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Rehearing denied by State v. Stukes, 2016 S.C. LEXIS 174 (S.C., July 15, 2016)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from Richland County. The Honorable DeAndrea G. Benjamin, Circuit Court Judge. Appellate Case No. 2015-000908.
State v. Stukes, 2015 S.C. App. Unpub. LEXIS 14 (S.C. Ct. App., Jan. 14, 2015)



DISPOSITION: REVERSED AND REMANDED.


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: HOLDINGS: [1]-Where defendant was convicted of criminal sexual conduct, the trial court erred in charging the jury that the complainant's testimony did not need to be corroborated by additional evidence pursuant to S.C. Code Ann. § 16-3-657, because the charge invited the jury to believe the victim, in violation of S.C. Const. art. V, § 21, and confused the jurors, as indicated by their query to the court; [2]-As the complainant testified she was raped and defendant testified they had consensual intercourse, and the jury was clearly confused as to whether it was required to accept the complainant's testimony as truth, the error was not harmless; [3]-The high court overruled State v. Rayfield, 631 S.E.2d 244 (S.C. 2006), to the extent it condoned the use of § 16-3-657 as a jury charge.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the intermediate appellate court was reversed.

 

5 of 275 DOCUMENTS


Hueble v. S.C. Dep't of Natural Res.


Opinion No. 27631


SUPREME COURT OF SOUTH CAROLINA


416 S.C. 220; 785 S.E.2d 461; 2016 S.C. LEXIS 96


September 22, 2015, Heard 
April 27, 2016, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from Greenwood County. Eugene C. Griffith, Jr., Circuit Court Judge. Appellate Case No. 2012-212006.
Hueble v. S.C. Dep't of Natural Res., 2012 S.C. App. Unpub. LEXIS 119 (S.C. Ct. App., Feb. 15, 2012)



DISPOSITION: REVERSED AND REMANDED.


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: HOLDINGS: [1]-Where petitioner asserted § 1983 and state law claim against respondent state agency and employee, his acceptance of respondents' offer of judgment under S.C. R. Civ. P. 68 entitled him to attorneys' fees and costs as the prevailing party under 42 U.S.C.S. § 1988 as he obtained a monetary judgment, which materially altered the legal relationship between the parties by imposing an enforceable obligation against respondents; thus, the trial court erred in denying petitioner's fee request; [2]-The trial court erred in finding special circumstances sufficient to justify denying fees because the judgment for $ 5,100 was not de minimus; the fact that petitioner did not obtain his primary objective, an injunction, did not mean he had not suffered an injury; and the fact that the employee recovered on his permissive counterclaim had no bearing on the merit of petitioner's award.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the intermediate appellate court affirming the trial court's order was reversed.

 

6 of 275 DOCUMENTS


State v. Legg


Opinion No. 27628


SUPREME COURT OF SOUTH CAROLINA


416 S.C. 9; 785 S.E.2d 369; 2016 S.C. LEXIS 83


February 9, 2016, Heard 
April 20, 2016, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from Horry County. Edward B. Cottingham, Circuit Court Judge. Appellate Case No. 2014-000568.



DISPOSITION: AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: HOLDINGS: [1]-Where defendant was convicted of lewd act on a minor after the trial court allowed a videotaped forensic interview of the victim to be played for the jury, S.C. Code Ann. § 17-23-175, which allowed the admission of both the taped interview and the child's live testimony, was not facially unconstitutional on procedural due process grounds for allowing the child's testimony to be presented twice; it could be applied constitutionally if the child was questioned on direct only as to the creation of the videotape prior to its publication to the jury and cross-examination.


OUTCOME: The ruling was affirmed.

 

7 of 275 DOCUMENTS


Freeman v. J.L.H. Invs., LP


Opinion No. 27586


SUPREME COURT OF SOUTH CAROLINA


414 S.C. 362; 778 S.E.2d 902; 2015 S.C. LEXIS 367


April 8, 2015, Heard 
November 4, 2015, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal From Pickens County. Doyet A. Early, III, Circuit Court Judge. Appellate Case No. 2014-000642.



DISPOSITION: AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: HOLDINGS: [1]-The car purchaser properly sought recovery for a closing fee violation under the South Carolina Regulation of Manufacturers, Distributors, and Dealers Act (Dealers Act), S.C. Code Ann. §§ 56-15-10 to -600 (2006 & Supp. 2014). Her decision to bring the class action lawsuit under the provisions of the Dealers Act rather than S.C. R. Civ. P. 23 was permissible and did not warrant the granting of a new trial to the car dealership; [2]-Although procedural compliance with the "Closing Fee" Statute, S.C. Code Ann. § 37-2-307 of the South Carolina Consumer Protection Code (SCCPC), enabled the dealership to charge a closing fee, it was required to accurately assess the amount of the fee charged and it had to be directly tied to the closing of a motor vehicle sale; [3]-The jury's award of actual damages was properly doubled under S.C. Code Ann. § 56-15-110(1).


OUTCOME: Judgment affirmed.

 

8 of 275 DOCUMENTS


Stephens v. CSX Transp., Inc.


Opinion No. 27587


SUPREME COURT OF SOUTH CAROLINA


415 S.C. 182; 781 S.E.2d 534; 2015 S.C. LEXIS 369


March 17, 2015, Heard 
November 4, 2015, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Rehearing denied by Stephens v. CSX Transp., 2016 S.C. LEXIS 13 (S.C., Feb. 11, 2016)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal From Hampton County. Carmen T. Mullen, Circuit Court Judge. Appellate Case No. 2013-000133.
Stephens v. CSX Transp., Inc., 400 S.C. 503, 735 S.E.2d 505, 2012 S.C. App. LEXIS 207 (S.C. Ct. App., 2012)



DISPOSITION: AFFIRMED IN PART, REVERSED IN PART, AND REMANDED.


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: HOLDINGS: [1]-The appellate court erred in restricting its analysis only to those jury charge issues related to the breach of railroad corporation's and the South Carolina Department of Transportation's (SCDOT's) duty of reasonable care; [2]-Although it was necessary to provide the jury with some type of instruction regarding impaired driving since the injured child's mother admitted to consuming alcohol prior to crossing the railroad tracks, since the mother's blood alcohol content was.018%, the father was entitled to have the jury instructed on the statutory presumption provided in S.C. Code Ann. § 56-5-2950(b)(1). In the absence of that instruction, it was arguable that the jury found the mother was impaired while driving and that the criminal act negated any negligence on the part of defendants and the father was prejudiced by the judge's refusal to charge his proposed instruction.


OUTCOME: Judgment affirmed in part, reversed in part, and remanded for a new trial.

 

9 of 275 DOCUMENTS


Columbia Venture, LLC v. Richland Cnty.


Opinion No. 27563


SUPREME COURT OF SOUTH CAROLINA


413 S.C. 423; 776 S.E.2d 900; 2015 S.C. LEXIS 281


November 18, 2014, Heard 
August 12, 2015, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Rehearing denied by Columbia Venture v. Richland Cnty., 2015 S.C. LEXIS 350 (S.C., Oct. 9, 2015)
Petition for certiorari filed at, 01/07/2016
Petition denied by Columbia Venture v. Richland County, 2016 S.C. LEXIS 63 (S.C., Mar. 21, 2016)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from Richland County. Appellate Case No. 2013-001067. John Hamilton Smith, Sr., Special Referee.
Columbia Venture LLC v. S.C. Wildlife Fed'n, 562 F.3d 290, 2009 U.S. App. LEXIS 6932 (4th Cir. S.C., 2009)



DISPOSITION: AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: HOLDINGS: [1]-In a suit brought by the purchaser of a large tract of land located in a known regulated floodway, the court upheld the special referee's decision that no taking by the county occurred because the county's adoption of floodway development restrictions and its required utilization of Federal Emergency Management Agency flood data, which prevented the development of the property, did not constitute a taking of any sort; [2]-The court further concluded that the special referee correctly determined that the purchaser's lack of reasonable investment-backed expectations coupled with the legitimate and substantial health and safety-related bases for the county's floodplain development restrictions outweighed it economic injury and under case law precedent, no regulatory taking occurred.


OUTCOME: Judgment affirmed.

 

10 of 275 DOCUMENTS


State v. Anderson


Opinion No. 27558


SUPREME COURT OF SOUTH CAROLINA


413 S.C. 212; 776 S.E.2d 76; 2015 S.C. LEXIS 265


April 9, 2015, Heard 
August 5, 2015, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Rehearing denied by State v. Anderson, 2015 S.C. LEXIS 370 (S.C., Sept. 3, 2015)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from Richland County. DeAndrea G. Benjamin, Circuit Court Judge. Appellate Case No. 2012-212905.



DISPOSITION: REVERSED.


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: HOLDINGS: [1]-The trial court's admission of a child victim's videotaped forensic interview did not violate the Sixth Amendment's Confrontation Clause because the child testified under oath in open court and was subject to cross-examination, and the fact that defendant would have to recall the child as an adverse witness in order to examine her about her videotaped statement did not render S.C. Code Ann. § 17-23-175 (2014) unconstitutional; [2]-The trial court erred in qualifying a witness as an expert in forensic interviewing and child abuse assessment because South Carolina courts did not recognize expertise in forensic interviewing, the interviewer improperly vouched for the child when she testified to those characteristics she observed in the child, and the prejudice to defendant was overwhelming.


OUTCOME: Conviction and sentence reversed.

 

11 of 275 DOCUMENTS


Jayroe v. Newberry Cnty.


Opinion No. 27548


SUPREME COURT OF SOUTH CAROLINA


413 S.C. 176; 775 S.E.2d 382; 2015 S.C. LEXIS 252


June 16, 2015, Heard 
July 22, 2015, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appellate Case No. 2015-000373.



DISPOSITION: QUESTION ANSWERED "NO".


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: HOLDINGS: [1]-Although defendant county and county administrator did not have the authority under S.C. Code Ann. § 22-1-10(A) to abolish part-time magistrate positions in the county, the reallocation of the county's three magisterial positions from a combination of full- and part-time judges to three full-time magistrates was not a change in the number of magisterial positions, since under S.C. Code Ann. § 22-8-40(C) (2007), four part-time magistrates equalled one full-time magisterial position; therefore, no position had been "abolished."


OUTCOME: The high court held that defendants did not have the authority to abolish part-time magistrate positions in the county.

 

12 of 275 DOCUMENTS


State v. Odom


Opinion No. 27517


SUPREME COURT OF SOUTH CAROLINA


412 S.C. 253; 772 S.E.2d 149; 2015 S.C. LEXIS 167


May 21, 2014, Heard 
April 22, 2015, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Rehearing denied by State v. Odom, 2015 S.C. LEXIS 285 (S.C., June 5, 2015)
Magistrate's recommendation at, Habeas corpus proceeding at Odom v. Adger, 2016 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 141370 (D.S.C., May 3, 2016)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from Oconee County. Appellate Case No. 2012-206186. R. Lawton McIntosh, Circuit Court Judge.
State v. Odom, 382 S.C. 144, 676 S.E.2d 124, 2009 S.C. LEXIS 73 (S.C., 2009)



DISPOSITION: AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: ISSUE: Whether defendant was properly convicted of criminal solicitation of a minor (S.C. Code Ann. § 16-15-342) based on his Internet chats with an undercover officer. HOLDINGS: [1]-The trial court properly refused to dismiss the indictments due to vindictive prosecution, as defendant did not show vindictiveness played any role in the decision to prosecute charges in one county after defense counsel subpoenaed the officer's records in a case filed in another county; [2]-The trial court erred by using Department of Motor Vehicle records to take judicial notice of defendant's age, an element of the offense, but the error was harmless in light of properly admitted evidence, including his physical appearance, that he was 18 or older at the time of the offense; [3]-Section 16-15-342 did not violate defendant's rights to equal protection and free speech and was not unconstitutional.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

 

13 of 275 DOCUMENTS


State v. Baker


Opinion No. 27497


SUPREME COURT OF SOUTH CAROLINA


411 S.C. 583; 769 S.E.2d 860; 2015 S.C. LEXIS 63


November 15, 2012, Heard 
February 11, 2015, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Rehearing denied by State v. Baker, 2015 S.C. LEXIS 160 (S.C., Apr. 9, 2015)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal From Sumter County. The Honorable Howard P. King, Circuit Court Judge. Appellate Case No. 2010-172951.
State v. Baker, 390 S.C. 56, 700 S.E.2d 440, 2010 S.C. App. LEXIS 92 (S.C. Ct. App., 2010)



DISPOSITION: REVERSED.


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: HOLDINGS: [1]-A defendant charged with in violation of former S.C. Code Ann. § 16-15-140 (2003) was prejudiced by an indictment that covered a six-year period with no specificity that was amended two weeks before trial when the indictment had earlier covered three consecutive summers; [2]-Because alibi required proof of continuous absence, it would have been impossible for him to produce evidence of an alibi that would cover a six-year period; [3]-The court recognized the difficulty the prosecution faced in identifying exact dates in child sexual abuse cases, but held that the class of criminal case should not translate into an exception that operates to circumvent constitutional and statutory principles.


OUTCOME: Judgment reversed.

In re Collie


Opinion No. 27467


SUPREME COURT OF SOUTH CAROLINA


410 S.C. 556; 765 S.E.2d 835; 2014 S.C. LEXIS 541


October 7, 2014, Heard 
November 26, 2014, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Rehearing denied by In re Collie, 2015 S.C. LEXIS 15 (S.C., Jan. 14, 2015)
Motion denied by Collie v. S.C. Comm'n on Lawyer Conduct, 136 S. Ct. 874, 193 L. Ed. 2d 706, 2016 U.S. LEXIS 67 (U.S., 2016)
US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Collie v. S.C. Comm'n on Lawyer Conduct, 2016 U.S. LEXIS 2699 (U.S., Apr. 18, 2016)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appellate Case No. 2014-000324.
In re Collie, 406 S.C. 181, 749 S.E.2d 522, 2013 S.C. LEXIS 281 (S.C., 2013)



DISPOSITION: DEFINITE SUSPENSION.


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: HOLDINGS: [1]-Pursuant to S.C. App. Ct. R. 413:24(a), an attorney was deemed to have admitted the factual allegations set forth in the formal charges the Office of Disciplinary Counsel filed with the Commission on Lawyer Conduct due to her default for failing to respond to the formal charge; [2]-Because the attorney was deemed to have admitted the factual allegations against her, the supreme court adopted the Commission Panel's recommendation as to the finding of misconduct and thus, found that the attorney violated S.C. App. Ct. R. 407:3.1, 3.2, 3.3(a), 3.4, and 8.4(e) and that she was subject to discipline pursuant to S.C. App. Ct. R. 413:7(a)(1) and (5); [3]-The allegations of misconduct during the underlying litigation warranted the attorney's suspension from the practice of law, Rule 7(b)(2).


OUTCOME: Attorney suspended for two years.

Abbeville County Sch. Dist. v. State


Opinion No. 27466


SUPREME COURT OF SOUTH CAROLINA


410 S.C. 619; 767 S.E.2d 157; 2014 S.C. LEXIS 491


September 18, 2012, Heard 
November 12, 2014, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Rehearing denied by Abbeville County Sch. Dist. v. State, 2015 S.C. LEXIS 50 (S.C., Jan. 23, 2015)
Supplemental opinion at Abbeville County Sch. Dist. v. State, 777 S.E.2d 547, 2015 S.C. LEXIS 338 (S.C., 2015)
Supplemental opinion at Abbeville Cnty. Sch. Dist. v. State, 2015 S.C. LEXIS 382 (S.C., Nov. 5, 2015)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal From Lee County. Thomas W. Cooper, Jr., Circuit Court Judge. Appellate Case No. 2007-065159.
Abbeville County Sch. Dist. v. State, 335 S.C. 58, 515 S.E.2d 535, 1999 S.C. LEXIS 83 (S.C., 1999)



DISPOSITION: AFFIRMED AS MODIFIED.


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: HOLDINGS: [1]-The Court found favor of plaintiff school districts that the overall public school funding scheme continues to disadvantage them and that they were denied the opportunity for a minimally adequate education; [2]-The Court held that it was necessary for the parties to reappear before the Court and present a plan to address the constitutional violation announced; [3]-The Court noted that the principle of separation of powers directed that the South Carolina Legislature, not the Court, was the proper institution to make major education policy choices and that plaintiff school districts must identify the problems facing their students.


OUTCOME: Judgment affirmed, as modified.

In the Interest of Kevin R.


Opinion No. 27430


SUPREME COURT OF SOUTH CAROLINA


409 S.C. 297; 762 S.E.2d 387; 2014 S.C. LEXIS 328


April 16, 2014, Heard 
August 6, 2014, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal From Richland County. The Honorable Robert E. Newton, Family Court Judge. The Honorable Gwendlyne Y. Smalls, Family Court Judge. Appellate Case No. 2012-212655.



DISPOSITION: AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: ISSUE: Whether the trial court erred in denying a juvenile's motion for a jury trial in his delinquency proceeding. HOLDINGS: [1]-Under McKeiver v. Pennsylvania, which remained good law, a jury trial in a juvenile proceeding was not a mandated right required by concerns of fundamental fairness under the U.S. Constitution; [2]-The juvenile did not meet his burden to prove that S.C. Code § 63-3-590, which allowed only bench trials in delinquency proceedings, violated either U.S. Const. amend. VI or S.C. Const. art. I, § 14.


OUTCOME: The trial court's order was affirmed.

Bd. of Trs. for the Fairfield County Sch. Dist. v. State


Opinion No. 27417


SUPREME COURT OF SOUTH CAROLINA


409 S.C. 119; 761 S.E.2d 241; 2014 S.C. LEXIS 261


November 7, 2013, Heard 
July 16, 2014, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from Richland County. Appellate Case No. 2012-212697. J. Ernest Kinard, Jr., Circuit Court Judge.



DISPOSITION: AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: HOLDINGS: [1]-Appellant, a school district's board of trustees, sued respondents, the State, a school district, a county treasurer, and the State Department of Education, seeking a declaration that S.C. Code Ann. § 59-63-485 (Supp. 2013) was unconstitutional special legislation in violation of S.C. Const. art. III, § 34(IX). The trial court properly granted respondents summary judgment because appellant failed to present any evidence that the legislature had neither a logical basis nor sound reason for enacting § 59-63-485.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

 

18 of 275 DOCUMENTS


In the Interest of Stephen W.


Opinion No. 27413


SUPREME COURT OF SOUTH CAROLINA


409 S.C. 73; 761 S.E.2d 231; 2014 S.C. LEXIS 260


April 2, 2014, Heard 
July 16, 2014, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from Richland County. Appellate Case No. 2012-213481. Angela R. Taylor, Family Court Judge.



DISPOSITION: AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: HOLDINGS: [1]- Appellant juvenile sought review of his adjudication of delinquency for possession of marijuana, alleging the family court erred in denying his motion for a jury trial. The appellate court held that S.C. Code Ann. § 63-3-590 (2010) and S.C. R. Fam. Ct. 9(a), which mandated bench trials in juvenile proceedings, were constitutional because the U.S. Constitution did not provide a right to a jury trial in a juvenile proceeding; [2]-As the juvenile adjudication process in family court was not of a like nature or similar to the manner in which juveniles were criminally charged at the time the South Carolina Constitution was enacted, S.C. Const. art. I, § 14 did not entitle juveniles to a jury trial in family court adjudication proceedings.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

 

19 of 275 DOCUMENTS


Harrell v. AG of the State (In re State Grand Jury Investigation)


Opinion No. 27412


SUPREME COURT OF SOUTH CAROLINA


409 S.C. 60; 760 S.E.2d 808; 2014 S.C. LEXIS 257


June 24, 2014, Heard 
July 9, 2014, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal From Richland County. Appellate Case No. 2014-001058. L. Casey Manning, Circuit Court Judge.



DISPOSITION: REVERSED.


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: ISSUE: Whether the circuit court erred in discharging the state grand jury after finding it lacked jurisdiction to investigate violations of the Ethics, Government Accountability, and Campaign Reform Act, S.C. Code Ann. §§ 8-13-100 to 8-13-1520 (Supp. 2013). HOLDINGS: [1]-The circuit court erred in concluding that the House Ethics Committee had exclusive jurisdiction over the complaint against the Speaker of the House of Representatives because the Attorney General's investigation was not circumscribed by the nature of the complaint that triggered the investigation; [2]-The Committee's concurrent civil regulatory authority did not affect the Attorney General's authority to initiate a criminal investigation; [3]-The circuit court acted within its authority under S.C. Code Ann. § 14-7-1630(G) (Supp. 2013) in assessing the jurisdiction of the state grand jury beyond the impanelment stage.


OUTCOME: Order reversed and case remanded.

State v. Nation


Opinion No. 27408


SUPREME COURT OF SOUTH CAROLINA


408 S.C. 474; 759 S.E.2d 428; 2014 S.C. LEXIS 215


February 5, 2014, Heard 
July 2, 2014, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Rehearing denied by State v. Nation, 2014 S.C. LEXIS 340 (S.C., Aug. 6, 2014)
US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Nation v. South Carolina, 2015 U.S. LEXIS 1792 (U.S., Mar. 9, 2015)
Petition denied by State v. Nation, 2015 S.C. LEXIS 121 (S.C., Mar. 9, 2015)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal From Greenwood County. Appellate Case No. 2011-199726. Frank R. Addy, Jr., Circuit Court Judge.



DISPOSITION: AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: HOLDINGS: [1]-Mandatory imposition of GPS monitoring on a child sex offender who violated probation did not violate double jeopardy or constitute cruel and unusual punishment, because such monitoring was not a punishment but a civil requirement, and sex offenders subject to GPS monitoring under S.C. Code Ann. § 23-3-540 could periodically petition for judicial review of the need for continued monitoring; [2]-The sex offender's due process, ex post facto, equal protection, and Fourth Amendment claims had been considered and rejected in earlier, similar cases.


OUTCOME: Judgment affirmed.

S.C. Libertarian Party v. S.C. State Election Comm'n


Opinion No. 27384


SUPREME COURT OF SOUTH CAROLINA


407 S.C. 612; 757 S.E.2d 707; 2014 S.C. LEXIS 133


April 24, 2014, Heard 
April 24, 2014, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appellate Case No. 2014-000775.



DISPOSITION: ORIGINAL JURISDICTION.


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: HOLDINGS: [1]-While the contingency placed in section 14 of the Equal Access to the Ballot Act, 2013 S.C. Acts 61, had been met, a political party was not entitled to an order that the State Election Commission conduct a primary for it because the party had always utilized the convention method of nominating candidates and the Act did not require the party to adopt the primary nomination process in S.C. Code Ann. § 7-11-30 in order to retain its convention method of nomination.


OUTCOME: Petition granted in part and denied in part, and Act declared in effect.

Amisub of S.C., Inc. v. S.C. Dep't of Health & Envtl. Control


Opinion No. 27382


SUPREME COURT OF SOUTH CAROLINA

Appellate Case No. 2013-001530.



DISPOSITION: RELIEF GRANTED.


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: HOLDINGS: [1]-The Department of Health and Human Services (DHEC) had a duty under S.C. Code Ann. § 44-7-140 of the Certification of Need and Health Facility Licensure Act, S.C. OUTCOME: Relief granted.

Holmes v. E. Cooper Cmty. Hosp., Inc.


Opinion No. 27370


SUPREME COURT OF SOUTH CAROLINA


408 S.C. 138; 758 S.E.2d 483; 2014 S.C. LEXIS 89


March 19, 2013, Heard 
March 26, 2014, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Rehearing denied by Holmes v. East Cooper Cmty. Hosp., 2014 S.C. LEXIS 206 (S.C., June 13, 2014)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appellate Case No. 2011-198092; Appellate Case No. 2012-209666. Appeal From Charleston County. Kristi Lea Harrington, Circuit Court Judge.
Holmes v. Tenet Healthsystem Med., Inc., 238 F.3d 412, 2000 U.S. App. LEXIS 36317 (4th Cir. S.C., 2000)



DISPOSITION: AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: HOLDINGS: [1]-The trial court properly granted respondents summary judgment dismissing petitioner's suit alleging they violated the parties' settlement agreement by denying her applications for surgical privileges. She was collaterally estopped from bringing the suit because she had unsuccessfully made the same legal arguments in her prior suits against respondents; [2]-Petitioner lacked standing to argue that the South Carolina Frivolous Civil Proceedings Sanctions Act denied litigants procedural due process by holding pro se litigants to an attorney's standard of expertise, as she was herself a licensed attorney and had been represented by counsel; [3]-She was properly sanctioned under S.C. Code Ann. § 15-36-10(C)(1), as she had made the same unsuccessful legal arguments in prior litigation, and repeatedly challenged respondents' credentialing decisions without a legal basis to do so.


OUTCOME: The summary judgment order and sanctions order were affirmed.

State v. Middleton


Opinion No. 27358


SUPREME COURT OF SOUTH CAROLINA


407 S.C. 312; 755 S.E.2d 432; 2014 S.C. LEXIS 52


March 20, 2013, Heard 
February 26, 2014, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Rehearing denied by State v. Middleton, 2014 S.C. LEXIS 107 (S.C., Apr. 2, 2014)
US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Middleton v. South Carolina, 135 S. Ct. 196, 190 L. Ed. 2d 152, 2014 U.S. LEXIS 5284 (U.S., 2014)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeal From Colleton County. Appellate Case No. 2011-196767. Perry M. Buckner, Circuit Court Judge.



DISPOSITION: AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: ISSUE: Whether trial court erred when it refused to instruct the jury on the lesser-included offense of assault and battery in the first degree. HOLDINGS: [1]-The trial judge misconstrued the statutory definition of assault and battery in the first degree as requiring an injury to the victim: [2]-It was undisputed that the elements of S.C. Code Ann. § 16-3-600(C)(1)(b) were met; [3]-The trial court's error was harmless beyond a reasonable doubt; [4]-None of the cases cited by the dissent stood for the proposition that a harmless error analysis was inappropriate where the judge erroneously failed to charge the lesser included offense; [5]-Any error in failing to charge the lesser-included offense was harmless because the erroneous instruction did not contribute to the verdict beyond a reasonable doubt.


OUTCOME: Defendant's convictions and sentences were affirmed.

Disabato v. S.C. Ass'n of Sch. Adm'rs


Opinion No. 27286


SUPREME COURT OF SOUTH CAROLINA


404 S.C. 433; 746 S.E.2d 329; 2013 S.C. LEXIS 171; 41 Media L. Rep. 2430


October 3, 2012, Heard 
July 17, 2013, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Counsel Amended July 24, 2013.


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appellate Case No. 2011-198146. Appeal from Richland County. G. Thomas Cooper, Jr., Circuit Court Judge.



DISPOSITION: REVERSED.


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: ISSUE: Whether the trial court erred in dismissing appellant's complaint under the South Carolina Freedom of Information Act, S.C. Code Ann, tit. 30, ch. 4, against a non-profit corporation engaged in political advocacy. HOLDINGS: [1]-The trial court correctly found that the Act burdened the corporation's freedoms of speech and association under the First Amendment, U.S. Const. amend. I; [2]-The trial court erred in applying the exacting or strict scrutiny standard to the Act, instead of intermediate scrutiny and the similar Clingman v. Beaver standard; [3]-The trial court erred in finding that the Act infringed on the corporation's First Amendment speech and association rights, as the Act served important governmental interests unrelated to the suppression of free speech and did not burden substantially more speech than necessary to advance those interests.


OUTCOME: The judgment was reversed.

Barton v. S.C. Dep't of Prob. Parole & Pardon Servs.


Opinion No. 27281


SUPREME COURT OF SOUTH CAROLINA


404 S.C. 395; 745 S.E.2d 110; 2013 S.C. LEXIS 162


May 1, 2013, Heard 
July 3, 2013, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeal From The Administrative Law Court. Appellate Case No. 2012-213234. S. Phillip Lenski, Administrative Law Judge.



DISPOSITION: REVERSED.


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: ISSUE: Whether the administrative law court (ALC) erred in affirming the South Carolina Parole Board's decision to deny parole to appellant inmate, who had been convicted of murder and sentenced to life imprisonment. HOLDINGS: [1]-The Board committed an ex post facto violation by not applying the former version of S.C. Code Ann. § 24-21-645 (Supp. 1984), which required only a majority vote to grant parole, instead of the current version, which required a two-thirds vote where the conviction was for a violent crime, because the crime was committed before § 24-21-645 was amended; [2]-The ALC and Board erred in interpreting current § 24-21-645 (Supp. 2012) as requiring an inmate convicted of a violent crime to obtain votes from two-thirds of the seven-member Board; instead, she needed, and obtained, the votes of only two-thirds of those Board members participating in the hearing.


OUTCOME: The judgment was reversed.

S.C. Pub. Interest Found. v. S.C. Transp. Infrastructure Bank


Opinion No. 27266


SUPREME COURT OF SOUTH CAROLINA


403 S.C. 640; 744 S.E.2d 521; 2013 S.C. LEXIS 132


June 19, 2012, Heard 
June 12, 2013, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appellate Case No. 2012-207128.



DISPOSITION: JUDGMENT FOR RESPONDENTS.


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: HOLDINGS: [1]-In their suit against respondents--the State, the South Carolina Transportation Infrastructure Bank, and state legislators--petitioners, a public interest group and an individual, had standing challenge the Bank's constitutionality, as the issue was a matter of public importance; [2]-S.C. Code Ann. § 11-43-140, which provided that legislators made up two of the seven members of the Bank's Board of Directors, did not violate state constitutional provisions barring dual office holding, as the ex officio exception applied since there was a sufficient constitutional nexus between the powers and responsibilities of the Directors and legislators; [3]-Section 11-43-140 did not violate separation of powers principles since legislators were a minority of the Board, and the Board's composition enabled it to benefit from the legislator members' wisdom without being dominated by them.


OUTCOME: Judgment entered for respondents.

 

31 of 275 DOCUMENTS


State v. Dykes


Opinion No. 27124


SUPREME COURT OF SOUTH CAROLINA


403 S.C. 499; 744 S.E.2d 505; 2013 S.C. LEXIS 111


September 18, 2012, Heard 
May 22, 2013, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Rehearing denied by State v. Dykes, 2013 S.C. LEXIS 245 (S.C., July 24, 2013)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appellate Case No. 2010-160047. Appeal from Pickens County. Charles B. Simmons, Jr., Special Circuit Court Judge.
State v. Dykes, 398 S.C. 351, 728 S.E.2d 455, 2012 S.C. LEXIS 103 (S.C., 2012)



DISPOSITION: AFFIRMED AS MODIFIED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant child sex offender convicted of a lewd act on a minor under S.C. Code Ann. § 16-15-140 (2006) CSO) appealed her mandatory enrollment in the satellite monitoring program (SMP) for the rest of her life under S.C. Code Ann. § 23-3-540(C) and (H) (Supp. 2011) by the Pickens County Circuit Court, South Carolina, as part of her penalty for violating her probation.


OVERVIEW: The state's highest court held that the CSO did not have a fundamental right to be let alone under the Due Process Clause, U.S. Const. amend. XIV, § 1. The CSO's mandatory enrollment in the SMP invoked minimal due process protection as it placed significant restraints on offenders that amounted to a liberty interest and the CSO had served her sentence. The stated purpose for § 23-3-540 of protecting the public from sex offenders and aiding law enforcement satisfied the rational relationship test for the initial mandatory imposition of satellite monitoring for certain child-sex crimes and § 23-3-540(C) was constitutional. The final sentence of § 23-3540(H) was unconstitutional as the absence of an opportunity for judicial review to assess a risk of re-offending for persons convicted of engaging in or attempting criminal sexual conduct with a minor in the first degree and lewd act was arbitrary and was not rationally related to the statute's purpose. The remainder of the statute did not have to be invalidated. The CSO had to comply with the monitoring requirement, but she could avail herself of the § 23-3-540(H) judicial review process outlined for the § 23-3-540(G) offenses.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the trial court was affirmed, as modified.

 

32 of 275 DOCUMENTS


Hampton v. Haley


Opinion No. 27244


SUPREME COURT OF SOUTH CAROLINA


403 S.C. 395; 743 S.E.2d 258; 2013 S.C. LEXIS 109


January 23, 2013, Heard 
May 8, 2013, Decided 
May 8, 2013, Refiled


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appellate Case No. 2012-212723.
Hampton v. Haley, 2013 S.C. LEXIS 84 (S.C., Apr. 24, 2013)



DISPOSITION: JUDGMENT FOR PETITIONERS.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioners, who were officers of state education and state employee associations, filed a petition for original jurisdiction and a complaint, challenging the decision by respondent, the South Carolina Budget and Control Board, to split the premium increase for a statewide, group health insurance plan equally between the State of South Carolina and enrollees.


OVERVIEW: The South Carolina Budget and Control Board decided to split the premium increase for a statewide, group health insurance plan equally between the State of South Carolina and enrollees. The officers of state education and employee associations argued that the Board did not have the power, except in limited circumstances, which were not applicable, to raise premiums for enrollees. They contended that the Board thus violated the separation of powers required by the South Carolina Constitution because it substituted its policy choices for those enacted by the South Carolina General Assembly. The Board contend that it had complete discretion to take the challenged action because the General Assembly simply appropriated funds without any indication for what the funds were appropriated. The court found that the Board violated the separation of powers provision, S.C. Const. art. I, § 8, by substituting its own policy for that of the General Assembly. Additionally, S.C. Code Ann. § 1-11-710 (1992) (amended 2012) mandated the expenditure of the funds appropriated for premium increases. Thus, the Board did not have the power to decline to spend all of the appropriated funds.


OUTCOME: The court entered judgment for the officers and declared that the Board's premium increase was unconstitutional as a violation of the separation of powers. The court directed the Board to apply the appropriated funds to the plan's premium increases.

 

33 of 275 DOCUMENTS


Bodman v. State


Opinion No. 27248


SUPREME COURT OF SOUTH CAROLINA


403 S.C. 60; 742 S.E.2d 363; 2013 S.C. LEXIS 89


November 29, 2011, Heard 
May 8, 2013, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appellate Case No. 2011-187466.



DISPOSITION: JUDGMENT FOR DEFENDANTS.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff taxpayer filed suit under S.C. App. Ct. R. 245 seeking to strike down the exemptions and caps to the state sales tax in toto because they were so numerous they no longer bore a rational relationship to their original purpose, arguing that S.C. Code Ann. §§ 12-36-2110 and 12-36-2120 (Supp. 2011) violated the Equal Protection Clause, S.C. Const. art. I, § 3, and constituted special legislation under S.C. Const. art. III, § 34, cl. IX.


OVERVIEW: The state's highest court held that the taxpayer lacked statutory and constitutional standing. Even if he had standing under the public importance exception, his claims failed. Sections 12-36-2110 and 12-36-2120 created 85 classification for equal protection purposes. The state's highest court could not determine whether each classification was supported by a rational basis as the taxpayer argued solely that the sheer number of exemptions and caps rendered the statutes arbitrary and unconstitutional. While the exemptions might be arbitrary in the political or economic sense, that did not mean they were arbitrary in the constitutional sense. The taxpayer's claim that the number of exemptions showed that they were arbitrary had been rejected. The taxpayer did not show that the classifications were not supported by a rational basis. The claim that the statutes constituted special legislation rested solely on the number of caps and exemptions. The state's highest court was concerned not with size or volume, but with content. Because the taxpayer insisted that the content of the caps and exemptions not be examined, he failed to prove they were unconstitutional.


OUTCOME: A judgment was entered in favor of defendants, the State of South Carolina and the South Carolina Department of Revenue.

 

34 of 275 DOCUMENTS


Hampton v. Haley


Opinion No. 27244


SUPREME COURT OF SOUTH CAROLINA


2013 S.C. LEXIS 84


January 23, 2013, Heard 
April 24, 2013, Filed


NOTICE:

THIS DECISION IS NOT FINAL UNTIL TIME EXPIRES TO FILE REHEARING MOTION AND, IF FILED, DETERMINED.


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Opinion withdrawn by, Substituted opinion at Hampton v. Haley, 403 S.C. 395, 743 S.E.2d 258, 2013 S.C. LEXIS 109 (S.C., May 8, 2013)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [*1] 
Appellate Case No. 2012-212723. ORIGINAL JURISDICTION.



DISPOSITION: JUDGMENT FOR PETITIONERS.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The South Carolina Budget and Control Board split a premium increase for the state employee group health insurance plan between the State and enrollees. Petitioner enrollees filed a declaratory judgment suit seeking an injunction prohibiting the increase of their insurance premiums and compelling the Board to use the funds appropriated in the 2012-2013 South Carolina Appropriations Act (AA) for premium increases.


OVERVIEW: The state's highest court held that the AA appropriated $ 51,528,219 for health insurance employer contributions. Although the Board members acknowledged that the South Carolina legislature fully funded the premium increases, they split the premium increase equally between the State and the enrollees. The Board violated the separation of powers doctrine under S.C. Const. art. I, § 8 since it substituted its policy choices for the legislature's. In light of the AA and S.C. Code Ann. § 1-11-710(A)(1) (Supp. 2012), the legislature mandated the appropriated funds be spent in full on the premium increases and afforded the Board no discretion. To allow the Board to decline appropriated funds based on its own policy choices would have been an impermissible delegation of the legislature's powers under the nondelegation doctrine. Statutes could not be construed to be unconstitutional when susceptible to a constitutional interpretation. The enrollees did not face irreparable harm as the premiums paid could be returned and the declaratory judgment provided the enrollees with an adequate remedy at law. An injunction was not required.


OUTCOME: A judgment was entered for the enrollees declaring that the Board's premium increase was unconstitutional as a violation of the separation of powers doctrine and directing the Board to apply the appropriated funds to the plan's premium increases and to return all premium increases collected from the enrollees. The request for injunctive relief was denied.

S.C. Dep't of Soc. Servs. v. Sarah W.


Opinion No. 27235


SUPREME COURT OF SOUTH CAROLINA


402 S.C. 324; 741 S.E.2d 739; 2013 S.C. LEXIS 48


July 11, 2012, Heard 
March 20, 2013, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Rehearing denied by SCDSS v. Sarah W., 2013 S.C. LEXIS 174 (S.C., May 15, 2013)
US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Sarah W. v. Sc, 2013 U.S. LEXIS 6350 (U.S., Oct. 7, 2013)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeal From Saluda County. Appellate Case No. 2012-208546. Richard W. Chewning III, Family Court Judge.
S.C. Dep't of Soc. Servs. v. Sarah W., 2011 S.C. App. Unpub. LEXIS 650 (S.C. Ct. App., Nov. 29, 2011)



DISPOSITION: REVERSED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner, the South Carolina Department of Social Services (DSS), challenged a court of appeals (South Carolina) decision reversing a family court order terminating respondent biological mother's parental rights pursuant to S.C. Code Ann. § 63-7-2570(8).


OVERVIEW: The mother challenged the constitutionality of § 63-7-2570(8). The supreme court disagreed, finding that § 63-7-2570(8) provided the requisite level of due process to preserve a parent's fundamental rights in a termination proceeding while at the same time recognizing the State's compelling interest in providing for the health and welfare of children who faced abuse, neglect, or abandonment. The court of appeals erred in finding that the DSS did not meet its burden of proving termination of the mother's parental rights was in the children's best interests. While at times she had taken steps to remedy the situation leading to removal of the children, she had failed to make the necessary lifestyle changes to provide them with a safe and stable environment. She refused to take responsibility for her drug activity, and failed to show that she could provide for the children without the help of the children's father. The father admitted that he could not maintain adequate housing and employment, and stipulated to prior sexual abuse of a minor, but the mother continued to cohabitate with him, even up until oral argument of the case.


OUTCOME: The decision of the court of appeals was reversed and the DSS was directed to immediately implement a plan consistent with the findings of the family court.

 

36 of 275 DOCUMENTS


State v. Harrison


Opinion No. 27228


SUPREME COURT OF SOUTH CAROLINA


402 S.C. 288; 741 S.E.2d 727; 2013 S.C. LEXIS 30


October 30, 2012, Heard 
February 27, 2013, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Rehearing denied by State v. Harrison, 2013 S.C. LEXIS 66 (S.C., Apr. 3, 2013)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeal From Greenwood County. Appellate Case No. 2010-178686. Frank R. Addy, Jr., Circuit Court Judge.



DISPOSITION: AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: After a jury trial, defendant was convicted of driving under suspension and of leaving the scene of an accident with death (S.C. Code Ann. § 56-5-1210 (2006)). The Greenwood County Circuit Court (South Carolina) sentenced him to 20 years' imprisonment for leaving the scene, and a concurrent sentence of six months' imprisonment for driving under suspension. He appealed.


OVERVIEW: After causing an accident that killed a motorcyclist, defendant kept on driving. The victim's traveling companion, another motorcyclist, followed defendant until he pulled over. The companion told defendant about the accident; defendant agreed to return to the scene, but instead drove off. He argued that the penalty portion of § 56-5-1210 violated the Eighth Amendment, U.S. Const. amend. VIII, because the gravity of his offense was not proportionate to the severity of the punishment, and that the trial court erred in ruling to the contrary. The high court disagreed. The trial court's statements at sentencing were the very embodiment of proportionality, and it performed the analysis envisioned by § 56-5-1210 in sentencing defendant based on the facts and circumstances of the case. Despite defendant's reprehensible behavior, the trial court did not sentence him to the maximum punishment allowed under § 56-5-1210. As the legislature could have rationally concluded that leaving the scene of an accident where death resulted posed a risk substantial enough to support the penalty portion of § 56-5-1210, that provision was not grossly disproportionate to the offense.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

 

37 of 275 DOCUMENTS


State v. Rice


Opinion No. 27210


SUPREME COURT OF SOUTH CAROLINA


401 S.C. 330; 737 S.E.2d 485; 2013 S.C. LEXIS 5


October 1, 2012, Submitted 
January 16, 2013, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appellate Case No. 2009-141166. Appeal from Anderson County. J. Cordell Maddox, Jr., Circuit Court Judge.



DISPOSITION: AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant juvenile pleaded guilty to armed robbery and assault with intent to kill in the Circuit Court, Anderson County (South Carolina). On appeal, he sought to resurrect his family court constitutional challenge to the waiver from family court to general sessions court as violative of Apprendi.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was charged as a juvenile. When defendant pleaded guilty he raised no objection to the family court waiver. Defendant argued that the South Carolina's juvenile transfer law violated his Sixth Amendment right to a jury trial and due process of law under Apprendi. The supreme court held that because South Carolina permitted only unconditional guilty pleas, and no jurisdictional claim was presented, defendant waived his right to assert a claim based on Apprendi. An erroneous order transferring a juvenile to general sessions court would be a judicial error, not a jurisdictional error. Defendant's Apprendi challenge failed on the merits. Apprendi was not applicable to a family court juvenile waiver hearing, for the decision whether to waive a juvenile to general sessions court in no manner determined the juvenile's guilt, innocence, or punishment. It merely determined the forum in which the case was to be tried.


OUTCOME: The supreme court affirmed the judgment.

 

38 of 275 DOCUMENTS


Dunes W. Golf Club, LLC v. Town of Mt. Pleasant


Opinion No. 27208


SUPREME COURT OF SOUTH CAROLINA


401 S.C. 280; 737 S.E.2d 601; 2013 S.C. LEXIS 2


April 3, 2012, Heard 
January 9, 2013, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appellate Case No. 2011-194211. Appeal from Charleston County. R. Markley Dennis, Jr., Circuit Court Judge.



DISPOSITION: AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant property owner sought review of a summary judgment from the Circuit Court of Charleston County (South Carolina), which ruled that respondent town had no liability on the owner's claims alleging violations of its equal protection and due process rights and an unconstitutional taking of its property.


OVERVIEW: In creating an open space zoning district, the town imposed land-use restrictions on all golf course properties, permitting only recreation and conservation uses. The town denied the owner's request to have its golf course property rezoned to allow residential development. The town granted a rezoning petition submitted by developers who owned another golf course. The court found no equal protection violation under U.S. Const. amend. XIV, § 1 because there were significant differences between the two rezoning petitions and no discriminatory animus was shown. The other developers provided more detail about their proposal, which made fewer changes to golf course areas of play and had general community support. There was no substantive due process violation, as determined under an arbitrary and capricious standard, because the town's exercise of its police power in creating the zoning district was rationally related to its legitimate land-use goals and the rezoning applied to all golf courses. Neither a categorical nor a regulatory taking occurred; the entire area was the relevant parcel, its use as a golf course would continue, and it retained significant value after the rezoning.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the circuit court's grant of summary judgment in favor of the town.

 

39 of 275 DOCUMENTS


Storm M. H. v. Charleston County Bd. of Trs.


Opinion No. 27201


SUPREME COURT OF SOUTH CAROLINA


400 S.C. 478; 735 S.E.2d 492; 2012 S.C. LEXIS 287


October 19, 2011, Heard 
December 12, 2012, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeal From Charleston County. J. C. Nicholson, Jr., Circuit Court Judge.



DISPOSITION: AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In a declaratory judgment action, appellant parent, on behalf of a student, and respondent superintendent, both sought review of the order of the Circuit Court, Berkeley County (South Carolina) which determined the student could enroll in a Magnet High School located in the Charleston County School District.


OVERVIEW: In terms of admission requirements, the school's brochure/application stated that students had to be residents of Charleston County and complete an application. The student resided with her parents in Berkeley County. The student was accepted by the school and confirmed her intention to provide a Charleston County residence address prior to enrollment. The court agreed with the Board that the General Assembly conferred discretionary authority on a board of trustees to set attendance criteria for particular schools and to determine which school in its district a student may attend. However, the Board's subsequent attempt to rescind that decision was unavailing because, in making its decision, the Board relied on the unlawful policy purporting to mandate Charleston County residency as a requirement for acceptance at the school. The "resident only" criterion ran afoul of S.C. Code Ann. § 59-63-30 because any child meeting the threshold established by that provision, either as a resident or a property owner of the subject school district, was entitled to attend that district's schools. Thus, the student was entitled to continue attending the school.


OUTCOME: The order of the trial court granting declaratory judgment in favor of the parent was affirmed.

 

40 of 275 DOCUMENTS


State v. Langford


Opinion No. 27195


SUPREME COURT OF SOUTH CAROLINA


400 S.C. 421; 735 S.E.2d 471; 2012 S.C. LEXIS 278


April 18, 2012, Heard 
November 21, 2012, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Related proceeding at State v. Phillips, 2012 S.C. Unpub. LEXIS 64 (S.C., Nov. 21, 2012)
Rehearing denied by State v. Langford, 2012 S.C. LEXIS 320 (S.C., Dec. 20, 2012)
US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Langford v. South Carolina, 2013 U.S. LEXIS 5561 (U.S., Oct. 7, 2013)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeal From Edgefield County. Appellate Case No. 2010-173128. William P. Keesley, Circuit Court Judge.



DISPOSITION: AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: After a jury trial in the Edgefield County Circuit Court (South Carolina), defendant was convicted of armed robbery, kidnapping, first-degree burglary, and civil conspiracy. He appealed.


OVERVIEW: Defendant's case was not tried until nearly two years after his arrest. After his appeal was perfected, the state public defender association filed an amicus brief challenging the constitutionality of S.C. Code Ann. § 1-7-330 (2005), which vested control of the criminal docket in the circuit solicitor. Although this issue had not been raised by defendant, the high court elected to address it because of its public importance. As § 1-7-330 was at odds with the court's inherent judicial power to control the order of its business, it violated the separation of powers principle embodied in S.C. Const. art. I, § 8 and was unconstitutional. However, defendant did not suffer any prejudice from the solicitor's controlling when his case would be called for trial. It rejected his claim that the solicitor, part of the executive branch, engaged in judge-shopping, as there was no evidence the trial court ruled in favor of the State on various issues out of animus towards defendant or allegiance to the State. The two-year delay in bringing the case to trial did not violate defendant's constitutional right to a speedy trial because there was no evidence of any actual prejudice to his case.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

 

41 of 275 DOCUMENTS


Town of Mt. Pleasant v. Chimento


Opinion No. 27197


SUPREME COURT OF SOUTH CAROLINA


401 S.C. 522; 737 S.E.2d 830; 2012 S.C. LEXIS 273


October 19, 2010, Heard 
November 21, 2012, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Rehearing denied by Town of Mt. Pleasant v. Chimento, 2013 S.C. LEXIS 7 (S.C., Jan. 10, 2013)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeal from Charleston County. R. Markley Dennis, Jr., Circuit Court Judge.



DISPOSITION: REVERSED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants were convicted in municipal court of violating the gaming statute, S.C. Code Ann. § 16-19-40(a) (2003). They appealed; the Charleston County Circuit Court (South Carolina) reversed. The town appealed.


OVERVIEW: Defendants were found playing Texas Hold'em and gambling in a rented home. The tenant took a "rake" out of the pot to cover the cost of refreshments he provided. While the municipal court found, based on expert testimony, that Texas Hold'em was a game of skill, as it found no clear indication that the legislature intended to criminalize only gambling on games of chance, it convicted defendants of violating § 16-19-40(a). The intermediate appellate court held that defendants were entitled to directed verdicts because a residence could not qualify as a "house used as a place of gaming" under § 16-19-40; alternatively, it held that § 16-19-40(a) was unconstitutionally overbroad and void for vagueness. The instant court held that since the house was used "as a place of gaming," and betting on a game of skill was a violation if done in a prohibited location, defendants violated § 16-19-40 and had not been entitled to directed verdicts. Because § 16-19-40(a) did not offend the First Amendment, U.S. Const. amend. I, it was not overbroad. Defendants lacked standing to assert that § 16-19-40(a) was void for vagueness, as their conduct clearly fell within the statutory proscription.


OUTCOME: The intermediate appellate court's order was reversed.

 

42 of 275 DOCUMENTS


Harleysville Mut. Ins. Co. v. State


Opinion No. 27189


SUPREME COURT OF SOUTH CAROLINA


401 S.C. 15; 736 S.E.2d 651; 2012 S.C. LEXIS 270


March 7, 2012, Heard 
November 21, 2012, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
ORIGINAL JURISDICTION.
Crossmann Cmtys. of N.C. v. Harleysville Mut. Ins. Co., 395 S.C. 40, 717 S.E.2d 589, 2011 S.C. LEXIS 277 (S.C., 2011)



DISPOSITION: ACT DECLARED UNCONSTITUTIONAL IN PART.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner insurer filed a petition challenging the constitutionality of 2011 S.C. Acts 88 (Act No. 26), codified at S.C. Code Ann. § 38-61-70 (Supp. 2011).


OVERVIEW: Act No. 26 regulated coverage provided by commercial general liability (CGL) insurance policies for construction-related work. The high court held the legislature did not violate the separation of powers doctrine, since by enacting Act No. 26, it did not retroactively overrule the court's interpretation of a statute. Under the rational basis test, Act No. 26 was not improper special legislation under S.C. Const. art. III, § 34, cl. IX, and did not violate the state and federal Equal Protection Clauses, as the legislature had a logical reason for enacting it. Though the legislature had the power to define the meaning of "occurrence" in CGL policies, it was a violation of the South Carolina and federal Contract Clauses to apply this new definition retroactively, as Act No. 26 substantially impaired preexisting contractual relationships by mandating that all CGL policies be legislatively amended to include a new statutory definition of "occurrence" and by applying this mandate retroactively. As the legislature did not need to enact Act No. 26 in order to address an emergency, its retroactivity provision was neither necessary nor reasonable, and, therefore, was unconstitutional.


OUTCOME: The high court found the retroactivity portion of Act No. 26, codified at § 38-61-70(E), unconstitutional, and severed that portion from the body of Act No. 26, which was otherwise constitutional.

Howard v. S.C. Dep't of Corr.


Opinion No. 27170


SUPREME COURT OF SOUTH CAROLINA


399 S.C. 618; 733 S.E.2d 211; 2012 S.C. LEXIS 184


December 1, 2011, Submitted 
September 12, 2012, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeal From Richland County. John McLeod, Administrative Law Court Judge.



DISPOSITION: AFFIRMED AS MODIFIED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant inmate challenging a prison disciplinary conviction by respondent, the South Carolina Department of Corrections (SCDC), for preparing a postconviction relief application for an illiterate inmate. The South Carolina Administrative Law Court (ALC) summarily dismissed the appeal for lack of jurisdiction pursuant to S.C. Code Ann. § 1-23-600(D) (Supp. 2011). The inmate sought further review.


OVERVIEW: The inmate challenged the SCDC's policy barring him from providing legal assistance to another inmate. The ALC held it lacked jurisdiction under § 1-23-600(D) to hear appeals in which inmates did not lose good time. As the loss of the opportunity to earn sentence-related credits did not implicate a State-created liberty interest, the high court overruled Furtick v. South Carolina Department of Corrections, 649 S.E.2d 35 (2007). The ALC erred in summarily dismissing the appeal, as it had jurisdiction to rule on the inmate's as-applied constitutional challenge to the policy. But as he did not allege there was no other source of aid for illiterate inmates, he did not show the policy violated Johnson v. Avery. He lacked standing to challenge the adequacy of the law library or library personnel. That he was not allowed to question his accuser or to present evidence to refute the charge did not violate his due process rights, as 1) no liberty interest was involved, 2) inmates had no constitutional right to confront and cross-examine witnesses, 3) he admitted the prohibited conduct, and 4) any defenses could have been raised and reviewed pursuant to the internal prison grievance process.


OUTCOME: The high court affirmed the ALC's dismissal of the appeal.

Adoptive Couple v. Baby Girl


Opinion No. 27148


SUPREME COURT OF SOUTH CAROLINA


398 S.C. 625; 731 S.E.2d 550; 2012 S.C. LEXIS 212


April 17, 2012, Heard 
July 26, 2012, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Rehearing denied by Adoptive Couple v. Baby Girl, 2012 S.C. LEXIS 176 (S.C., Aug. 22, 2012)
US Supreme Court certiorari granted by, Motion granted by Adoptive Couple v. Baby Girl, 133 S. Ct. 831, 184 L. Ed. 2d 646, 2013 U.S. LEXIS 11 (U.S., 2013)
Reversed by, Remanded by Adoptive Couple v. Baby Girl, 2013 U.S. LEXIS 4916 (U.S., June 25, 2013)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeal from Charleston County. The Hon. Deborah Malphrus, Family Court Judge.



DISPOSITION: AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant adoptive parents sought review of the order of the Charleston County Court (South Carolina) which denied the private adoption and required them to transfer a child to appellee biological father, a member of the Cherokee Nation, based on the Federal Indian Child Welfare Act, 25 U.S.C.S. § 1901 et seq. (ICWA). The child presently resided with her biological father and his parents in Oklahoma.


OVERVIEW: The biological mother and father did not live together prior to the baby's birth. The father did not support the mother financially. The mother sent a text message to the father asking if he would rather pay child support or surrender his parental rights. The father responded via text message that he would relinquish his rights, but testified that he believed he was relinquishing his rights to the mother. The family court held that the father met the ICWA's definition of "parent" by both acknowledging his paternity through the pursuit of court proceedings as soon as he realized the child had been placed up for adoption and establishing his paternity through DNA testing. The appellate court agreed. The adoptive parents did not follow the clear procedural directives of 25 U.S.C.S. § 1913(a) in obtaining the father's consent. Moreover, even if that "consent" was valid, the Father's subsequent legal campaign to obtain custody rendered any such consent withdrawn. Therefore, neither the father's signature on the "Acceptance of Service" document, nor his stated intentions to relinquish his rights, were effectual forms of voluntary consent under the ICWA.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the family court was affirmed.

 

46 of 275 DOCUMENTS


State v. Whitner


Opinion No. 27142


SUPREME COURT OF SOUTH CAROLINA


399 S.C. 547; 732 S.E.2d 861; 2012 S.C. LEXIS 138


October 18, 2011, Heard 
July 11, 2012, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Related proceeding at, Magistrate's recommendation at Whitner v. Div. of Appellate Def., 2015 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 94626 (D.S.C., June 30, 2015)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeal from Greenville County. John C. Few, Circuit Court Judge.
Whitner v. Sustakovitch, 2012 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 41663 (D.S.C., Mar. 27, 2012)



DISPOSITION: AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: After a panel of the intermediate appellate court denied defendant's motion to suppress a tape recording of his telephone conversation with the victim, a jury in the Greenville County Circuit Court (South Carolina) found him guilty of criminal sexual conduct with a minor in the first degree. He appealed.


OVERVIEW: Defendant's daughter testified that defendant molested her when she was five or six. Defendant argued that the panel erred in denying his motion to suppress a recording of his phone conversation with the victim wherein he admitted the abuse, because the recording, intercepted without the prior consent of either party, violated the South Carolina Homeland Security Act (Wiretap Act), S.C. Code Ann. § 17-30-10 et. seq. (Supp. 2010). The high court disagreed. The Wiretap Act allowed the victim's mother, as her guardian, to vicariously consent on the victim's behalf to record the phone conversation. The evidence supported the panel's finding that the mother had a good faith basis for recording the conversation, as she testified she believed this would help her decide if the victim needed counseling. Interception of the phone conversation was an not unreasonable invasion of privacy under S.C. Const. art. I, § 10. A forensic videotape of an interview with the victim was properly admitted under S.C. Code Ann. § 17-23-175 (Supp. 2010), as the forensic interviewer did not improperly lead the victim, she answered the questions on her own accord, and the tape included no bolstering testimony.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

 

47 of 275 DOCUMENTS


State v. Dykes


Opinion No. 27124


SUPREME COURT OF SOUTH CAROLINA


398 S.C. 351; 728 S.E.2d 455; 2012 S.C. LEXIS 103


September 22, 2011, Heard 
May 9, 2012, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Later proceeding at State v. Dykes, 2012 S.C. LEXIS 117 (S.C., June 8, 2012)
Subsequent appeal at State v. Dykes, 2013 S.C. LEXIS 111 (S.C., May 22, 2013)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeal from Greenville County. Charles B. Simmons, Jr., Special Circuit Court Judge.



DISPOSITION: REVERSED AND REMANDED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant sex offender, convicted of lewd act on a child under the age of 16, was charged with violating the terms of her probation. The Greenville County Circuit Court (South Carolina) revoked her probation for two years; ordered that her probation be terminated upon release; and ordered that she be subject to satellite monitoring for the rest of her life, pursuant to S.C. Code Ann. § 23-3-540(C). She appealed.


OVERVIEW: Although appellate presented expert testimony at the revocation hearing that she posed a low risk of reoffending, the trial court found that it was statutorily mandated to impose lifetime satellite monitoring without making any findings as to her likelihood of reoffending. Since she had been convicted of lewd act, there was no statutory mechanism for her to seek relief from this lifetime monitoring. Moreover, she could not leave the state without prior approval and could only be gone overnight in the case of an emergency. The high court held that requiring a convicted sex offender who was under no probationary or similar restrictions to submit to satellite monitoring for the rest of her life if she posed a low risk of reoffending violated her substantive due process rights under U.S. Const. amend. XIV. Further, § 23-540's travel policy infringed on the offender's fundamental right to travel without any consideration of whether such a restriction was warranted. If the trial court found that an offender had a low risk of re-offending but still imposed monitoring, the offender could petition for release from the monitoring after 10 years, consistent with § 23-3-540(H).


OUTCOME: The case was remanded so the trial court could make a determination of appellant's risk of reoffending.

 

48 of 275 DOCUMENTS


State v. Green


Opinion No. 27108


SUPREME COURT OF SOUTH CAROLINA


397 S.C. 268; 724 S.E.2d 664; 2012 S.C. LEXIS 69


February 23, 2012, Heard 
April 4, 2012, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Rehearing denied by State v. Green, 2012 S.C. LEXIS 141 (S.C., May 3, 2012)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeal From Aiken County. Doyet A. Early, III, Circuit Court Judge.



DISPOSITION: AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: After a jury trial in the Aiken County Circuit Court (South Carolina), defendant was convicted of criminal solicitation of a minor (S.C. Code Ann. § 16-15-342 (Supp. 2011)) and attempted criminal sexual conduct (CSC) with a minor in the second-degree (S.C. Code Ann. § 16-3-655(B)(1) (Supp. 2011)). He appealed.


OVERVIEW: Defendant chatted over the Internet with a officer posing as a 14-year-old girl. Defendant sent her pictures of his penis, stated he could show it to her in person, and arranged to meet her. He was arrested at the meeting site. He argued that § 16-15-342 was unconstitutionally overbroad. The high court disagreed. Speech used to further the sexual exploitation of children was not constitutionally protected due to the State's compelling interest in preventing such conduct, and § 16-15-342 was narrowly drafted to prohibit criminal conduct rather than protected speech. Nor was it unconstitutionally vague, as it was sufficiently precise to provide fair notice to those to whom it applied. That an actual minor was not the subject of defendant's intent did not preclude his conviction of either crime. Evidence of his specific intent to commit a sexual battery on a child and an overt act toward the commission of the offense was sufficient to convict him of attempted CSC. Photos of his penis, while offensive, were properly admitted to show his intent. He was not entitled to a charge of attempted assault and battery of an aggravated nature as a lesser included offense of attempted CSC.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

McLeod v. Starnes


Opinion No. 27100


SUPREME COURT OF SOUTH CAROLINA


396 S.C. 647; 723 S.E.2d 198; 2012 S.C. LEXIS 52


February 15, 2011, Heard 
March 7, 2012, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Starnes v. McLeod, 2012 U.S. LEXIS 6984 (U.S., Oct. 1, 2012)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeal from Lexington County. Richard W. Chewning, III, Family Court Judge.



DISPOSITION: REVERSED AND REMANDED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant mother sued respondent father in the Lexington County Family Court (South Carolina) for one child's college expenses, an increase in support for another child, and attorney's fees and costs. The trial court (1) dismissed the claim for college expenses, (2) reduced support for the second child, (3) reduced support payable from the father's bonus, (4) reduced his obligation, and (5) denied attorney's fees and costs. The mother appealed.


OVERVIEW: The mother said it was error to find making the father pay college expenses violated equal protection. The supreme court agreed because (1) its prior decision finding an equal protection violation wrongly applied strict scrutiny, (2) permitting such an order did not improperly treat divorced parents differently than non-divorced parents, and (3) requiring the payment under limited circumstances was rationally related to a legitimate state interest in ensuring its youth's education. Awarding the father a credit for alleged child support overpayment was error because it was based on an erroneous calculation of the parties' incomes. It was error to reduce the percentage of his bonus payable as child support because the reduction was not explained. It was error to deny the mother's request for attorney's fees and costs because (1) these expenses were at least half her income, while the father's were less than one-third his income, and (2) his conduct caused the litigation, as he did not pay the full amount of his bonus that was due, and his claim that a child did not need support after the age of majority prompted the mother's suit, from which she received significant benefit.


OUTCOME: The trial court's judgment was reversed, and the matter was remanded to determine whether and in what amount the father had to contribute to a child's college education.

Grimsley v. S.C. Law Enforcement Div.


Opinion No. 27085


SUPREME COURT OF SOUTH CAROLINA


396 S.C. 276; 721 S.E.2d 423; 2012 S.C. LEXIS 1


November 16, 2011, Heard 
January 3, 2012, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appeal after remand at, Remanded by Grimsley v. S.C. Law Enforcement Div., 2014 S.C. App. LEXIS 28 (S.C. Ct. App., Feb. 26, 2014)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeal from Richland County. G. Thomas Cooper, Jr., Circuit Court Judge.



DISPOSITION: REVERSED AND REMANDED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant employees sued respondent State and division, alleging that the division imposed a requirement in a statutorily authorized retirement program that was contrary to law. The Richland County Circuit Court (South Carolina) dismissed the complaint. The employees appealed.


OVERVIEW: As part of a rehire process, the division required, among other things, the employees to sign a form, which provided that they would have a reduction of 13.6 percent in their salary to cover the cost of the division's employer portion of retirement. The trial court dismissed the complaint for failure to exhaust administrative remedies under the South Carolina Retirement Contribution Procedures Act (Retirement Act). The trial court also found that the employees did not have a property interest rooted in state law on which their claim could have been based. The appellate court found that the trial court erred in finding that the employees were required to exhaust their administrative remedies under the Retirement Act. Under S.C. Code Ann. § 9-21-30, the Retirement Act applied to a dispute between a member and the retirement systems. The complaint here involved neither the retirement systems in general nor any administrative decision by the retirement systems. Because there was no controversy involving the retirement systems, the Retirement Act's administrative remedies were not implicated here. Further, S.C. Code Ann. § 9-11-90 provided a basis to assert a property interest.


OUTCOME: The judgment was reversed and the case was remanded.

 

51 of 275 DOCUMENTS


State v. Inman


Opinion No. 27081


SUPREME COURT OF SOUTH CAROLINA


395 S.C. 539; 720 S.E.2d 31; 2011 S.C. LEXIS 416


September 21, 2011, Heard 
December 28, 2011, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Rehearing denied by State v. Inman, 2012 S.C. LEXIS 58 (S.C., Jan. 25, 2012)
US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Inman v. S.C., 2012 U.S. LEXIS 6667 (U.S., Oct. 1, 2012)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeal From Pickens County. Edward W. Miller, Circuit Court Judge.



DISPOSITION: AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed a judgment of the Pickens County Court (South Carolina) that convicted him of murder, burglary, criminal sexual conduct, and kidnapping and sentenced him to death, arguing that his guilty plea to the murder charge should be vacated because it was an invalid conditional guilty plea in that defendant was insisting upon being sentenced by a jury and that the trial court erred in denying defendant's motion for a mistrial.


OVERVIEW: Defendant raped and murdered a college student in her apartment. He pled guilty to the charges against him, and the trial judge imposed a capital sentence. On review, the court held that defendant did not enter an invalid conditional plea. A review of the plea colloquy revealed that defendant entered his plea knowingly and voluntarily as he repeatedly acknowledged that he understood the charge against him, understood the consequences of his plea, and understood the rights he was waiving by pleading guilty, including the right to have a jury determine his guilt and sentence. Therefore, defendant's plea was valid. The court agreed with defendant that prosecutorial misconduct occurred in that the prosecutors used intimidation on a defense expert, who indicated that she felt threatened by the prosecutors. However, the trial court did not err in denying defendant's motion for a mistrial because defendant failed to show prejudice resulting from the prosecutorial misconduct and defendant's sentence of death was not imposed in violation of his due process rights. Lastly, the death sentence was neither excessive nor disproportionate to the crime.


OUTCOME: The judgment of conviction and sentence was affirmed.
