McMaster v. Columbia Bd. of Zoning Appeals


Opinion No. 27075


SUPREME COURT OF SOUTH CAROLINA


395 S.C. 499; 719 S.E.2d 660; 2011 S.C. LEXIS 394


October 19, 2011, Heard 
December 12, 2011, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeal from Richland County. J. Michelle Childs, Circuit Court Judge.



DISPOSITION: AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, landlord and tenant, sought review of a decision of the Richland County Circuit Court (South Carolina), upholding appellee city's finding that the landlord and tenant were in violation of Columbia, S.C. City Code § 17-55, which restricted residency in a certain area to no more than three unrelated individuals living together, and that the ordinance was constitutional under the Due Process Clause of the South Carolina Constitution.


OVERVIEW: The landlord's property was occupied by four unrelated young women, all of whom were undergraduate students at the University of South Carolina. The occupants were friends, shared meals and expenses, and operated as a single household. The ordinance defined "family" in part as three unrelated persons residing together as a single housekeeping unit. Appellants argued that this definition arbitrarily and capriciously deprived them of a cognizable property interest in violation of the Due Process Clause, S.C. Const. art. I, § 3. The court found that there was a rational relationship between the ordinance's definition of "family" and the legitimate governmental interests the ordinance sought to further, which was controlling the undesirable qualities associated with mass student congestion. Groups of unrelated persons typically had different living styles in comparison to groups of related persons; limiting the number of unrelated persons who may live together in single family homes furthered the goal of creating family-oriented neighborhoods that were safe and quiet for young children.


OUTCOME: The court held that the ordinance was constitutional.

Emerson Elec. Co. v. S.C. Dep't of Revenue


Opinion No. 27073


SUPREME COURT OF SOUTH CAROLINA


395 S.C. 481; 719 S.E.2d 650; 2011 S.C. LEXIS 390


September 21, 2011, Heard 
December 12, 2011, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeal from Richland County. Deborah B. Durden, Administrative Law Judge.



DISPOSITION: AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant taxpayer sought review of a decision of the Richland County Administrative Law Court (South Carolina), upholding appellee Department of Revenue's disallowance of certain expense deductions claimed by the taxpayer.


OVERVIEW: The taxpayer's principal place of business was in Missouri; it did business in multiple states. The taxpayer had excluded from its taxable income dividends received from its wholly-owned subsidiaries because, for both federal and South Carolina income tax purposes, such dividends were not subject to income tax under I.R.C. § 243 (2006) or S.C. Code Ann. § 12-6-1110. The court held that under the matching principle, and given the plain meaning of S.C. Code Ann. § 12-6-2220(2), the taxpayer could not claim deductions for interest expenses related to the dividends that were not related to South Carolina income. The interest expenses were properly allocated to the taxpayer's principal place of business, Missouri. The court held that § 12-6-2220 did not violate the Commerce Clause because it treated all nonresident taxpayers the same; South Carolina could not control the fact that some taxpayers were impacted differently due to the law of their principal places of business.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the ALC.

 

54 of 275 DOCUMENTS


Sandlands C&D, LLC v. County of Horry


Opinion No. 27042


SUPREME COURT OF SOUTH CAROLINA


394 S.C. 451; 716 S.E.2d 280; 2011 S.C. LEXIS 294; 41 ELR 20298


May 24, 2011, Heard 
September 19, 2011, Filed


DISPOSITION:  [***1] CERTIFIED QUESTION ANSWERED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff landfill operator brought an action in federal court challenging the constitutionality of an ordinance enacted by defendant county that regulated the disposal of solid wastes in the county. The United States District Court for the District of South Carolina certified a question of state law to the court.


OVERVIEW: The operator had a permit to accept construction and demolition (C & D) waste in a neighboring county. The county, through a non-profit corporation, operated a municipal solid waste landfill and a C & D landfill. The court held that the ordinance represented a valid exercise of the county's police powers, as articulated in S.C. Code Ann. § 4-9-25. The South Carolina Solid Waste Policy and Management Act, S.C. Code Ann. § 44-96-10 et seq. (2002) (SWPMA), did not expressly or impliedly preempt the county's authority to regulate solid waste. Rather, the SWPMA was laden with references to the counties' involvement in the management and regulation of solid waste, such as S.C. Code Ann. § 44-96-80(A), (J), (K). The ordinance did not conflict with the legislature's intent to require regional planning as demonstrated in S.C. Code Ann. Regs. § 61-107.17.C.3. The ordinance regulating the flow of waste neither frustrated the purpose of the SWPMA, nor interfered with need determination for landfill permitting pursuant to the DON Regulation.


OUTCOME: The court held that the state act did not preempt the county ordinance.

 

55 of 275 DOCUMENTS


Bd. of Trs. of the Sch. Dist. v. State


Opinion No. 27035


SUPREME COURT OF SOUTH CAROLINA


395 S.C. 276; 718 S.E.2d 210; 2011 S.C. LEXIS 283


November 30, 2010, Heard 
August 29, 2011, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Rehearing denied by Bd. of Trs. v. State, 2011 S.C. LEXIS 423 (S.C., Oct. 20, 2011)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
ORIGINAL JURISDICTION.



DISPOSITION: JUDGMENT FOR PLAINTIFF.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff, a school district board of trustees, and defendants, the State, a county legislative delegation, and the General Assembly, jointly petitioned the court to determine the constitutionality of Act 308 of the South Carolina Acts of 2010 (Act 308).


OVERVIEW: The General Assembly passed Act 308, which transferred the oversight of financial operations of the school district from its board of trustees to a finance committee to be appointed by the legislative delegation. The Governor vetoed Act 308. The House of Representatives voted to override the Governor's veto, 33 to 10, as did the Senate, by a vote of 1 to 0. The court rejected the General Assembly's recently-adopted practice of allowing only one member of a local delegation to carry a bill affecting only that locality, which circumvented and violated the mandated two-thirds requirement for overriding a veto in S.C. Const. art. IV, § 21. Because a quorum was present in each house, the veto override votes of 33 to 10 in the House of Representatives and 1 to 0 in the Senate fell short of the constitutionally mandated two-thirds requirement, which required two-thirds of a quorum. Therefore, the Governor's veto was sustained.


OUTCOME: The court held that Act 308 had been vetoed by the Governor.

 

56 of 275 DOCUMENTS


Charleston County Sch. Dist. v. Harrell


Opinion No. 27011


SUPREME COURT OF SOUTH CAROLINA


393 S.C. 552; 713 S.E.2d 604; 2011 S.C. LEXIS 245


January 18, 2011, Heard 
July 25, 2011, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeal From Charleston County. Roger M. Young, Circuit Court Judge.



DISPOSITION: AFFIRMED IN PART, REVERSED IN PART, AND REMANDED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant school district filed suit against respondents, the Speaker of the House, President of the Senate, Governor, and the State, seeking a declaratory judgment that 2005 S.C. Acts 189 was special legislation in violation of S.C. Const. art. III, § 34 and S.C. Const. art. XIII, § 7. It challenged an order of the Circuit Court, Charleston County (South Carolina), which granted respondents' motion to dismiss under S.C. R. Civ. P. 12(b)(6).


OVERVIEW: The supreme court held that the circuit court erred when it included matters outside the complaint in deciding respondents' motion to dismiss. In support of its decision to grant the motion to dismiss, the circuit court cited to a South Carolina federal district court case that found the county had a unique geography with nearly one hundred miles of coastline that was divided by rivers and linked by bridges. In using the federal district court case to supply facts from outside the complaint, the circuit court impermissibly went beyond the proper parameters of a motion to dismiss. The school district stated a sufficient cause of action challenging the constitutionality of Act 189 to withstand a Rule 12(b)(6) motion to dismiss. It alleged that the South Carolina Charter Schools Act was a general law that applied uniformly to every county in South Carolina. Furthermore, it alleged that Act 189 improperly singled out only its county as the recipient of special rules for areas covered by the Charter Schools Act without a rational basis for doing so. The circuit court properly dismissed the Governor as a party. The complaint did not demonstrate a nexus between him and Act 189.


OUTCOME: The supreme court affirmed the dismissal of the governor as a party. It reversed the order granting respondents' motion to dismiss and remanded the case to the circuit court for further proceedings.

 

57 of 275 DOCUMENTS


Cabiness v. Town of James Island


Opinion No. 26989


SUPREME COURT OF SOUTH CAROLINA


393 S.C. 176; 712 S.E.2d 416; 2011 S.C. LEXIS 202


February 2, 2011, Heard 
June 20, 2011, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: As Corrected July 28, 2011.
Rehearing denied by Cabiness v. Town of James Island, 2011 S.C. LEXIS 318 (S.C., July 21, 2011)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeal From Charleston County J. Cordell Maddox, Jr., Circuit Court Judge.



DISPOSITION: AFFIRMED IN PART, REVERSED IN PART, AND REMANDED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, a City and four individuals (collectively, the City), challenged an order of the Charleston County Circuit Court (South Carolina) granting a petition for incorporation filed by respondents, a Town, its mayor and an alliance for self-government (collectively, the Town), pursuant to S.C. Code Ann. § 5-1-24 (Supp. 2010).


OVERVIEW: The Town's third incorporation petition, expressly excluding all properties that were not contiguous under S.C. Code Ann. § 5-1-30(A)(4), was insufficient pursuant to S.C. Code Ann. § 5-1-24 because the inclusion of a significant number of properties was contingent upon a judicial determination, which left the voters unaware of whether large portions of the Town would ever be incorporated. Although the conclusion that the petition was insufficient was dispositive of the case, in the interest of judicial economy, the appellate court reached the remaining issues to provide guidance for future incorporation petitions. It first held that the 2005 amendments to S.C. Code Ann. § 5-1-30(A)(4) were not unconstitutional special legislation because those incorporating and those annexing were not part of the same class and, therefore, the amendments did not result in disparate treatment among class members. Also, courts must adhere to the plain language of the definition of contiguity and require incorporating areas with issues pertaining to contiguity to conceptually eliminate publicly-owned property from the map of their proposed boundaries and determine if the properties would then touch.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the order upholding the Town's incorporation. It affirmed the circuit court's conclusion that the incorporation scheme at issue was not unconstitutional special legislation, and its holding that the term "publicly-owned property" did not require fee simple ownership by the federal, state, or county governments. However, it reversed the circuit court's analysis of contiguity, and remanded the matter to the circuit court.

Boiter v. S.C. DOT


Opinion No. 26981


SUPREME COURT OF SOUTH CAROLINA


393 S.C. 123; 712 S.E.2d 401; 2011 S.C. LEXIS 183


January 6, 2011, Heard 
June 6, 2011, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Rehearing denied by Boiter v. S.C. DOT, 2011 S.C. LEXIS 322 (S.C., July 21, 2011)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeal From Spartanburg County. J. Mark Hayes, II, Circuit Court Judge.



DISPOSITION: AFFIRMED IN PART AND REVERSED IN PART.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs, two motorcycle accident victims, filed four suits against defendants, the South Carolina Department of Transportation (SCDOT) and the South Carolina Department of Public Safety (SCDPS) in the Spartanburg County Circuit Court (South Carolina). A jury awarded them $ 1.875 million. The circuit court granted defendants' motion to reduce the verdict to $ 300,000 each, for a total of $ 600,000. The victims appealed.


OVERVIEW: The victims argued that the two-tier cap on damages created by S.C. Code Ann. § 15-78-120 (2005), creating a higher limitation of liability for claims against state physicians and a lower limit for all other state entities, violated Equal Protection. The court held that two hypotheses supported the different classifications: (1) relieving the government from the hardships of unlimited liability; and (2) furthering accountable and competent healthcare while promoting affordable medical liability insurance, pursuant to S.C. Code Ann. § 15-78-20(a), (g) (2005). The circuit court erred, however, in tying the number of occurrences to the number of injuries sustained by the victims. Two independent and separate acts of negligence occurred -- one by SCDOT and one by SCDPS. SCDOT was negligent in not having a re-lamping policy in place, and SCDPS was negligent in not following its own policy to notify a SCDOT technician when a light had burned out. Because there were two separate entities which committed two separate acts of negligence, each agency's act was a separate occurrence, entitling the victims to a combined verdict of $ 1.2 million.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the circuit court's finding that the two-tier statutory cap on damages was constitutional but reversed the holding that only one occurrence had taken place and ordered that each plaintiff was entitled to $ 600,000, or a combined verdict of $ 1.2 million.

Lewis v. Lewis


Opinion No. 26973


SUPREME COURT OF SOUTH CAROLINA


392 S.C. 381; 709 S.E.2d 650; 2011 S.C. LEXIS 169


April 20, 2010, Heard 
May 9, 2011, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeal from Williamsburg County. R. Wright Turbeville, Family Court Judge. George M. McFaddin, Jr., Family Court Judge.
Lewis v. Lewis, 2008 S.C. App. Unpub. LEXIS 165 (S.C. Ct. App., Nov. 21, 2008)



DISPOSITION: REVERSED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In a divorce action, the Court of Appeals (South Carolina) reversed a family court's determination of the value of a former marital home, and reversed and modified the family court's award of expert witness fees to petitioner wife. The wife's application for a writ of certiorari review was granted.


OVERVIEW: The wife retained an expert real estate appraiser to render an opinion on the value of the former marital residence. The family court acted within its discretion when it accepted the appraiser's valuation because respondent husband failed to demonstrate error in the valuation. Furthermore, the family court acted within its discretion in ordering the husband to pay the wife for expert witness fees. The family court found the various experts credible and accepted their valuations. Moreover, the experts' valuations were material to the relief the wife sought and obtained. Further, there was a large disparity in the parties' incomes, with the husband making almost $20,000 more per month than the wife. Accordingly, the appellate court concurred in the family court's allocation of expert witness fees to the husband.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the court of appeals' decision and reinstated the family court's order.

 

61 of 275 DOCUMENTS


Ahrens v. State


Opinion No. 26966


SUPREME COURT OF SOUTH CAROLINA


392 S.C. 340; 709 S.E.2d 54; 2011 S.C. LEXIS 159


November 3, 2010, Heard 
May 2, 2011, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: As Corrected May 26, 2011.
Rehearing denied by Ahrens v. State, 2011 S.C. LEXIS 194 (S.C., May 26, 2011)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeal from Richland County. Appeal from Georgetown County. James C. Williams, Jr., Circuit Court Judge.
Layman v. State, 368 S.C. 631, 630 S.E.2d 265, 2006 S.C. LEXIS 154 (S.C., 2006)



DISPOSITION: AFFIRMED IN PART; REVERSED IN PART.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: On remand in an action involving a contract dispute between certain retirees and the State and its retirement systems (collectively, the State), both parties appealed from orders of the Richland County Circuit Court (South Carolina), holding that no contract prevented the State from changing the terms of S.C. Code Ann. §§ 9-1-1790(A) and 9-11-90(4)(a) (the Working Retirees statutes) and that the State could not require certain contributions.


OVERVIEW: No contract existed between the State and the retirees, either by way of forms signed or by representations made. The Working Retiree statutes did not create a binding contractual right. Accordingly, summary judgment for the State was proper as to the constitutional issues raised by retirees because those issues were premised on the existence of a contract. However, the circuit court erred in holding that the State was estopped from applying the State Retirement System Preservation and Investment Reform Act, 2005 S.C. Acts 153, which required that retired members pay the employee contribution as if they were active members, but without accruing additional service credit. Under the circumstances, estoppel could not lie against the State, and the retirees failed to establish the elements of equitable estoppel. Any reliance created by the forms, brochures, or statements of the State and its agents did not cause a prejudicial change in the retirees' position. Because the court concluded that the named retirees had neither a contractual nor an equitable remedy, it declined to address the issue of class certification.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed in part and reversed in part the circuit court's judgment.

In re S.C. Bar


Opinion No. 26964


SUPREME COURT OF SOUTH CAROLINA


392 S.C. 328; 709 S.E.2d 633; 2011 S.C. LEXIS 150


September 22, 2010, Heard 
April 25, 2011, Filed


DISPOSITION:  [***1] LETTER OF CAUTION.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The South Carolina Office of Disciplinary Counsel Hearing Panel determined that respondent attorney was subject to discipline for violating S.C. App. Ct. R. 413:7(a)(5) and 407:8.4(e), both of which provided that a lawyer could be disciplined for engaging in conduct tending to pollute the administration of justice, and Rule S.C. App. Ct. R. 413:7(a)(6), regarding the attorney's oath of office. A majority of the Panel recommended an admonition.


OVERVIEW: The attorney sent an e-mail message to opposing counsel in a domestic matter in which the attorney commented on opposing counsel's daughter's drug use and opposing counsel's parenting skills. The court determined that such an e-mail could only inflame the passions of everyone involved, make litigation more intense, and undermine a lawyer's ability to objectively represent his or her client. That kind of personal attack against a family member of opposing counsel with no connection to the litigation brought into question the integrity of the judicial system and prejudiced the administration of justice. Thus, the attorney did violated Rules 8.4(e) and 7(a)(5). In addition, he violated his attorney's oath, which was ground for discipline under Rule 7(a)(6). The civility clause contained within the lawyer's oath was not unconstitutionally vague and overbroad. A person of common intelligence does not have to guess at the meaning of the civility oath, and there was no substantial amount of protected free speech penalized by the civility oath. Finally, the court considered the attorney's acknowledgement of misconduct and his sincere remorse in reaching its conclusion.


OUTCOME: The court issued a private letter of caution with a finding of minor misconduct to the attorney.

 

63 of 275 DOCUMENTS


Berberich v. Jack


Opinion No. 26955


SUPREME COURT OF SOUTH CAROLINA


392 S.C. 278; 709 S.E.2d 607; 2011 S.C. LEXIS 106


December 2, 2010, Heard 
April 4, 2011, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeal From Florence County. Thomas A. Russo, Circuit Court Judge.



DISPOSITION: REVERSED AND REMANDED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant worker sought review of the decision of the trial court (South Carolina), which found in favor of respondent homeowner in the workers' negligence action after he slipped and fell from a wet ladder while working at the homeowner's home.


OVERVIEW: The worker brought a negligence action after he slipped and fell from a wet ladder while working at the homeowner's home. The trial court denied his request to charge the jury on the definitions of recklessness, willfulness, and wantonness and refused to instruct the jury that heightened forms of wrongdoing could not be compared to ordinary negligence under comparative negligence. The supreme court reversed and remanded, stating that under South Carolina's comparative negligence system, all forms of conduct amounting to negligence, including ordinary negligence, gross negligence, and reckless, willful, or wanton conduct, might be compared to and offset by any conduct that fell short of conduct intended to cause injury or damage. A trial court should therefore instruct the jury on the definitions of those various forms of negligence whenever requested by a party. The jury instructions in the case had the potential to confuse the jury.


OUTCOME: The judgment was reversed and remanded for a new trial.

 

64 of 275 DOCUMENTS


State v. Sheppard


Opinion No. 26927


SUPREME COURT OF SOUTH CAROLINA


391 S.C. 415; 706 S.E.2d 16; 2011 S.C. LEXIS 28


November 16, 2010, Heard 
February 7, 2011, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeal from Lexington County, James W. Johnson, Jr., Circuit Court Judge.



DISPOSITION: AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was convicted by a jury in the Lexington County Circuit Court (South Carolina) of securities fraud, obtaining property under false pretenses, and conspiracy. The trial court imposed a sentence of imprisonment. Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: Defendant contended that (1) he was denied a fair trial because of a third party's contact with a juror; (2) the state grand jury lacked subject matter jurisdiction over the counts of obtaining property under false pretenses and conspiracy; (3) S.C. Code Ann. § 14-7-1820 (Supp. 2009) violated the constitutional prohibitions against ex post facto laws; and (4) the circuit court abused its discretion by imposing a sentence disproportionate to those of his coconspirators. On appeal, the court found that defendant's first argument failed because defendant did not preserve that issue for review and the court would not apply the plain error rule. In addition, defendant was actually challenging the sufficiency of the indictment when he argued that the grand jury did not have jurisdiction over the counts of obtaining property under false pretenses and conspiracy. Further, because defendant failed to timely challenge these counts in the indictment and instead went to trial on the charges, he could not now, having lost at trial, come back to challenge the sufficiency of the indictment. Finally, defendant did not preserve his ex post facto law and disproportionate sentence arguments.


OUTCOME: The circuit court's conviction and sentence were affirmed.

City of Rock Hill v. Harris


Opinion No. 26917


SUPREME COURT OF SOUTH CAROLINA


391 S.C. 149; 705 S.E.2d 53; 2011 S.C. LEXIS 19


September 21, 2010, Heard 
January 24, 2011, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeal from York County. Lee S. Alford, Circuit Court Judge.



DISPOSITION: AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of a decision of the York County Circuit Court (South Carolina), which convicted him of possession of beer by a person under the age of 21 in violation of S.C. Code Ann. § 63-19-2440.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was a rear passenger in a vehicle stopped for a traffic violation. After admitting to the officer that he had consumed two beers, defendant was cited for possession of beer under 21 and for public disorderly conduct. A municipal court convicted defendant of the possession charge. He thereafter appealed his conviction to the circuit court, which upheld the conviction. S.C. Const. art. XVII, §14, prohibited the sale of alcoholic beverages to persons under the age of 21 years. Defendant argued that § 63-19-2440 conflicted with S.C. Const. art. XVII, §14, because the statute restricted more than the sale of alcoholic beverages to 18 to 20 year olds by also restricting their possession and consumption. The court disagreed and upheld the conviction. With respect for the principle that every presumption would be made in favor of the constitutionality of a statute, the court construed the term sale broadly. Thus, when the legislature reserved its power to restrict the sale of alcoholic beverages, the court determined that it meant to also reserve its power to restrict the purchase and possession of alcoholic beverages.


OUTCOME: The decision was affirmed.

 

66 of 275 DOCUMENTS


Jackson v. Sanford


Opinion No. 26918


SUPREME COURT OF SOUTH CAROLINA


398 S.C. 580; 731 S.E.2d 722; 2011 S.C. LEXIS 17


December 2, 2010, Heard 
January 24, 2011, Filed


DISPOSITION:  [***1] JUDGMENT FOR PETITIONER.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner mayor brought an action seeking a declaratory judgment in the court's original jurisdiction that respondent governor's veto of certain appropriations to the State Budget and Control Board was unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: The mayor argued that the governor's veto of one source of appropriated funds while leaving the remainder of the total appropriation, and the specified "objects and purposes" of the appropriation, intact, was unconstitutional under S.C. Const. art. IV, § 21. The court agreed. The result of the governor's veto was that the total appropriation for each of the programs, positions, and expenses was reduced by the amount the general assembly had designated to be drawn from the general fund, but the programs, positions, and expenses themselves were not eliminated. This was an improper modification of legislation. The governor attempted to veto funds arising from a particular source, but he did not veto the purpose to which those funds were allocated. The net effect of the veto was a veto of part of an item, resulting in modification of legislation, which was an unconstitutional exercise of the veto power. The governor was constitutionally permitted to veto an item in its entirety, but not partially.


OUTCOME: Judgment was entered for the mayor.

Travelscape, LLC v. S.C. Dep't of Revenue


Opinion No. 26913


SUPREME COURT OF SOUTH CAROLINA


391 S.C. 89; 705 S.E.2d 28; 2011 S.C. LEXIS 12


May 13, 2010, Heard 
January 18, 2011, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeal From Administrative Law Court. Ralph K. Anderson, III, Administrative Law Court Judge.



DISPOSITION: AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The South Carolina Department of Revenue assessed sales taxes and penalties against appellant, an online travel company; it appealed pursuant to S.C. Code Ann. § 12-60-460 (Supp. 2009). An administrative law court (ALC) affirmed the assessment of the tax but not the penalties. Appellant sought judicial review.


OVERVIEW: Appellant contracted with hotels to discount their rates for reservations made on its website. Appellant added a facilitation fee, service fee, and tax recovery charge to the room rate, retaining the facilitation and service fees as its compensation; it did not pay sales tax on those fees. The ALC ruled that under S.C. Code Ann. § 12-36-920, appellant owed a seven percent sales tax on the gross proceeds received from furnishing hotel accommodations in South Carolina. The high court held that 1) the fees appellant charged were taxable under § 12-36-920(A) as "gross proceeds"; 2) while it did not physically "furnish" accommodations, it was in the business of doing so; and 3) it conducted the business of furnishing accommodations in South Carolina, despite a lack of physical presence there, as it entered into contracts with South Carolina hotels and sent its employees to South Carolina to negotiate such contracts. The tax did not violate the Dormant Commerce Clause ( U.S. Const. art. I, § 8, cl. 3) as applied to appellant because appellant had a sufficient physical presence in the state, and the tax was fairly apportioned; i.e., it was internally and externally consistent.


OUTCOME: The ALC's decision was affirmed.

 

68 of 275 DOCUMENTS


City of Greenville v. Bane


Opinion No. 26888


SUPREME COURT OF SOUTH CAROLINA


390 S.C. 303; 702 S.E.2d 112; 2010 S.C. LEXIS 349


September 23, 2010, Heard 
November 8, 2010, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeal from Greenville County. Edward W. Miller, Circuit Court Judge.



DISPOSITION: REVERSED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was convicted in municipal court of violating Greenville, S.C., Code § 24-32 (1985), a city ordinance prohibiting molesting or disturbing others. On appeal, the Greenville County Circuit Court (South Carolina) affirmed the conviction, finding the ordinance was constitutional and that the trial court properly denied defendant's motions for a directed verdict and a new trial. Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was loudly preaching against homosexuality on a public sidewalk. An officer cited defendant for violating Greenville, S.C., Code § 24-32 (1985) after he directed a comment to three young women as they passed by. The appellate court found that the lower court erred in affirming the trial court's denial of defendant's directed verdict motion as to § 24-32(1), (2), (4), (5). The City presented no evidence that, other than through his speech, defendant engaged in any conduct that would disturb or molest others. Further, Greenville, S.C., Code § 24-32(3) was unconstitutionally vague because the words "humiliate," "insult," and "scare" were not sufficiently definite to give reasonable notice of the prohibited conduct. This provision was subjective because the words that humiliated, insulted, or scared one person may not have the same effect on another person. Thus, people of common intelligence may have been forced to guess at the provision's meaning and may have differed as to its applicability. Greenville, S.C., Code § 24-32(3) was not precisely written in a way that would notify a person of the certain specific conduct that is prohibited.


OUTCOME: The conviction was reversed.

 

69 of 275 DOCUMENTS


Branham v. Ford Motor Co.


Opinion No. 26860


SUPREME COURT OF SOUTH CAROLINA


390 S.C. 203; 701 S.E.2d 5; 2010 S.C. LEXIS 291; CCH Prod. Liab. Rep. P18,474


April 9, 2009, Heard 
August 16, 2010, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Rehearing denied by Branham v. Ford Motor Co., 2010 S.C. LEXIS 390 (S.C., Nov. 17, 2010)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeal From Hampton County. R. Markley Dennis, Jr., Circuit Court Judge.



DISPOSITION: AFFIRMED IN PART; REVERSED IN PART; AND REMANDED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant motor vehicle manufacturer appealed a judgment of the Hampton County Circuit Court (South Carolina) in a product liability action upon a jury verdict for plaintiff passenger of $ 16 million in actual damages and $15 million in punitive damages against the manufacturer and the driver of a vehicle in a rollover accident while the passenger was in the back seat of the vehicle.


OVERVIEW: The passenger alleged that a design defect added to the rollover propensity of the vehicle. The court held that the risk-utility test with its requirement of showing a feasible alternative design based on evidence in existence at or before the time of manufacture was the exclusive test to use in a products liability design case. The passenger had to point to a design flaw in the vehicle and show how an alternative design would have prevented the product from being unreasonably dangerous. This alternative design had to account for its associated costs, safety and functionality. However, while the passenger produced evidence of a feasible alternative design, retrial was necessary. Whether a product was defective had to be measured against information known at the time the product was placed into the stream of commerce. Introduction of post-manufacture evidence and evidence of other rollover accidents with insufficient similarity prejudiced the manufacturer. Also, counsel for the passenger in his closing argument on punitive damages impermissibly asked the jury to punish the manufacturer not only for harm to the passenger but also for harm to others involved in rollover accidents.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment of the trial court in part and remanded for a new trial on liability using the risk-utility test and on damages.

State v. Bixby


Opinion No. 26871


SUPREME COURT OF SOUTH CAROLINA


388 S.C. 528; 698 S.E.2d 572; 2010 S.C. LEXIS 294


November 18, 2009, Heard 
August 16, 2010, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Rehearing denied by State v. Bixby, 2010 S.C. LEXIS 379 (S.C., Sept. 23, 2010)
US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Bixby v. South Carolina, 563 U.S. 963, 131 S. Ct. 2154, 179 L. Ed. 2d 940, 2011 U.S. LEXIS 3313 (2011)
Magistrate's recommendation at, Habeas corpus proceeding at Bixby v. McFadden, 2014 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 170186 (D.S.C., July 31, 2014)
Stay granted by, Remanded by Bixby v. Stirling, 2017 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 55798 (D.S.C., Apr. 12, 2017)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeal from Abbeville County. Alexander S. Macaulay, Circuit Court Judge.
State v. Bixby, 373 S.C. 74, 644 S.E.2d 54, 2007 S.C. LEXIS 153 (S.C., 2007)



DISPOSITION: AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed following his convictions on two counts of murder and on related charges and his sentence by the Abbeville County Circuit Court (South Carolina) to death. Two officers died at defendant's home where they were attempting to deal with a dispute concerning a right of way across the property that officials sought to use in order to expand a state road.


OVERVIEW: On appeal, the court declined to consider juror qualification issues because defense counsel used only seven of ten available peremptory strikes. A wiretap placed on the telephone at defendant's home pursuant to S.C. Code Ann. § 17-30-95 (2002) once the officers' confrontation with defendant began was constitutional. According to the court, the wiretap was akin to a warrantless search conducted when exigent circumstances demand immediate action. The court also held that admitting a video of funeral rites for one of the murdered officers during sentencing was not error because it was victim impact evidence that showed footage of actual mourners; in addition, its traditional trappings of a law enforcement officer's funeral demonstrated the general loss suffered by society. Ordering defendant to submit to a mental evaluation did not violate his right against self-incrimination under the Fifth Amendment or S.C. Const. art. I, § 12 because defendant had announced his intent to introduce evidence concerning his mental health during the sentencing phase and the trial court conditioned the examination on protecting defendant from self-incrimination.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's death sentence.

Vasquez v. State


Opinion No. 26852


SUPREME COURT OF SOUTH CAROLINA


388 S.C. 447; 698 S.E.2d 561; 2010 S.C. LEXIS 286


March 17, 2010, Heard 
August 9, 2010, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Rehearing denied by Vasquez v. State, 2010 S.C. LEXIS 370 (S.C., Sept. 22, 2010)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeal From Horry County. John M. Milling, Circuit Court Judge.
State v. Vazquez, 364 S.C. 293, 613 S.E.2d 359, 2005 S.C. LEXIS 128 (S.C., 2005)



DISPOSITION: REVERSED AND REMANDED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner inmate was convicted of murder, kidnapping, armed robbery, and criminal conspiracy. The inmate was sentenced to death for the murders. The state's highest court vacated the life sentences for kidnapping with regard to the murder victims, but affirmed the convictions and remaining sentences. The inmate's petition for post-conviction relief (PCR) was denied by the Horry County Circuit Court (South Carolina).


OVERVIEW: The inmate's petition for a writ of certiorari was granted. The state's highest court held that trial counsel's failure to object to the solicitor's opening statement during the guilt phase, in which he described the inmate, a Muslim, as a domestic terrorist, and his closing argument at sentencing, in which he drew a correlation between September 11, 2001, and the acts for which the inmate was convicted constituted deficient performance. The comments so infected the trial with unfairness as to make the death sentence a denial of due process. The inmate's actions did not constitute terrorism under S.C. Code Ann. § 16-23-710(18) (Supp. 2009). The statements improperly evoked religious prejudice, and served only to inflame the passions and prejudice of the jury. There was a reasonable probability that counsel's failure to object to the comments affected the death sentence. The inmate received ineffective assistance of counsel under the Sixth Amendment, U.S. Const. amend. VI. Because the solicitor's comments were primarily confined to the penalty phase, they did not warrant a reversal of his convictions; they only entitled the inmate to a new sentencing hearing.


OUTCOME: The judgment was reversed. The matter was remanded to the trial court for a new sentencing hearing.

 

72 of 275 DOCUMENTS


Webb v. Sowell


Opinion No. 26807


SUPREME COURT OF SOUTH CAROLINA


387 S.C. 328; 692 S.E.2d 543; 2010 S.C. LEXIS 121


January 7, 2009, Heard 
April 19, 2010, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeal from Kershaw County. Robert S. Armstrong, Family Court Judge.



DISPOSITION: REVERSED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff father brought an action against defendant mother and third-party defendant son in the Kershaw County Family Court (South Carolina), seeking to reduce child support. The mother counterclaimed for the son's college expenses. The trial court denied the father's motion to dismiss based on the Equal Protection clause. The parents were ordered to divide college expenses, and the father appealed.


OVERVIEW: S.C. Code Ann. § 63-3-530(A)(17) (2007) provided that child support orders terminate when the child reaches age 18, marries, or becomes self-supporting. However, support could be continued beyond age 18 for certain "exceptional circumstances." In Risinger v. Risinger, 273 S.C. 36, 253 S.E.2d 652 (1979), it was held that secondary education could be considered such an exceptional circumstance. The court held that the statute, as interpreted by Risinger, violated the Equal Protection Clauses of the United States and South Carolina constitutions, U.S. Const. amend. XIV, § 1; S.C. Const. art. I, § 3. Because the statute only allows for the continuation of support beyond the age of 18, a court could order a parent subject to a support order at the time his or her child reached age 18 to pay college expenses, but could not order a parent not subject to such an order to pay college expenses. There was no rational basis for the varied treatment of the class of parents subject to a support order as compared to those parents not subject to such an order. The statute, as interpreted by Risinger, failed the rational basis test.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the family court's denial of the father's motion to dismiss.

 

73 of 275 DOCUMENTS


Segars-Andrews v. Judicial Merit Selection Comm'n


Opinion No. 26791


SUPREME COURT OF SOUTH CAROLINA


387 S.C. 109; 691 S.E.2d 453; 2010 S.C. LEXIS 81


March 2, 2010, Heard 
March 23, 2010, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
ORIGINAL JURISDICTION.



DISPOSITION: COMPLAINT DISMISSED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner judge filed an action challenging a decision of respondent, the Judicial Merit Selection Commission (JMSC), finding her unqualified for office. The judge raised several constitutional challenges, including separation of powers and a claim that legislative membership on the JMSC violated the constitutional prohibition against dual office holding.


OVERVIEW: The judge was elected to the family court in 1993. Her term was set to expire and she applied for re-election with the JMSC; no other individual filed for the judgeship. A disgruntled litigant opposed her re-election. A majority of the JMSC found the judge unqualified, thereby foreclosing the legislature from reelecting her. The judge argued that the composition of the JMSC, established in S.C. Code Ann. § 2-19-10(B), should not include legislators. She also argued that because the disgruntled litigant had filed a grievance that had been dismissed and because the Court of Appeals had affirmed her decision not to recuse herself, which the litigant had challenged, the JMSC, by revisiting the issue, had usurped the power of the judicial branch. The court held that because the JMSC acted in its political capacity, there was no showing of a S.C. Const. art. I, § 8 separation of powers violation. The judge's challenge presented a nonjusticiable political question. Because the JMSC was a constitutional office which exercised part of the sovereign, legislators could properly serve on the JMSC without violating the dual office rule, S.C. Const. art. III, § 24 and S.C. Const. art. VI, § 3.


OUTCOME: The court dismissed the judge's complaint.

 

74 of 275 DOCUMENTS


Sea Cove Dev., LLC v. Harbourside Cmty. Bank


Opinion No. 26789


SUPREME COURT OF SOUTH CAROLINA


387 S.C. 95; 691 S.E.2d 158; 2010 S.C. LEXIS 54


February 4, 2010, Heard 
March 22, 2010, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeal From Charleston County. J. Mark Hayes, II, Circuit Court Judge.



DISPOSITION: AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant borrower brought a breach of contract and promissory estoppel action against respondent lenders after the borrower's loan application was denied. The Charleston County Circuit Court (South Carolina) granted summary judgment to the lenders, finding that the borrower's claims were barred by the lender statute of frauds, S.C. Code Ann. § 37-10-107 (2002). The borrower's motion to alter or amend was denied. The borrower appealed.


OVERVIEW: The borrower challenged the constitutionality of § 37-10-107, which prohibited certain legal and equitable actions arising out of the loan of money where there was no writing evidencing the parties' alleged agreement. The borrower argued that the exclusion of consumer loans from the statute was not related to the subject of consumer law and, therefore, the statute should not have been located in the Consumer Protection Code. However, § 37-10-107 touched upon consumer issues and gave reasonable notice of the subject matter of the act. Accordingly, the statute did not violate S.C. Const. art. III, § 17. With regard to the application of § 37-10-107, the trial court correctly found that the borrower's claims were subject to the writing requirement of the statute because its members sought a loan from the lender for an amount in excess of $ 50,000. None of the documents or exhibits contained any indication of an agreement between the parties as contemplated by § 37-10-107. There were no genuine issues of material fact regarding the borrower's causes of action for breach of contract and promissory estoppel, even when the evidence was viewed in the light most favorable to the borrower.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment.

Austin v. Stokes-Craven Holding Corp.


Opinion No. 26784


SUPREME COURT OF SOUTH CAROLINA


387 S.C. 22; 691 S.E.2d 135; 2010 S.C. LEXIS 31


June 11, 2009, Heard 
March 8, 2010, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Rehearing denied by Austin v. Stokes-Craven, 2010 S.C. LEXIS 170 (S.C., Apr. 21, 2010)
Costs and fees proceeding at Austin v. Stokes-Craven Holding Corp., 406 S.C. 187, 750 S.E.2d 78, 2013 S.C. LEXIS 279 (S.C., 2013)
Related proceeding at, Remanded by Stokes-Craven Holding Corp. v. Robinson, 2015 S.C. LEXIS 328 (S.C., Sept. 9, 2015)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeal From Clarendon County. John C. Hayes, III, Circuit Court Judge.



DISPOSITION: AFFIRMED IN PART, REVERSED IN PART, AND REMANDED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff buyer sued defendant automobile dealership under common law and state and federal law seeking recovery for damages sustained from his purchase of a defective used truck. Based on a jury's verdict, the Clarendon County Court (South Carolina) awarded the buyer $ 26,371 in actual damages and $ 216,600 in punitive damages. Both parties sought review of the trial court's decision.


OVERVIEW: The buyer contended that the dealership failed to disclose that the used truck had sustained "wreck" damage from its prior owner; he also contended that the dealership's sales people made numerous false statements about the prior owner, the truck's condition, and applicable warranties prior to his purchase. On appeal, the court upheld admission of the testimony of the buyer's expert witnesses. The dealership was properly denied a judgment notwithstanding the verdict because the buyer's testimony that the truck was not worth anything and his expert's testimony that the truck had a zero retail value established actual damages. However, the buyer was erroneously awarded recovery under the Federal Odometer Act, 49 U.S.C.S. § 32710(a), since he failed to show a violation of this law with the requisite intent to defraud. The verdicts on the fraud and state law unfair trade practices claims were not inconsistent, and the punitive damage award was not excessive. As to the buyer's cross-appeal, the court found that he was not entitled to prejudgment interest, but that he should have been awarded attorney's fees under the South Carolina Dealer's Act, S.C. Code Ann. § 56-15-110(1) (2006).


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial judge's award of $ 1,500 in actual damages and $ 4,500 in attorney's fees awarded the buyer under the Odometer Act and the trial court's denial of his request for attorney's fees and costs under the Dealer's Act and remanded the case for entry of a judgment reflecting these rulings. The court affirmed the trial court's judgment in all other respects.

 

76 of 275 DOCUMENTS


Hendricks v. S.C. Dep't of Corr.


Opinion No. 26744


SUPREME COURT OF SOUTH CAROLINA


385 S.C. 625; 686 S.E.2d 191; 2009 S.C. LEXIS 536


September 17, 2009, Submitted 
November 23, 2009, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeal From Richland County. Carolyn C. Matthews, Administrative Law Court Judge.



DISPOSITION: AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant inmate sought review in the Richland County Administrative Law Court (South Carolina) of a decision of respondent, the South Carolina Department of Corrections (SCDC), rejecting the inmate's challenge to the constitutionality of SCDC Policy GA-01.03. The Administrative Law Court affirmed the SCDC's decision, and the inmate appealed.


OVERVIEW: SCDC Policy GA-01.03, Access to the Courts, provided limits on inmates' ability to photocopy documents in the law library, among other restrictions. The inmate argued that not being able to photocopy legal documents he generated unconstitutionally hindered his access to the courts. The court held that the inmate could not challenge the constitutionality of the policy because he failed to state that he had suffered an actual injury due to the policy. He merely alleged that he had almost missed deadlines due to the photocopy policy and that he might be injured in the future by missing a deadline. This fells short of the actual injury requirement. Because no actual injury had occurred, the inmate's meaningful access to the courts had not been unconstitutionally hindered. The court cautioned SCDC that its policy not allowing inmates to make photocopies when he or she is willing and able to pay was not reasonable.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the administrative law court's decision denying the inmate's challenge.

State v. Allen


Opinion No. 26743


SUPREME COURT OF SOUTH CAROLINA


386 S.C. 93; 687 S.E.2d 21; 2009 S.C. LEXIS 526


September 15, 2009, Heard 
November 16, 2009, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Rehearing denied by State v. Allen, 2009 S.C. LEXIS 623 (S.C., Dec. 17, 2009)
Petition denied by State v. Allen, 2010 S.C. LEXIS 208 (S.C., May 24, 2010)
US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Allen v. South Carolina, 130 S. Ct. 3329, 176 L. Ed. 2d 1229, 2010 U.S. LEXIS 4276 (U.S., 2010)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeal From Richland County. G. Thomas Cooper, Jr., Circuit Court Judge.



DISPOSITION: AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant pleaded guilty in the Richland County Circuit Court (South Carolina) to two counts of murder, assault and battery with intent to kill, arson in the second and third degrees, and pointing and presenting a firearm. After a sentencing hearing conducted by the trial judge, defendant was sentenced to death for the murders and given lesser sentences on the other charges. He appealed the trial court's imposition of a death sentence.


OVERVIEW: Defendant went on a killing spree. He told police he began killing people because an inmate in federal prison had told him he could get him a job as a mafia hit man, but was tired of waiting and embarked on his own killing spree. The court held that it was clear from the entirety of the sentencing order that the death sentence was based upon defendant's characteristics and his crimes. Defendant also challenged the constitutionality of S.C. Code Ann. § 16-3-20(C) because he was denied the opportunity to present mitigating evidence to a jury. The court held that the sentencing judge was required to and did consider mitigating factors; defendant's due process rights were not violated.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's sentence of death.

Law Firm of Paul L. Erickson, P.A. v. Boykin


Opinion No. 26694


SUPREME COURT OF SOUTH CAROLINA


383 S.C. 497; 681 S.E.2d 575; 2009 S.C. LEXIS 204


May 28, 2009, Heard 
July 27, 2009, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Rehearing denied by Law Firm of Erickson v. Boykin, 2009 S.C. LEXIS 605 (S.C., Sept. 3, 2009)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeal from Horry County. B. Hicks Harwell, Jr., Circuit Court Judge.
Law Firm of Paul L. Erickson, P.A., v. Boykin, 375 S.C. 204, 651 S.E.2d 606, 2007 S.C. App. LEXIS 136 (S.C. Ct. App., 2007)



DISPOSITION: REVERSED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner judgment creditor sought to enforce a North Carolina judgment against respondent judgment debtors. The trial court upheld the judgment creditors' challenge to the North Carolina default judgment. The Court of Appeals of South Carolina affirmed. The judgment creditor appealed.


OVERVIEW: The judgment creditor argued that the trial court failed to accord the proper presumption of correctness due the North Carolina judgment and that judgment debtors did not meet their burden of proof to show the judgment was invalid. The intermediate appellate court held, however, that S.C. Code Ann. § 15-35-940 shifted the burden of proving the foreign judgment's regularity to the creditor when the debtor challenged the judgment in South Carolina court proceedings, and that presumption of validity of a foreign judgment did not apply in that circumstance. The appellate court found, however, that the presumption of personal jurisdiction presumed when a foreign judgment appeared on its face to be a record of a court of general jurisdiction arose not from state case law, but rather from the United States Constitution. This reading of § 15-35-940 arguably violated the Supremacy Clause, U.S. Const. art. VI, cl. 2, and/or the Privileges and Immunities Clause, U.S. Const. art. IV, § 2, cl. 1. The Jay Group, Ltd. v. The Bootery of Haywood Mall, Inc., 335 S.C. 114, 515 S.E.2d 542 (S.C. Ct. App. 1999) was overruled. The last sentence of § 15-35-940(B) was stricken.


OUTCOME: The decision of the intermediate appellate court was reversed. The last sentence of S.C. Code Ann. § 15-35-940(B) was stricken.

 

79 of 275 DOCUMENTS


State v. Neuman


Opinion No. 26676


SUPREME COURT OF SOUTH CAROLINA


384 S.C. 395; 683 S.E.2d 268; 2009 S.C. LEXIS 162


March 17, 2009, Heard 
June 29, 2009, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeal From McCormick County. William P. Keesley, Circuit Court Judge.



DISPOSITION: AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was convicted in the McCormick County Circuit Court (South Carolina) of taking of hostages by an inmate (S.C. Code Ann. § 24-13-450 (2007)). He appealed, contending that § 24-13-450 was unconstitutionally vague.


OVERVIEW: Defendant and another inmate handcuffed three corrections officers (COs), two of whom were also assaulted, locked them in a storage locker, and unsuccessfully tried to escape. Defendant argued that as § 24-13-450 did not include the word "unlawfully," it effectively criminalized lawful activity. The high court held that while defense counsel did not cite the vagueness doctrine in his trial argument, his assertions regarding U.S. Const. amends. V and XIV, and his claim that § 24-13-450 was "almost impossible to defend," included a due process challenge, and were sufficiently broad to incorporate the vagueness doctrine. Defendant did not have standing to challenge § 24-13-450 for vagueness, as his conduct fell within even the most restrictive application of the statute. He could be found guilty under § 24-13-450 if, by coercion or physical force, he carried away any person as a hostage "or for any other reason whatsoever." Thus, the COs did not have to be "hostages" for defendant to be convicted. Further, § 24-13-450 was not unconstitutionally vague, as a person of ordinary intelligence and judgment would be sufficiently apprised of conduct that would come within its purview.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

Sojourner v. Town of St. George


Opinion No. 26680


SUPREME COURT OF SOUTH CAROLINA


383 S.C. 171; 679 S.E.2d 182; 2009 S.C. LEXIS 161


June 24, 2009, Submitted 
June 24, 2009, Submitted 
June 29, 2009, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeal from Dorchester County. Patrick R. Watts, Master-In-Equity.



DISPOSITION: AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant owner of property sued respondents town and county, challenging an ordinance adopted by the town which authorized the sale of its water and sewer system to the county. The Dorchester County Master-In-Equity (South Carolina) held that the town was authorized to sell the water and sewer system pursuant to the ordinance. The owner appealed.


OVERVIEW: The owner alleged that the town's agreement to sell violated S.C. Code Ann. § 5-31-620 (2004) and S.C. Code Ann. § 5-31-640 (2004) because there was no written petition from any residents, qualified registered electors, or freeholders within the town requesting an election on the matter. The appellate court found that there was no compelling interest to support § 5-31-640, and that, thus, it violated the Equal Protection Clause. Municipal utilities, as with other matters that affected public health and safety of the entire community, were of general interest to the community. The owner's argument that property owners were so much more affected by water and sewer system operations than other voters so as to create a compelling state interest in special statutory protection was meritless. Further, § 5-31-620 and § 5-31-640 were mutually dependent and were not severable, and thus § 5-31-620 was also unconstitutional. Pursuant to the authority granted in S.C. Code Ann. § 5-7-40 in conjunction with the expressed legislative intent in S.C. Code Ann. § 5-7-10, the town was fully authorized to sell its water and sewer system pursuant to the ordinance.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

Edwards v. State


Opinion No. 26662


SUPREME COURT OF SOUTH CAROLINA


383 S.C. 82; 678 S.E.2d 412; 2009 S.C. LEXIS 145


June 3, 2009, Heard 
June 4, 2009, Filed


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The state supreme court, exercising its original jurisdiction, considered petitioner aggrieved parties' request for declarations that respondent Governor had to request, accept, and distribute federal funds the State was eligible to receive from the federal government under a federal law. Although the State had appropriated the funds in its budget, the Governor had not formally applied for them be he disagreed with how the money had to be spent.


OVERVIEW: In response to an economic recession, the United States Congress passed a law, the American Recovery and Reinvestment Act of 2009, Pub. L. No. 111-5 (ARRA), that permitted State Fiscal Stabilization funds to be paid to the states. The United States Congress specified in the ARRA how the federal funds were to be spent. The General Assembly appropriated the funds in its budget, and indicated an intent to accept and spend the funds as directed under the ARRA. The Governor disagreed with how the funds had to be spent and vetoed provisions of the budget, including the ARRA funds, but the General Assembly overrode the vetoes. However, the Governor did not formally apply to receive the funds because he still claimed an absolute discretion to determine how the funds were to be spent. The aggrieved parties sought declarations from the state supreme court that the Governor had to request, accept, and distribute the funds. The state supreme court issued a writ of mandamus directing the Governor to apply for them and see to it that the State received them, as it was his job under S.C. Const. art. IV, § 15 to see that the laws were executed and not to determine what the law should be.


OUTCOME: The state supreme court issued a writ of mandamus compelling the Governor to apply for the federal funds at issue, and take all legal and necessary steps to effectuate the State's receipt of those funds for the purposes set forth by the United States Congress, based on the requirement that he was legally obligated to do so.

API v. S.C. Dep't of Revenue


Opinion No. 26645


SUPREME COURT OF SOUTH CAROLINA


382 S.C. 572; 677 S.E.2d 16; 2009 S.C. LEXIS 109


January 6, 2009, Heard 
May 4, 2009, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
ORIGINAL JURISDICTION.



DISPOSITION: ACT DECLARED UNCONSTITUTIONAL.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioners institute and corporation filed a complaint against respondents State, legislators, and others, seeking a declaratory judgment that 2008 S.C. Acts 338, § 3 (Act), found at S.C. Code Ann. §§ 12-28-340, 12-36-2120, was unconstitutional as violating the one subject provision of S.C. Const. art. III, § 17. Intervenor association was added as a party.


OVERVIEW: The institute and the corporation argued that § 3 of the Act violated the one subject rule. The State and the legislators did not contend that § 3 was related to the other sections, but submitted that §§ 1, 2 related to one subject. The association argued that all three sections of the Act pertained to one subject since (1) §§ 1, 2 provided tax exemptions while § 3 protected a tax credit and (2) all three sections provided a direct benefit to businesses. The court disagreed. While §§ 1, 2 set forth tax exemptions, protection of a tax credit was not mentioned in § 3. Instead, § 3 required, among other things, that motor fuel terminals offer petroleum products that had not been blended with ethanol. It was also significant that the heading for § 3, found in the body of the Act, made no mention of taxes, though the headings for §§ 1, 2 did so explicitly. The provisions of the Act did not relate to one subject, and it violated S.C. Const. art. III, § 17. To sever only part of the Act would have required the court to go beyond its proper role and to intrude into the province of the legislature. Thus the entire Act was found in violation of S.C. Const. art. III, § 17.


OUTCOME: 2008 S.C. Acts 338, found at S.C. Code Ann. §§ 12-28-340, 12-36-2120, was declared unconstitutional in its entirety.

Berry v. State


Opinion No. 26618


SUPREME COURT OF SOUTH CAROLINA


381 S.C. 630; 675 S.E.2d 425; 2009 S.C. LEXIS 59


November 19, 2008, Submitted 
March 23, 2009, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeal From Cherokee County. Doyet A. Early, III, Post-Conviction Relief Judge. ON WRIT OF CERTIORARI.



DISPOSITION: REVERSED AND REMANDED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant pleaded guilty in the Cherokee County Circuit Court (South Carolina) to manufacturing methamphetamine, second offense, and was sentenced to a term of imprisonment. As part of a plea agreement, an accompanying possession with intent to distribute methamphetamine charge was dismissed. Defendant's application for postconviction relief was denied. Defendant filed a petition for a writ of certiorari.


OVERVIEW: Defendant's plea was enhanced to a second offense by defendant's prior conviction for possession of drug paraphernalia. Defendant contended that his plea was improperly enhanced and that he did not receive effective assistance of counsel as his plea counsel neither challenged the State of South Carolina's reliance on the paraphernalia conviction for enhancement purposes, nor informed defendant of the potential challenge. On appeal, the court found that defendant's conviction for possession of drug paraphernalia could not be used for enhancement purposes as it did not relate to drugs as statutorily mandated by S.C. Code Ann. §§ 44-53-470 and 44-53-375(B)(2) (Supp. 2007). Further, plea counsel's failure to inform defendant of the potential challenge to the use of the paraphernalia conviction for enhancement purposes amounted to deficient representation under the Sixth Amendment. Moreover, defendant established prejudice from the representation as he repeatedly said at the postconviction hearing that he would have gone to trial had he known that his paraphernalia conviction did not qualify as a prior offense for enhancement purposes.


OUTCOME: The court granted defendant postconviction relief and returned him to his pre-guilty plea position, defendant's conviction and sentence for manufacturing methamphetamine were vacated, and the accompanying indictment for possession with intent to distribute methamphetamine was restored as an active charge. The charges were remanded to the general sessions court for disposition.

O'Brien v. S.C. Orbit


Opinion No. 26533


SUPREME COURT OF SOUTH CAROLINA


380 S.C. 38; 668 S.E.2d 396; 2008 S.C. LEXIS 253


February 5, 2008, Heard 
August 18, 2008, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Petition withdrawn by O'Brien v. Sc Orbit, 2008 S.C. LEXIS 339 (S.C., Nov. 20, 2008)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeal From Charleston County.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner city employee filed a declaratory judgment action against respondents, the city, a retirement benefits investment trust, its trustees, and a municipal association, seeking a ruling that the city's decision to place funds for employee retirement benefits in a trust that invested in equity securities violated S.C. Const. art. X, § 11.


OVERVIEW: The city provided its employees with retirement pay and other post-employment benefits (OPEBs). A regulation of the South Carolina Governmental Accounting Standards Board allowed a city to pay its annual required contribution into an irrevocable trust established to earn a return sufficient to meet all current and future OPEBs obligations. The municipal association established the trust to receive funds from municipalities, to invest those funds in a way seeking the highest rate of return, and to disburse those funds in order to defray the future expenses of OPEBs. The city indicated that it would invest in the trust only on the condition that the investment portfolio included equity securities. The high court held the city's investment in the trust was designed to circumvent S.C. Const. art. X, § 11, which precluded political subdivisions from investing funds intended for post-employment benefits in equity securities. Because the obvious purpose of the trust was to invest in equity securities and attempt to circumvent the constitutional prohibition on doing so, it had an illegal purpose and therefore violated S.C. Code Ann. § 62-7-404.


OUTCOME: The high court declared the city's investment in the trust unconstitutional, ordered the trust dissolved, and required the return of funds invested to the members.

In re Care & Treatment of Lasure


Opinion No. 26528


SUPREME COURT OF SOUTH CAROLINA


379 S.C. 144; 666 S.E.2d 228; 2008 S.C. LEXIS 236


June 26, 2008, Heard 
August 11, 2008, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Magistrate's recommendation at, Habeas corpus proceeding at LaSure v. S.C. Dep't of Mental Health, 2014 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 77340 (D.S.C., Jan. 17, 2014)
Related proceeding at LaSure v. S. C. Mental Health, 2016 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 128146 (D.S.C., Feb. 29, 2016)
Related proceeding at Lasure v. Scaturo, 2016 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 175959 (D.S.C., Sept. 15, 2016)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeal From Edgefield County. William P. Keesley, Circuit Court Judge.



DISPOSITION: AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: A jury in the Edgefield County Circuit Court (South Carolina) found appellant to be a sexually violent predator, and the trial court then entered an order of commitment to the Department of Mental Health for appellant's long term control, care, and treatment. Appellant sought review.


OVERVIEW: Appellant pled guilty to one count of lewd act upon a child. Appellant argued that the Sexually Violent Predator (SVP) Act, S.C. Code § 44-48-10 et seq. (Supp. 2007), violated S.C. Const. art. XII, § 2. Specifically, appellant complained he should have been placed into this particular SVP program from the beginning of his criminal sentence. The appellate court disagreed. Even if S.C. Const. art. XII, § 2 was read to require some rehabilitation for inmates, it did not mandate any specific programs that were required to be provided by the general assembly or the Department of Corrections and, more importantly, it did not mandate any particular timetable for the furnishing of any rehabilitative services. The South Carolina Constitution did not mandate the provision of any specific type of rehabilitative program. The SVP Act did not violate S.C. Const. art. XII, § 2.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

 

86 of 275 DOCUMENTS


State v. McGrier


Opinion No. 26489


SUPREME COURT OF SOUTH CAROLINA


378 S.C. 320; 663 S.E.2d 15; 2008 S.C. LEXIS 327


February 21, 2008, Heard 
June 23, 2008, Refiled


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeal From Greenwood County. Kenneth G. Goode, Circuit Court Judge.
State v. McGrier, 2008 S.C. LEXIS 143 (S.C., May 12, 2008)



DISPOSITION: REVERSED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant challenged an order of the Circuit Court, Greenwood County (South Carolina), which found that he willfully violated the terms and conditions of the Community Supervision Program (CSP) of the South Carolina Department of Probation, Parole, and Pardon Services, revoked his CSP, and sentenced him to six-months imprisonment. Defendant argued that the CSP statute, S.C. Code Ann. § 24-21-560(D) (2007), was unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: Defendant pleaded guilty to distribution of crack cocaine. After serving 85 percent of his term of imprisonment, defendant was released to the CSP. Defendant argued that the revocation from the CSP resulted in the imposition of a greater sentence than his original sentence without the benefit of the requisite constitutional protections. The supreme court held that the circuit court erred in re-incarcerating defendant because he had served 85 percent of the original sentence and 19 months as a result of several CSP revocations. Defendant had not received notice that the terms of his original sentence would be modified and that a greater punishment would be imposed. Although he was not convicted a second time, he was given an additional sentence for violating the CSP. Section 24-21-560(D) limited the total amount of time an inmate could be incarcerated after a CSP revocation to the length of the remaining balance of the sentence for the no parole offense. A circuit court could not impose a sentence for a CSP revocation that would result in an inmate being incarcerated for an aggregate period of time that extended beyond the unsuspended portion of the original sentence.


OUTCOME: The supreme court reversed the judgment of the circuit court.

 

87 of 275 DOCUMENTS


S.C. Pub. Interest Found. v. Harrell


Opinion No. 26506


SUPREME COURT OF SOUTH CAROLINA


378 S.C. 441; 663 S.E.2d 52; 2008 S.C. LEXIS 186


March 4, 2008, Heard 
June 23, 2008, Filed


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioners, a public interest foundation and individuals, filed an action against respondents, the Speaker of the South Carolina House of Representatives, the President of the South Carolina Senate, and the State of South Carolina, claiming that certain acts passed by the general assembly violated the one subject rule of S.C. Const. art. III, § 17.


OVERVIEW: The supreme court held that 2007 S.C. Act. 49, §§ 3 and 4 violated S.C. Const. art. III, § 17 because they did not deal with the South Carolina Critical Needs Nursing Initiative Act. However, those provisions were severable from the remainder of Act 49, and the surviving sections of the Act could stand alone. The purposes of Act 49 could be complied with by upholding the Critical Needs Nursing Initiative Act. The supreme court found that 2007 S.C. Act 83 §§ 5, 6, and 8 did not relate to the main purpose of the Energy Freedom and Rural Development Act, and it severed those sections. The remaining sections of Act 83 sufficiently related to the goals of promoting hydrogen and alternative energy as to fall within the ambit of the Act. 2007 S.C. Act 110, § 57 was relevant to the stated title of the Research and Development Tax Credit Reform Act and did not violate the one-subject rule. The majority of 2007 S.C. Act 116 was sufficiently related to the subject of raising revenue to withstand a one-subject challenge. However, 2007 S.C. Act 116, §§ 11 and 68 were unrelated to the subject of revenue raising and violated S.C. Const. art. III, § 17.


OUTCOME: The supreme court struck those provisions of the acts that violated the one-subject provision and upheld the remaining portions of the challenged acts.

 

88 of 275 DOCUMENTS


Drummond v. State


Opinion No. 26498


SUPREME COURT OF SOUTH CAROLINA


378 S.C. 362; 662 S.E.2d 587; 2008 S.C. LEXIS 160


April 16, 2008, Heard 
June 2, 2008, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeal from Richland County. J. Ernest Kinard, Jr., Circuit Court Judge.



DISPOSITION: AFFIRMED IN PART; REVERSED IN PART; REMANDED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant representative sued respondents, the State of South Carolina and South Carolina Department of Revenue, in the Beaufort County Circuit Court (South Carolina), for a putative class of persons denied a state sales tax exemption. The court granted summary judgment against the representative on his cause of action under the South Carolina Taxpayers' Bill of Rights and dismissed the remaining causes of action. The representative appealed.


OVERVIEW: The representative contended that the South Carolina Department of Revenue improperly promulgated regulations that did not conform to S.C. Code Ann. § 12-36-2120(28)(b) (Supp. 2007) because the regulations allowed a sales tax exemption only for diabetic supplies sold pursuant to a prescription or written authorization, which the representative claimed was not required under the statute. On appeal, the court found that the representative's allegations regarding the promulgation of regulations by the Department of Revenue in disregard of § 12-36-2120(28)(b) failed to state a cause of action under S.C. Code Ann. § 12-58-170 (2000) (repealed 2005) because § 12-58-170 specifically referred to disregarding Department of Revenue's published procedures which did not include the alleged disregard of a statute. Moreover, the trial court properly refused injunctive or mandamus relief as provided in S.C. Code Ann. § 12-60-60 (Supp. 2007). However, the representative was entitled to pursue the issue as an action in circuit court under S.C. Code Ann. § 1-23-150 (2005) for a declaratory judgment that the regulations exceeded the Department of Revenue's authority.


OUTCOME: The trial court's dismissal of the representative's action regarding the alleged improper promulgation of regulations by the Department of Revenue in violation of a statute was affirmed. The trial court's dismissal of the representative's action for a declaratory judgment that the Department of Revenue exceed its authority in promulgating regulations was reversed, and the cause was remanded for a determination on the merits.

 

89 of 275 DOCUMENTS


State v. Bolin


Opinion No. 26494


SUPREME COURT OF SOUTH CAROLINA


378 S.C. 96; 662 S.E.2d 38; 2008 S.C. LEXIS 152


March 5, 2008, Heard 
May 19, 2008, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Decision reached on appeal by State v. Bolin, 381 S.C. 557, 673 S.E.2d 885, 2009 S.C. App. LEXIS 24 (S.C. Ct. App., 2009)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeal From York County. John C. Hayes, III, Circuit Court Judge.



DISPOSITION: AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was indicted for, among other things, possession of a pistol by a person under 21 years of age pursuant to S.C. Code Ann. § 16-23-30 (2003 & Supp. 2007). He filed a motion to quash the indictment on the ground that § 16-23-30 was unconstitutional. The State challenged a judgment of the Circuit Court of York County (South Carolina), which granted the motion to quash.


OVERVIEW: Defendant argued that because he was old enough to be sui juris, and S.C. Const. art. I, § 20 granted him the right to bear arms, he could not be charged with a crime for handgun possession. The supreme court held that S.C. Code Ann. § 16-23-30 (2003 & Supp. 2007) did not violate S.C. Const. Art. I, § 20. Although a person under the age of 21 was prohibited from possessing a handgun, that did not prevent a person under the age of 21 from possessing other types of guns. Defendant also argued that S.C. Code Ann. § 16-23-30 (2003 & Supp. 2007) violated S.C. Const. art. XVII, § 14 because the age group of 18 to 20-year-olds was treated differently than adults. The supreme court found that S.C. Code Ann. § 16-23-30 (2003 & Supp. 2007) violated the plain language of S.C. Const. art. XVII, § 14. By expressly allowing the regulation of the sale of alcoholic beverages to the 18 to 20-year-old age group, and not stating any other situation in which the general assembly could restrict the rights of that age group, art. XVII, § 14 precluded the general assembly from prohibiting that age group's possession of handguns.


OUTCOME: The supreme court affirmed the judgment.

McKnight v. State


Opinion No. 26484


SUPREME COURT OF SOUTH CAROLINA


378 S.C. 33; 661 S.E.2d 354; 2008 S.C. LEXIS 142


April 1, 2008, Submitted 
May 12, 2008, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeal from Horry County. James E. Brogdon, Circuit Court Judge. B. Hicks Harwell, Jr., Post-Conviction Relief Judge.
State v. McKnight, 352 S.C. 635, 576 S.E.2d 168, 2003 S.C. LEXIS 23 (S.C., 2003)



DISPOSITION: REVERSED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Horry County Circuit Court (South Carolina) entered a judgment that denied petitioner's application for postconviction relief that alleged numerous grounds for finding ineffective assistance of counsel in a case where she was convicted of homicide by child abuse pursuant to S.C. Code Ann. § 16-3-85 (2003). The state supreme court granted a petition for writ of certiorari filed by petitioner to review that decision.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner gave birth to a nearly full-term stillborn baby girl. An autopsy revealed inflammations, as well as the presence of a by-product of cocaine. The autopsy report concluded that death occurred secondary to the inflammations and cocaine consumption. Petitioner was subsequently charged with homicide by child abuse pursuant to S.C. Code Ann. § 16-3-85 (2003). The public defender represented her. A first trial ended in a mistrial. At the second trial, a jury convicted her of that charge. The state supreme court affirmed. The trial court then found on petitioner's application for postconviction relief that trial counsel had not been ineffective. The state supreme court found that (1) counsel provided an inadequate defense in the second trial by presenting an expert witness who bolstered the State's theory of the cause of death, (2) counsel was ineffective for failing to investigate contradictory medical evidence and an expert's opinion ruling out natural causes of death, (3) counsel was ineffective for failing to challenge the jury charge on criminal intent, and (4) counsel was ineffective for failing to offer an autopsy report into evidence.


OUTCOME: The state supreme court reversed the trial court's judgment.

State v. McGrier


Opinion No. 26489


SUPREME COURT OF SOUTH CAROLINA


2008 S.C. LEXIS 143


February 21, 2008, Heard 
May 12, 2008, Filed


NOTICE:

THIS DECISION IS NOT FINAL UNTIL TIME EXPIRES TO FILE REHEARING MOTION AND, IF FILED, DETERMINED.


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Opinion withdrawn by, Substituted opinion at, Rehearing denied by State v. McGrier, 2008 S.C. LEXIS 327 (S.C., June 23, 2008)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [*1] 
Appeal From Abbeville County. Kenneth G. Goode, Circuit Court Judge.



DISPOSITION: REVERSED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: After the Abbeville County Circuit Court (South Carolina) revoked defendant's community supervision program (CSP) and ordered him to serve a six-month term in imprisonment, he appealed.


OVERVIEW: Defendant alleged on appeal that the CSP statute, specifically S.C. Code Ann. § 24-21-560(D), was unconstitutional given a revocation from the CSP resulted in the imposition of a greater sentence than his original sentence without the benefit of the requisite constitutional protections. The instant court agreed. If, as in defendant's case, successive revocations were ordered, the two-year period could produce a never-ending cycle of participation in CSP and an incarceration period which would clearly exceed or extend past the originally ordered term of incarceration. As a result, defendant's procedural due process rights were violated by the circuit court's order. Although defendant was on notice that the completion of the CSP was a requirement of his originally-imposed sentence, he would not have been aware that his participation in this program could potentially be in perpetuity. Furthermore, a literal interpretation of the CSP statute would improperly permit a CSP violation to become a separate and distinct criminal offense from that which a defendant was convicted without the benefit of the requisite Sixth Amendment constitutional protections.


OUTCOME: The judgment was reversed.
