Foothills Brewing Concern, Inc. v. City of Greenville


Opinion No. 26467


SUPREME COURT OF SOUTH CAROLINA


377 S.C. 355; 660 S.E.2d 264; 2008 S.C. LEXIS 109


January 9, 2008, Heard 
March 31, 2008, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Rehearing denied by Brewing v. City of Greenville, 2008 S.C. LEXIS 155 (S.C., May 7, 2008)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeal From Greenville County. John C. Few, Circuit Court Judge.



DISPOSITION: REVERSED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Respondent restaurants and bars filed a declaratory judgment action against appellant city in the Greenville County Circuit Court (South Carolina), contending that Greenville, S.C., Ordinance No. 2006-91 was invalid and requesting injunctive relief. The court issued an order declaring that the ordinance was both unconstitutional and preempted by South Carolina law, and permanently enjoined the city from enforcing the ordinance. The city appealed.


OVERVIEW: After the State of South Carolina enacted the South Carolina Clean Indoor Air Act of 1990, S.C. Code Ann. § 44-95-10 et seq. (2002), the city enacted Greenville, S.C., Ordinance No. 2006-91 to more comprehensively regulate smoking in public places. The city argued that the ordinance was not preempted by South Carolina law and was consistent with both the Constitution and the general law of the State of South Carolina. On appeal, the court found that Act 445 amended S.C. Code Ann. § 44-95-20, but did not preempt the city from enacting the ordinance, and the trial court erred when it isolated a phrase from S.C. Code Ann. § 16-17-504 and interpreted it in such a way as to accomplish preemption under the Clean Indoor Air Act. Moreover, the Clean Indoor Air Act did not preempt the entire field of indoor smoking. In addition, the ordinance did not violate S.C. Const. art. VIII, § 14 because the city did not seek to criminalize any conduct. As such, the ordinance did not set aside the criminal laws of the State of South Carolina. Finally, the ordinance was consistent with the Constitution and the general law of the State of South Carolina.


OUTCOME: The trial court's order was reversed, and the ordinance passed by the city was held to be valid and enforceable.

State v. Dingle


Opinion No. 26444


SUPREME COURT OF SOUTH CAROLINA


376 S.C. 643; 659 S.E.2d 101; 2008 S.C. LEXIS 57


October 30, 2007, Heard 
February 27, 2008, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Rehearing denied by State v. Dingle, 2008 S.C. LEXIS 385 (S.C., Apr. 16, 2008)
Magistrate's recommendation at, Habeas corpus proceeding at Dingle v. Stevenson, 2009 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 110503 (D.S.C., June 25, 2009)
Magistrate's recommendation at, Habeas corpus proceeding at Dingle v. Stevenson, 2014 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 183211 (D.S.C., Sept. 16, 2014)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeal From Sumter County. Howard P. King, Circuit Court Judge.



DISPOSITION: AFFIRMED AS MODIFIED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: A postconviction relief court vacated defendant's sentences and remanded the matter to the circuit court for sentencing consistent with the intent of the plea agreement or for a new trial. Upon remand, the Sumter County Circuit Court (South Carolina) resentenced defendant rather than grant him a new trial. Defendant challenged the order.


OVERVIEW: Defendant argued that resentencing him would have triggered the no-parole clause in S.C. Code Ann. § 24-21-640 (2007), thereby thwarting his plea agreement in which the State agreed that he would be eligible for parole. However, defendant's interpretation of the statute that the remand of his sentences was a subsequent conviction rendering him ineligible for parole led to an absurd result. Thus, specific performance of the plea bargain could still be accomplished. Moreover, the State had affirmatively stated its intent to comply with the plea bargain. Defendant's argument that he was entitled to choose between resentencing or a new trial failed as the State had not violated the agreement. It was only the way the plea judge structured the sentence that rendered defendant parole ineligible. Finally, defendant had received the benefit of his bargain even though the death sentence later became unconstitutional for minors where the parties had agreed to structure the sentence such that he was eligible for parole. The order was modified to the extent the circuit court made findings inconsistent with the parole board's authority and the Ex Post Facto Clause.


OUTCOME: The order was modified to the extent the circuit made findings inconsistent with law. The order was affirmed as modified.

Moore v. Moore


Opinion No. 26429


SUPREME COURT OF SOUTH CAROLINA


376 S.C. 467; 657 S.E.2d 743; 2008 S.C. LEXIS 31


November 15, 2007, Heard 
February 11, 2008, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeal From Charleston County. Jocelyn B. Cate, Family Court Judge.



DISPOSITION: AFFIRMED AS MODIFIED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Respondent wife filed an action pursuant to S.C. Code Ann. § 20-4-50(a) (Supp. 2006) against appellant husband in the Charleston County Family Court (South Carolina), for an emergency hearing and an order of protection against the husband. The court entered an order of protection from domestic abuse in favor of the wife and denied the husband's motion for reconsideration. The husband appealed.


OVERVIEW: The husband challenged the issuance of the family court's order on due process grounds under U.S. Const. amend. XIV, § 1 and S.C. Const. art. I, § 3. First, he contended that permitting the hearing under S.C. Code Ann. § 20-4-50 within 24 hours of the service of the petition violated due process. Secondly, he asserted that the family court's denial of his motion for a continuance constituted an abuse of discretion and violated his right to due process as he was prohibited from procuring counsel. On appeal, the court found that the husband was provided procedural due process prior to the issuance of the order of protection. However, the order of protection issued pursuant to the emergency hearing was temporary and did not represent a final adjudication of the merits of the action. Therefore, the finding of physical abuse was not a final adjudication and was not to be used against the husband in future litigation. Finally, neither the South Carolina Protection from Domestic Abuse Act, nor the family court's decision denying the husband a continuance constituted a violation of the Equal Protection Clauses under U.S. Const. amend. XIV, § 1 and S.C. Const. art. I, § 3.


OUTCOME: The judgment was modified to make the issuance of the order of protection a temporary order, rather than a final adjudicative order, and, as modified, the judgment was affirmed.

Layman v. State


Opinion No. 26427


SUPREME COURT OF SOUTH CAROLINA


376 S.C. 434; 658 S.E.2d 320; 2008 S.C. LEXIS 21


September 19, 2007, Heard 
January 28, 2008, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Modified and rehearing denied by Layman v. State, 376 S.C. 434, 2008 S.C. LEXIS 90 (S.C., 2008)
Rehearing denied by Layman v. State, 2008 S.C. LEXIS 104 (S.C., Mar. 10, 2008)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeal from Richland County. John L. Breeden, Circuit Court Judge.
Layman v. State, 368 S.C. 631, 630 S.E.2d 265, 2006 S.C. LEXIS 154 (S.C., 2006)



DISPOSITION: AFFIRMED AS MODIFIED


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Circuit Court of Richland County, South Carolina, awarded attorneys' fees of $8.66 million to counsel for plaintiffs, the participants in a teachers and employee retention incentive program (TERI plaintiffs), under S.C. Code Ann. § 15-77-300 (2005) (the state action statute). Both parties, the TERI plaintiffs, and defendants, the State and the state retirement system, sought appellate review.


OVERVIEW: Because the State and the retirement system breached an unambiguous contract with the TERI plaintiffs by applying the requirements of 2005 S.C. Acts 1697, to them, there was no reasonable basis in law or fact on which to defend the breach of the contract with certain TERI participants. As such, the State and the retirement system were not substantially justified in pressing their claim, and the TERI plaintiffs were entitled to attorneys' fees under the state action statute. However, the circuit judge's award of attorneys' fees using the percentage-of-the-recovery method was improper and resulted in an award that was unreasonable under the state action statute. Thus, the award of $8.66 million to counsel for the TERI plaintiffs was vacated. A reasonable award of attorneys' fees was properly calculated using the lodestar method, enhanced by a multiplier and the addition of counsel's expenses to reflect the actual amount of work performed, expenses incurred, and the benefit obtained for all of the old TERI participants. Based on a calculation representative of this conclusion, reasonable attorneys' fees in the amount of $445,226.60 was assessed to the State and the retirement system.


OUTCOME: The circuit judge's decision to award attorneys' fees under the state action statute was affirmed, but the amount awarded was vacated and reduced using a lodestar calculation and an appropriate modifier.

Marquez v. Caudill


Opinion No. 26421


SUPREME COURT OF SOUTH CAROLINA


376 S.C. 229; 656 S.E.2d 737; 2008 S.C. LEXIS 16


November 14, 2007, Heard 
January 22, 2008, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeal From Horry County Robert S. Armstrong, Family Court Judge.



DISPOSITION: AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Horry County Family Court (South Carolina) directed respondent stepfather to retain custody of the stepson, and that respondent grandmother could have visitation with the stepson and his sister. It further directed the grandmother to pay part of the stepfather's attorney fees, and ruled that both parties pay fees of the guardians ad litem equally. Both parties appealed. The state supreme court certified the appeals from the appellate court.


OVERVIEW: A woman gave birth to a son, the stepson and soon thereafter married the stepfather. Seven years after the stepson was born, the woman and stepfather had a biological child together. A few years later, the woman committed suicide. The woman's mother, the grandmother, sought custody of the stepson and visitation with the biological daughter. The family court ruled that the stepfather could adopt the stepson and the grandmother could have visitation with the two children. Because the grandmother could earn a substantial living, the trial court had her pay part of the stepfather's attorney fees but concluded that the guardian ad litem fees should be divided evenly. The state supreme court found that the stepfather had established a psychological parent relationship with the child. It also found that the statute allowing the grandmother to have visitation, S.C. Code Ann. § 20-7-420(A)(33) (Supp. 2006), was not facially unconstitutional and awarding the grandmother visitation was in the best interests of the children to further their relationship with the mother's family. It further found that the attorney fee and guardian ad litem decisions were proper under the circumstances.


OUTCOME: The state supreme court affirmed the trial court's judgment.

State v. Morris


Opinion No. 26418


SUPREME COURT OF SOUTH CAROLINA


376 S.C. 189; 656 S.E.2d 359; 2008 S.C. LEXIS 9


October 16, 2007, Heard 
January 14, 2008, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: As Corrected January 17, 2008, February 4, 2008.


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeal from Greenville County. James W. Johnson, Jr., Circuit Court Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: After the Greenville County Circuit Court (South Carolina) denied defendant's motion to dismiss the indictment, he was convicted of engaging in a scheme to commit securities fraud and 22 counts of securities fraud. He appealed.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was the chairman of a company that sold debentures to the public and transferred the sale proceeds to its parent company. There was evidence defendant knew of the parent company's dire financial state, but misled investors with false assurances of its profitability. The appellate court held that as defendant was not compelled to appear before the state securities commissioner, or to answer questions before the commissioner's representatives, his pretrial statements to them did not entitle him to immunity. As he was not in custody when he made the statements, Miranda did not apply. There was no evidence the State pursued a civil action investigation solely to obtain evidence for a criminal prosecution. The trial court properly instructed the jury that defendant committed securities fraud if he intentionally misled investors, or knew that there was a danger his conduct would mislead them. A co-author of the State's primary practice manual on corporate law was qualified to testify as an expert on securities laws. The trial court properly excluded a bankruptcy examiner's report as hearsay, refused to dismiss the indictment, and denied defendant's request for a continuance.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

State v. Tillinghast


Opinion No. 26384


SUPREME COURT OF SOUTH CAROLINA


375 S.C. 201; 652 S.E.2d 400; 2007 S.C. LEXIS 352


September 19, 2007, Heard 
October 22, 2007, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: As Corrected October 24, 2007.


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeal From Cherokee County. J. Mark Hayes, II, Circuit Court Judge.



DISPOSITION: REVERSED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Circuit Court of Cherokee County (South Carolina), in determining that it had jurisdiction to hear the State's appeal, found that a magistrate erred by ruling that S.C. Code Ann. § 20-7-8920 was unconstitutional, directing a verdict of not guilty in defendant's favor. Thus, defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: At issue was whether the circuit court had jurisdiction to hear the State's appeal from the magistrate's orders. After review of the relevant case law regarding issues implicating a defendant's double jeopardy rights, the appeals court found that whether or not the magistrate erred in his ruling of law, defendant had been acquitted by said ruling and was thus out of court. Given the double jeopardy implications of the circuit court's reversal of that ruling, it erred by finding that the State could appeal from the magistrate's order.


OUTCOME: The judgment was reversed.

 

99 of 275 DOCUMENTS


State v. Houey


Opinion No. 26381


SUPREME COURT OF SOUTH CAROLINA


375 S.C. 106; 651 S.E.2d 314; 2007 S.C. LEXIS 333


June 5, 2007, Heard 
September 10, 2007, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: As Corrected September 24, 2007.
As Corrected September 13, 2007.


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeal From Cherokee County. J. Mark Hayes, II, Circuit Court Judge.



DISPOSITION: AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was charged with second-degree criminal sexual conduct with a minor. The solicitor moved pursuant to S.C. Code Ann. § 16-3-740(B) (2003) for an order requiring defendant to submit to testing for HIV and other diseases. The Cherokee County Circuit Court (South Carolina) ordered the testing. Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: Defendant argued the failure of S.C. Code Ann. § 16-3-740(B) to require a probable cause determination resulted in an unconstitutional invasion of his privacy. The appellate court disagreed. The special needs exception allowed a search unsupported by probable cause when special needs, beyond the normal need for law enforcement, made the warrant and probable cause requirements of the Fourth Amendment impracticable. The State had a valid interest in protecting the health and safety of victims, and a special need existed which allowed the State to test pursuant to the statute. Defendant also claimed that § 16-3-740(B) was unconstitutionally vague, inasmuch as it allowed testing without regard to the timing of an alleged crime. The appellate court disagreed. Defendant's arguments regarding the timing of testing addressed evidentiary concerns in the context of the criminal proceeding, but were not relevant in light of the State's interest in preventing the spread of HIV and Hepatitis B and the State's interest in protecting the health of alleged victims. These interests were advanced regardless of the time of testing.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

Davis v. Richland County Council


Opinion No. 26289


SUPREME COURT OF SOUTH CAROLINA


372 S.C. 497; 642 S.E.2d 740; 2007 S.C. LEXIS 98


October 4, 2006, Heard  
March 12, 2007, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeal From Richland County. J. Ernest Kinard, Jr., Circuit Court Judge.



DISPOSITION: REVERSED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Richland County Circuit Court (South Carolina) dissolved a temporary injunction and denied a motion for a preliminary injunction to stay enforcement of 2005 S.C. Acts 207 (1961), which altered the method of appointment of the members of the county recreation commission. Appellant members sought further review.


OVERVIEW: At issue was the constitutionality of 2005 S.C. Acts 207 (1961) (Act), and whether the circuit court erred in ruling that the members lacked standing to challenge the same and that they had not met the requirements of demonstrating a need for an injunction. The appeals court first held that it was error for the circuit court to rule that the members lacked standing merely because their suit was filed after their terms expired. But, because the members also sought a declaratory judgment seeking to declare the Act unconstitutional, which the circuit court did not reach in light of its finding regarding standing, the appeals court reviewed that issue in the interest of judicial economy. In so doing, the court found that the Act took the authority to recommend county recreation commission members away from the Richland County Legislative Delegation, and gave that authority to the Richland County Council. The Act also sought to devolve appointment authority of a local body away from the Legislative Delegation, and confer it upon the County Council. Hence, the Act was found to be unconstitutional special legislation, in violation of S.C. Const. art. VIII, §§ 1 and 7.


OUTCOME: The judgment was reversed.

B&A Dev., Inc. v. Georgetown County


Opinion No. 26273


SUPREME COURT OF SOUTH CAROLINA


372 S.C. 261; 641 S.E.2d 888; 2007 S.C. LEXIS 73


January 17, 2007, Heard  
February 26, 2007, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Rehearing denied by B&A Dev. v. Georgetown County, 2007 S.C. LEXIS 138 (S.C., Mar. 22, 2007)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeal From Georgetown County. Steven H. John, Circuit Court Judge.
B&A Dev., Inc. v. Georgetown County, 361 S.C. 453, 605 S.E.2d 551, 2004 S.C. App. LEXIS 301 (S.C. Ct. App., 2004)



DISPOSITION: AFFIRMED AS MODIFIED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner taxpayers filed a class action against respondents, the county, the county organizations, and the county officials, in the Georgetown County Circuit Court (South Carolina), alleging that the county had unlawfully imposed excessive taxes on their real and personal property. The circuit court dismissed the action. The Court of Appeals of South Carolina affirmed the dismissal. The taxpayers filed a request for a writ of certiorari.


OVERVIEW: The taxpayers sought a refund because of excessive millage regarding the funding of the county's school district. In addition, they captioned the case as a class action. The state supreme court found that the South Carolina Revenue Procedures Act (RPA), S.C. Code Ann. § 12-60-10 et seq. (2000 & Supp. 2006), provided an administrative remedy in the form of a refund for both real and personal property taxes under S.C. Code Ann. §§ 12-60-2560 and 12-60-2940 (2000). Thus, pursuant to S.C. Code Ann. §§ 12-60-20 and 12-60-80 (Supp. 2006) of the RPA, the taxpayers were required to exhaust their administrative remedies before proceeding to the circuit court. However, the circuit court neither certified a class nor denied class certification. Moreover, class action status was not presented as an issue to the intermediate appellate court. Therefore, the issue, on the merits, was unpreserved for review. Nonetheless, certain language in the intermediate appellate court's opinion could have been construed as commenting on the substance of the issue. Only when the class certification issue was argued could the issue have been preserved for subsequent appellate review and comment.


OUTCOME: The court granted the taxpayers' request for a writ of certiorari, affirmed the dismissal of the lawsuit for failure to exhaust administrative remedies, and vacated the intermediate appellate court's judgment to the extent that it addressed the unpreserved class action issue.

 

102 of 275 DOCUMENTS


Arnal v. Fraser


Opinion No. 26253


SUPREME COURT OF SOUTH CAROLINA


371 S.C. 512; 641 S.E.2d 419; 2007 S.C. LEXIS 16


December 7, 2006, Submitted  
January 22, 2007, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1]
Rehearing denied by Arnal v. Fraser, 2007 S.C. LEXIS 113 (S.C., Mar. 7, 2007)
US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Arnal v. Fraser, 128 S. Ct. 136, 169 L. Ed. 2d 29, 2007 U.S. LEXIS 9213 (U.S., Oct. 1, 2007)


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal From Beaufort County. Judy C. Bridges, Family Court Judge. Wayne M. Creech, Family Court Judge.
Arnal v. Arnal, 371 S.C. 10, 636 S.E.2d 864, 2006 S.C. LEXIS 343 (S.C., 2006)



DISPOSITION: AFFIRMED IN PART; REVERSED IN PART.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In post decree proceedings between a father and a mother, and while a previous appeal was pending, the Beaufort County Family Court (South Carolina) entered multiple orders addressing various issues, which were appealed by the parties.


OVERVIEW: The father argued that all orders should have been vacated because, by the time this action was filed he had moved to Georgia while the mother and the parties' son lived in North Carolina. The appellate court disagreed. The father's ordered obligations to pay support and medical expenses were not stayed by the appeal, and the trial court retained jurisdiction to enforce those payments. The trial court retained jurisdiction to enforce the provision requiring the father to pay his share of the child's uncovered medical expenses during the appeal. The trial court did not err by requiring both parties to split the costs for these expenses. By first requiring the father to videotape the administration of medicine as a condition of his visitation, and then by suspending the father's overnight visitation for failure to comply with that condition, the trial court improperly issued orders that were affected by a matter on appeal. The failure of the family court to appoint a GAL strictly for medical advisement did not constitute an abuse of discretion. The contempt sentence was improperly based on the void videotaping order.


OUTCOME: The order requiring the father to pay his share of medical expenses, the order requiring both parents to share non-medical and educational expenses, and the denial of the father's request for a medical GAL were affirmed. The suspension of the father's overnight visitation, and the finding of contempt for violating an order that was void for lack of jurisdiction were reversed.

 

103 of 275 DOCUMENTS


In the Interest of Johnny Lee W.


Opinion No. 26233


SUPREME COURT OF SOUTH CAROLINA


371 S.C. 217; 638 S.E.2d 682; 2006 S.C. LEXIS 387


October 5, 2006, Heard  
December 4, 2006, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from Sumter County. R. Wright Turbeville, Family Court Judge. George M. McFaddin, Jr., Family Court Judge.



DISPOSITION: VACATED IN PART AND REMANDED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant, a juvenile, pled guilty to three charges of disturbing schools and no contest to threatening a public official charge. The Sumter County Family Court (South Carolina) adjudicated appellant a delinquent, accepted his pleas and remanded him for evaluation. Later, appellant was sentenced. Appellant sought review.


OVERVIEW: The issue was whether appellant's guilty pleas were invalid as conditional guilty pleas. Appellant had challenged the constitutionality of S.C. Code Ann. § 16-17-420 (2005) by moving to quash. The trial court denied the motion. Appellant had stipulated to the fact that if the statute was constitutional, that his conduct violated it. The State conceded at oral argument that appellant's pleas were conditional. The appellate court ruled that a trial court was not permitted to accept a conditional plea. Accordingly, the trial court erred by accepting the conditional pleas to the charges of disturbing schools. Appellant did not appeal his guilty plea to malicious injury to property, therefore, that plea was unaffected by his entering conditional pleas to the disturbing school charges. Further, appellant's no contest plea to threatening a public official was not entered as a conditional plea; therefore, it was not vacated. Appellant's sentence was vacated because it was a combination sentence for all of the charges against him.


OUTCOME: The pleas to disturbing schools were vacated and the case was remanded.

 

104 of 275 DOCUMENTS


In re Amir X.S.


Opinion No. 26219


SUPREME COURT OF SOUTH CAROLINA


371 S.C. 380; 639 S.E.2d 144; 2006 S.C. LEXIS 351


September 20, 2006, Heard  
November 6, 2006, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Corrected December 14, 2006.
Rehearing denied by In the Interest of Amir X.S., 2007 S.C. LEXIS 27 (S.C., Jan. 18, 2007)
US Supreme Court certiorari denied by AMIR X. S. v. SOUTH CAROLINA, 127 S. Ct. 2981, 168 L. Ed. 2d 704, 2007 U.S. LEXIS 7748 (U.S., June 18, 2007)



DISPOSITION: AFFIRMED IN PART; VACATED IN PART.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant juvenile sought review of a decision of the Greenwood County, South Carolina, Family Court, upholding the constitutionality of S.C. Code Ann. § 16-17-420 (2003), under U.S. Const. amend. I, and, in accordance with appellee State's juvenile petition, adjudicating him delinquent for violating the statute, committing him to 90 days in the custody of the Department of Juvenile Justice, and imposing one year of probation.


OVERVIEW: Appellant argued that § 16-17-420 was unconstitutionally overly broad because it punished a substantial amount of protected free speech in relation to its plainly legitimate sweep. But the statute did not prohibit the kind of clearly expressive conduct historically subject to overbreadth adjudication in the school context. By its terms, the statute did not apply to protected speech, and it did not broadly regulate conduct. Instead, § 16-17-420 criminalized conduct that disturbed or interfered with schools, or was obnoxious. Such conduct was not protected by the First Amendment and so § 16-17-420 was not a substantial threat to protected speech requiring overbreadth adjudication. Appellant also argued that the statute was unconstitutionally vague. However, appellant did not have standing to make a facial vagueness challenge to the constitutionality of § 16-17-420. His disruptive classroom conduct fell within the most narrow application of § 16-17-420, and he had prior notice that the conduct was prohibited. Since appellant did not have standing to challenge § 16-17-420 on grounds of vagueness, the family court's ruling that § 16-17-420 was not unconstitutionally vague was vacated.


OUTCOME: The supreme court affirmed the family court's decision that § 16-17-420 was not unconstitutionally overbroad, but vacated the family court's decision that the statute was unconstitutionally vague.

Sloan v. S.C. Bd. of Physical Therapy Exam'rs


Opinion No. 26209


SUPREME COURT OF SOUTH CAROLINA


370 S.C. 452; 636 S.E.2d 598; 2006 S.C. LEXIS 302


June 7, 2006, Heard  
September 25, 2006, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1]
Rehearing denied by Sloan v. SCBPT (Doctor's Care), 2006 S.C. LEXIS 375 (S.C., Nov. 13, 2006)


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from Richland County. J. Ernest Kinard, Jr., Circuit Court Judge.



DISPOSITION: AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, the physicians, the physical therapists, and the professional associations, sued appellees, the South Carolina Board of Physical Therapy Examiners (Board), the South Carolina Chapter of the American Physical Therapy Association, and the South Carolina Attorney General, in the Richland County Circuit Court (South Carolina), for a declaratory judgment. The appellants challenged the grant of summary judgment to the appellees.


OVERVIEW: The Board endorsed an Attorney General opinion and announced that, pursuant to S.C. Code Ann. § 40-45-110(A)(1), it would investigate complaints against physical therapists employed by referring physicians. The physicians and the physical therapists claimed that a physician could lawfully employ a therapist and refer patients to that therapist, while the associations alleged violations of constitutional rights. On appeal, the court found that § 40-45-110(A)(1) prohibited a physical therapist from working as an employee of a physician when the physician referred patients to the therapist for services. Additionally, the Board's endorsement of the Attorney General's opinion was not improper rule making as it was not a regulation under S.C. Code Ann. § 1-23-10(4) (2005). Moreover, the interpretation of the statute did not improperly infringe upon physical therapists' right to practice medicine under S.C. Code Ann. § 40-47-40 (2001), violate physical therapists' equal protection rights under U.S. Const. amend. XIV, § 1 and S.C. Const. art. I, § 3, or violate physical therapists' substantive and procedural due process rights under U.S. Const. amend. XIV, § 1 and S.C. Const. art. I, § 3.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

 

106 of 275 DOCUMENTS


State v. Allen


Opinion No. 26204


SUPREME COURT OF SOUTH CAROLINA


370 S.C. 88; 634 S.E.2d 653; 2006 S.C. LEXIS 278


May 4, 2006, Heard  
August 21, 2006, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal From Greenwood County. J. Michael Baxley, Circuit Court Judge.



DISPOSITION: AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Greenwood County Circuit Court (South Carolina) revoked appellant's probationary sentence. Appellant claimed on appeal that the trial court erred in revoking his probation based on his association with a person who had a criminal record. He argued that the condition was so overly broad that it violated due process.


OVERVIEW: The court upheld the standard probation condition that a probationer not associate with a person with a criminal record. It was not so overly broad that it violated due process in all cases or in appellant's case. It implicitly required a finding that a probationer knew the person in question had a criminal record during the period of association and that the association was not simply an unknowing or incidental encounter. The State had to show that a probationer intentionally had knowing and substantial contact with a person who had a criminal record or that the probationer developed a significant or meaningful relationship with that person over a substantial period of time. The evidence here was that appellant associated with a second man, who had a criminal record, when both were involved in an illicit drug transaction that ended in a shooting. Appellant's car, which appellant drove to the hospital, arrived on the scene immediately after the shooting. The record showed a substantial and significant contact between appellant and the second man during a criminal transaction, not an unknowing or incidental encounter. Thus, there was sufficient evidence to support the revocation.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the revocation.

 

107 of 275 DOCUMENTS


Dreher v. Dreher


Opinion No. 26205


SUPREME COURT OF SOUTH CAROLINA


370 S.C. 75; 634 S.E.2d 646; 2006 S.C. LEXIS 282


June 20, 2006, Heard  
August 21, 2006, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal From Richland County. Amy W. McCulloch, Probate Court Judge.



DISPOSITION: REVERSED AND REMANDED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant widow sought an order granting her an elective share of a decedent's estate, declaring a trust invalid as illusory and thus includable in the calculation of her elective share, and removing respondent personal representative. The Richland County Probate Court (South Carolina) granted the request for an elective share but found the trust was not illusory pursuant to S.C. Code Ann. § 62-7-112 (Supp. 2004). The widow appealed.


OVERVIEW: The trial court construed S.C. Code Ann. § 62-7-112 (Supp. 2004) to prohibit a finding that a revocable inter vivos trust was illusory when a settlor retained substantial control of the trust, and concluded the statutory provision accordingly modified prior case law. The appellate court found that this construction was erroneous. The second sentence of S.C. Code Ann. § 62-7-112 clearly conveyed that no part of the statute, including the first sentence, should have been construed to prevent a finding that a revocable inter vivos trust was illusory for purposes of the elective share. Decedent retained substantial control because he retained such extensive powers over the assets of the trust that he had until his death the same rights in the assets after creation of the trust that he had before its creation. The trust at issue was illusory and thus invalid for elective share purposes, but remained valid for all other purposes.


OUTCOME: The judgment was reversed and the case was remanded.

 

108 of 275 DOCUMENTS


Burriss v. Anderson County Bd. of Educ.


Opinion No. 26175


SUPREME COURT OF SOUTH CAROLINA


369 S.C. 443; 633 S.E.2d 482; 2006 S.C. LEXIS 226


May 2, 2006, Heard  
June 26, 2006, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1]
Rehearing denied by Burriss v. Anderson County Bd. of Educ., 2006 S.C. LEXIS 262 (S.C., July 20, 2006)


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal From Anderson County. Alexander S. Macaulay, Circuit Court Judge.



DISPOSITION: AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, the school district and the boards of trustees chairmen, brought a declaratory judgment action against appellees, the county board of education and the state officials, in the Anderson County Circuit Court (South Carolina), to challenge a county's two-tiered school governance system. The circuit court granted the county board's summary judgment motion and dismissed the complaint. The school district and the chairmen appealed.


OVERVIEW: The school district and the chairmen contended that the county's two-tiered school governance system violated the Equal Protection Clause, under U.S. Const. amend. XIV, and the South Carolina Constitution's guarantees of no taxation without representation, under S.C. Const. Art. X, § 5, and uniform taxation within a jurisdiction, under S.C. Const. Art. X, § 6, because of the county board's review authority over individual school districts' budgets. On appeal, the court found that the State's establishment of the two-tiered school governance in the county did not result in vote dilution and therefore did not offend the Equal Protection Clause's guarantee of one person, one vote. Further, the mere fact that voters in the individual school districts directly elected fewer than all of the county board members who made decisions affecting those districts did not subject those voters to taxation without representation in violation S.C. Const. art. X, § 5. Finally, the special levies exception to the uniformity requirement in S.C. Const. art. X, § 6 applied to the school districts because the districts received special benefits from the school taxes.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

S.C. Pub. Interest Found. v. Judicial Merit Selection Comm'n


Opinion No. 26164


SUPREME COURT OF SOUTH CAROLINA


369 S.C. 139; 632 S.E.2d 277; 2006 S.C. LEXIS 205


June 7, 2006, Heard  
June 8, 2006, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from Richland County. J. Ernest Kinard, Jr., Circuit Court Judge.



DISPOSITION: AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, a public interest foundation and a citizen, sued appellees, the South Carolina Judicial Merit Selection Commission and its members, in the Richland County Circuit Court (South Carolina), for a declaratory judgment that the Commission did not adequately investigate whether a judicial candidate qualified for a judicial seat. The trial court found that the claim was moot and not proper for judicial review. The foundation appealed.


OVERVIEW: After a judicial candidate was found qualified by the Commission for a judicial circuit seat and was subsequently elected to the seat by the South Carolina General Assembly, the foundation and the citizen challenged the Commission's determination. In particular, they alleged that the Commission was unreasonable in finding that the candidate was a resident of the judicial circuit to which he was elected. The foundation and the citizen argued that the trial court erred in determining that the issue was nonjusticiable. The state supreme court found that S.C. Const. art. V, § 27, in unequivocal terms, vested the power to determine the qualifications for judicial candidates in the South Carolina General Assembly. In addition, pursuant to the Constitution, the General Assembly had established criteria in S.C. Code Ann. §§ 2-19-35 and 2-19-20(D) (2005) for the Commission to consider when making the determination of whether a judicial candidate was qualified. Thus, the issue presented to the court was nonjusticiable. However, the foundation and the citizen could properly challenge the residency of the judicial candidate as provided for in S.C. Code Ann. § 7-5-230 (Supp. 2005).


OUTCOME: The decision was affirmed.

Palmetto Princess, LLC v. Town of Edisto Beach


Opinion No. 26157


SUPREME COURT OF SOUTH CAROLINA


369 S.C. 50; 631 S.E.2d 76; 2006 S.C. LEXIS 184


January 4, 2006, Heard  
May 30, 2006, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal From Colleton County. Howard P. King, Circuit Court Judge.

CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant town challenged a summary judgment of the Colleton County Court (South Carolina), in appellee cruise ship operator's action for a declaratory judgment. OVERVIEW: The cruise ship operator applied for a license to offer gambling cruises from the town. The court observed that it had previously held that a cruise such as that offered by the cruise ship operator was a legal activity and that the Gambling Cruise Prohibition Act, S.C. Code Ann. § 3-11-200(A), specifically allowed the type of activity offered by the cruise ship operator.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the county court.

Layman v. State


Opinion No. 26146


SUPREME COURT OF SOUTH CAROLINA


368 S.C. 631; 630 S.E.2d 265; 2006 S.C. LEXIS 154


December 1, 2005, Heard  
May 4, 2006, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1]
Rehearing denied by Layman v. State, 2006 S.C. LEXIS 198 (S.C., June 1, 2006)
Appeal after remand at, Costs and fees proceeding at Layman v. State, 376 S.C. 434, 658 S.E.2d 320, 2008 S.C. LEXIS 21 (S.C., 2008)
Appeal after remand at, Decision reached on appeal by, Sub nomine at Ahrens v. State, 2011 S.C. LEXIS 159 (S.C., May 2, 2011)


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from Richland County. John L. Breeden, Circuit Court Judge.



DISPOSITION: DECLARATORY JUDGMENT ISSUED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant retiree participants brought an action against appellees, the State of South Carolina and the South Carolina Retirement System, in the Richland County Circuit Court (South Carolina), for breach of contract, estoppel, and violations of the Takings and Due Process Clauses of the South Carolina and Federal Constitutions. The participants requested for the case to be heard in the Supreme Court of South Carolina's original jurisdiction.


OVERVIEW: The participants were either South Carolina Teacher and Employee Retention Incentive Program (TERI) participants or working retiree participants who retired before July 1, 2005 under the old TERI program, S.C. Code Ann. § 9-1-2210 (Supp. 2004), or the old working retiree statute, S.C. Code Ann. § 9-1-1790 (Supp. 2004). They claimed that the requirement in the South Carolina State Retirement System Preservation and Investment Reform Act, 2005 S.C. Acts 1697, that they contribute a percentage of their annual salary to the retirement program was a breach of contract. They also asserted that the State was estopped from requiring further contributions due to their reliance on the terms of the former programs. The court found that the South Carolina Legislature bound itself to the terms in the old TERI statute. Thus, the State could not unilaterally alter its agreement with the old TERI program participants by forcing them to make contributions. Further, the old working retiree statute did not create a binding contract between the State and the working retirees prohibiting the State from altering the statute exempting them from further contributions to the retirement system.


OUTCOME: The court declared that the State unlawfully breached its contract with the participants in the old TERI program. In addition, the court remanded the working retiree participants' breach of contract claim to the trial court for a case by case factual determination of whether any actions of the State with regard to individual working retirees constituted a breach of contract.

Mims Amusement Co. v. S.C. Law Enforcement Div.


Opinion No. 26046


SUPREME COURT OF SOUTH CAROLINA


366 S.C. 141; 621 S.E.2d 344; 2005 S.C. LEXIS 283


June 16, 2005, Heard  
October 3, 2005, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal From Berkeley County. Daniel F. Pieper, Circuit Court Judge.



DISPOSITION: REVERSED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant South Carolina Law Enforcement Division (SLED) presented a video gaming machine to a magistrate under S.C. Code Ann. § 12-21-2712 (2000). The magistrate ordered the machine destroyed under S.C. Code Ann. § 12-21-2710 (2000). Appellee owner appealed. The Berkeley County Circuit Court (South Carolina) ruled that the magistrate had to make a factual determination on the legality of the machine, and remanded the case. SLED appealed.


OVERVIEW: Law enforcement officials seized a video gaming machine, which belonged to the owner, from a sports bar and grill. The owner contended that he had a right to a jury trial, under the South Carolina Constitution, in the civil forfeiture proceeding to determine whether the machine was an illegal gambling device. The state supreme court found that the owner of the video game machine seized by law enforcement authorities did not have a constitutional right, pursuant to S.C. Const. art. I, § 14, to a jury trial in the civil forfeiture proceeding to determine whether the machine was an illegal gambling device because the device, at the moment of seizure, was deemed an item of contraband per se. The owner's constitutional right to due process of law was, therefore, satisfied by the post-seizure hearing before the magistrate to determine the legality of the machine, with the right to appeal the ruling to higher courts.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the circuit court was reversed.

Webb v. CSX Transp., Inc.


Opinion No. 26004


SUPREME COURT OF SOUTH CAROLINA


364 S.C. 639; 615 S.E.2d 440; 2005 S.C. LEXIS 181


December 1, 2004, Heard  
June 20, 2005, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1]
Rehearing denied by Webb v. CSX Transp., 2005 S.C. LEXIS 226 (S.C., July 21, 2005)


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal From Anderson County. J. Cordell Maddox, Jr., Circuit Court Judge.



DISPOSITION: AFFIRMED IN PART; REVERSED IN PART.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff personal representative filed a wrongful death action against defendant railroad, alleging that it was negligent and that it violated S.C. Code Ann. § 58-17-3420 (1976) by not replacing a burned bridge. A jury awarded plaintiff actual and punitive damages. Plaintiff appealed the Anderson County Circuit Court's (South Carolina) failure to allow damages for the railroad's violation of § 58-17-3420; the railroad cross-appealed.


OVERVIEW: The decedent was riding in a car that was struck by a train as the car crossed the tracks. Arsonists' destruction of a bridge over the tracks obliged motorists to use the crossing. The trial judge, sitting in equity and reviewing plaintiff 's § 58-17-3420 claim, found that the railroad's failure to replace the bridge was not the proximate cause of the accident. The appellate court agreed, finding there was no "continuous operation" linking the destroyed bridge and the accident. The prejudicial admission before the jury of evidence relevant only to the bridge replacement issue, and plaintiff's use of this in closing, entitled the railroad to a new trial. Evidence that the railroad clear cut vegetation near the crossing was inadmissible as a subsequent remedial measure (S.C. R. Evid. 407). In view of evidence that the engineer failed to sound his horn for the statutorily prescribed distance, and that vegetation at the crossing was so overgrown as to obstruct motorists' view of oncoming trains, the issue of punitive damages was properly submitted to the jury. But evidence of acts unrelated to crossing safety in South Carolina was not constitutionally permissible under Campbell.


OUTCOME: The order denying plaintiff relief on the statutory claim was affirmed. The jury verdicts were reversed, and the matter was remanded for a new trial.

City of N. Charleston v. County of Charleston


Opinion No. 25966


SUPREME COURT OF SOUTH CAROLINA


363 S.C. 527; 611 S.E.2d 920; 2005 S.C. LEXIS 113


March 1, 2005, Heard  
April 11, 2005, Filed



DISPOSITION:  [***1] JUDGMENT FOR PLAINTIFFS.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff city, and others, brought an original action against defendant county, and others, challenging the constitutionality of a tax ordinance enacted by the county pursuant to S.C. Code Ann. § 12-37-223A (Supp. 2004).


OVERVIEW: An earlier county ordinance had been invalidated because it was inconsistent with S.C. Code Ann. § 12-37-223A (Supp. 2004). The county then adopted its current ordinance tracking the language of S.C. Code Ann. § 12-37-223A. The appellate court found that S.C. Const. art. X, § 3 mandated statewide uniformity in property tax exemptions enacted by the general assembly. Where the South Carolina Constitution required statewide uniformity, a local option law was not valid. S.C. Code Ann. § 12-37-223A (Supp. 2004) was patently invalid since it enacted an exemption that was not uniform throughout the state. The court declared S.C. Code Ann. § 12-37-223A unconstitutional and held that the county's ordinance was therefore invalid.


OUTCOME: Judgment was entered for the city.

Evans v. State


Opinion No. 25963


SUPREME COURT OF SOUTH CAROLINA


363 S.C. 495; 611 S.E.2d 510; 2005 S.C. LEXIS 108


April 21, 2004, Submitted  
April 4, 2005, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal From York County. Lee S. Alford, Circuit Court Judge. John C. Hayes, III, Trial Judge.
State v. Evans, 322 S.C. 78, 470 S.E.2d 97, 1996 S.C. LEXIS 45 (S.C., 1996)



DISPOSITION: AFFIRMED IN PART; REVERSED IN PART.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was convicted in his absence in the York County Circuit Court (South Carolina) of trafficking in cocaine. The trial court sentenced defendant to 25 years in prison and a fine. Defendant's petition for postconviction relief was denied. Defendant filed a petition for a writ of certiorari.


OVERVIEW: Defendant asserted that he had a right to obtain documents pertaining to the impanelment of the state grand jury which indicted him to determine whether the state grand jury which indicted defendant was properly impaneled pursuant to S.C. Code Ann. § 14-7-1630 (Supp. 2003). On appeal, the court found that impanelment documents, including the State of South Carolina's petition, supporting materials, and the impaneling judge's order, could be released to defendant prior to trial upon timely request or to defendant in a postconviction relief proceeding. The State could redact information not related to a particular defendant's case or information concerning other persons or matters under consideration by the state grand jury. Release of the documents could be appropriate because (1) S.C. Code Ann. § 14-7-1770 (Supp. 2003) was not a complete prohibition on the release of the information; (2) release of the documents usually was not prohibited by secrecy provisions or other concerns following the issuance of a true bill of indictment; and (3) defendant had the right to review the documents to determine whether to timely challenge the legality of the state grand jury which indicted him.


OUTCOME: The postconviction relief judge's decision prohibiting the release of the impanelment documents to defendant was reversed. The denial of defendant's postconviction relief application was affirmed.

City of Charleston v. Masi


Opinion No. 25941


SUPREME COURT OF SOUTH CAROLINA


362 S.C. 505; 609 S.E.2d 301; 2005 S.C. LEXIS 42


January 4, 2005, Heard  
February 14, 2005, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal From Charleston County. Thomas L. Hughston, Jr., Circuit Court Judge.
Kizer v. Clark, 360 S.C. 86, 600 S.E.2d 529, 2004 S.C. LEXIS 179 (S.C., 2004)



DISPOSITION: AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff town, and others, sued defendant election district, and others, seeking injunctive and declaratory relief as to who, after the town's incorporation, was entitled to vote in district elections and serve as commissioners. The Charleston County Circuit Court (South Carolina) declined to decide the issues raised and denied the election district's request for attorney's fees. The town and the election district appealed.


OVERVIEW: The complaint alleged that as a function of the incorporation of the town, which overlapped certain areas in the election district, the territorial boundaries of the election district were reduced such that the town residents should have been prohibited from participating in district elections. After the filing of the appeal, in a separate case, the appellate court found the town to be a nullity because it had been created by unconstitutional special legislation. In this case, the appellate court found that the earlier opinion nullifying the town rendered this case nonjusticiable. While the questions involved could have arisen again, the questions did not "usually become moot" before they could have been reviewed. Further, the issues involved did not appear to present a "recurring dilemma" which the appellate court needed to address to clarify the law. The case did not fit within the public importance exception to mootness because there was no imperative or manifest urgency in obtaining a decision on whether the town residents can vote in the district's election when the town did not exist.


OUTCOME: The case was dismissed as moot.

 

117 of 275 DOCUMENTS


Sloan v. Wilkins


Opinion No. 25933


SUPREME COURT OF SOUTH CAROLINA


362 S.C. 430; 608 S.E.2d 579; 2005 S.C. LEXIS 28


December 2, 2004, Heard  
January 28, 2005, Filed



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Court finds Act No. 187 violates one subject requirement of Article III, § 17 of the constitution. Certain provisions which are germane to Act 187, as set forth, are within one subject requirements of Article III and are upheld. Offending provisions of Act 187 are stricken.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner citizen, and others, brought an original action against, among others, respondents, a speaker and a senate president, seeking a declaration that 2004 S.C. Acts 187 violated S.C. Const. art. III, § 17.


OVERVIEW: Initially, the speaker and the senate president asserted that the citizen was without standing to proceed with the action. The court disagreed. Standing was conferred upon a party when an issue was of such public importance as to require its resolution for future guidance. In light of the great public importance, the court found that the citizen had standing to maintain the action. The myriad provisions comprising 2004 S.C. Acts 187 simply did not comprise one subject. On the contrary, 2004 S.C. Acts 187 was teeming with subjects, from life sciences provisions to the establishment of a culinary arts institute. 2004 S.C. Acts 187 violated the one subject requirement of S.C. Const. art. III, § 17. However, the offending portions of 2004 S.C. Acts 187 were severable. The court analyzed and struck the offending portions of 2004 S.C. Acts 187.


OUTCOME: The court struck certain offending portions of the act.

 

118 of 275 DOCUMENTS


State v. Crisp


Opinion No. 25928


SUPREME COURT OF SOUTH CAROLINA


362 S.C. 412; 608 S.E.2d 429; 2005 S.C. LEXIS 18


November 30, 2004, Heard  
January 24, 2005, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal From Anderson County. John W. Kittredge, Circuit Court Judge.



DISPOSITION: REVERSED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant pled guilty to murder and possession of a firearm or knife during the commission of a violent crime. After a non-jury sentencing hearing, the Anderson County Circuit Court (South Carolina) sentenced him death. He appealed.


OVERVIEW: The State asserted defendant's prior murder conviction and physical torture as statutory grounds for the death penalty. Questioning defendant about his waiver of his right to a jury trial, the trial court told him jurors would sometimes lie about their willingness to impose the death penalty so as to get on the jury and ensure that it would not be imposed. The supreme court held that by making these comments, the trial court improperly injected its personal opinion into defendant's decision to waive a jury trial. The error was fundamental and prejudicial and required reversal of defendant's guilty plea. Ring did not involve jury-trial waivers and was not implicated when a defendant pled guilty. S.C. Code Ann. § 16-3-20(B) was not unconstitutional in light of Ring, and a capital defendant could be sentenced by a judge after knowingly and voluntarily waiving his right to a jury trial. Aggravating circumstances listed in § 16-3-20(C)(a) (2003) were not elements of murder and did not have to be alleged in the indictment. As the State identified the aggravating circumstances and evidence it intended to use, the trial court had subject matter jurisdiction to sentence defendant to death.


OUTCOME: Defendant's guilty plea was reversed and the case was remanded for further proceedings.

 

119 of 275 DOCUMENTS


State v. Sullivan


Opinion No. 25923


SUPREME COURT OF SOUTH CAROLINA


362 S.C. 373; 608 S.E.2d 422; 2005 S.C. LEXIS 3


December 2, 2004, Heard  
January 10, 2005, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1]
Rehearing denied by State v. Sullivan, 2005 S.C. LEXIS 59 (S.C., Feb. 17, 2005)
Related proceeding at Sullivan v. County of Spartanburg, 2005 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 45649 (D.S.C., May 10, 2005)
Related proceeding at Sullivan v. Wells, 2010 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 111290 (D.S.C., May 26, 2010)


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal From Spartanburg County John C. Few, Circuit Court Judge.



DISPOSITION: AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed a judgment by the Spartanburg County Circuit Court (South Carolina) that convicted him of criminal domestic violence of a high and aggravated nature under S.C. Code Ann. § 16-25-65 (2003); defendant claimed that the phrase "purposeful infliction of shame and disgrace" in the statute was unconstitutionally vague.


OVERVIEW: Prior to voir dire and before sentencing, defendant moved to strike and for a new trial, claiming that § 16-25-65 was unconstitutionally vague. The state supreme court held that "shame" and "disgrace" were common terms, so that a person of common intelligence need not guess at the meanings. Likewise, a person of common intelligence could understand what "purposeful infliction" meant. Consequently, the phrase "purposeful infliction of shame and disgrace" did not offend due process under either U.S. Const. amend. XIV, § 1 or S.C. Const. art. I, § 3.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

State v. Wood


Opinion No. 25907


SUPREME COURT OF SOUTH CAROLINA


362 S.C. 135; 607 S.E.2d 57; 2004 S.C. LEXIS 287


October 5, 2004, Heard  
December 6, 2004, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1]
Related proceeding at State v. Wood, 362 S.C. 520, 608 S.E.2d 435, 2004 S.C. App. LEXIS 347 (S.C. Ct. App., 2004)
Rehearing denied by State v. Wood, 2005 S.C. LEXIS 33 (S.C., Jan. 20, 2005)
Related proceeding at State v. McCall, 364 S.C. 205, 612 S.E.2d 453, 2005 S.C. App. LEXIS 73 (S.C. Ct. App., 2005)
US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Wood v. South Carolina, 545 U.S. 1132, 125 S. Ct. 2942, 162 L. Ed. 2d 873, 2005 U.S. LEXIS 4929 (2005)
Habeas corpus proceeding at, Stay granted by Wood v. Byars, 2012 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 177186 (D.S.C., Dec. 14, 2012)


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal From Greenville County John W. Kittredge, Circuit Court Judge.



DISPOSITION: AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed from a judgment of the Greenville County Circuit Court (South Carolina), which convicted defendant of murder in violation of S.C. Code Ann. § 16-3-10 (1976) and sentenced defendant to death.


OVERVIEW: After defendant failed to stop his moped for a police officer who had activated his lights and siren, the officer blocked the moped with the police vehicle. Witnesses saw defendant fire gunshots in the police officer's driver's side window, flee on the moped, and then get into another vehicle driven by someone else. A jury convicted defendant of murder and possession of a weapon during the commission of a violent crime and sentenced him to death. On appeal, the court held that the trial court had a reasonable basis for discharging a black juror who was initially adamant that she could not vote for the death penalty in any situation. Although the potential juror later said that she could impose the death penalty, she admitted that she changed her mind due to defense counsel's appeal to her to help make the jury racially balanced. Defendant was not entitled to a jury charge on voluntary manslaughter because the officer's conduct in making a lawful stop did not constitute adequate provocation. Defendant's argument that the trial court lacked subject matter jurisdiction was rejected because aggravating circumstances did not have to alleged in the indictment for murder.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's judgment.

 

121 of 275 DOCUMENTS


Glover v. County of Charleston


Opinion No. 25904


SUPREME COURT OF SOUTH CAROLINA


361 S.C. 634; 606 S.E.2d 773; 2004 S.C. LEXIS 289


October 7, 2004, Heard  
December 6, 2004, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal From Charleston County. A. Victor Rawl, Circuit Court Judge.



DISPOSITION: AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In an action that appellant property owners brought against respondents, a county and a planning commission, to challenge a Unified Development Ordinance (UDO), the property owners appealed from a summary judgment that was entered in favor of respondents by the Charleston County Circuit Court (South Carolina). The property owners asserted claims for due process and equal protection violations and for an unlawful taking without compensation.


OVERVIEW: Before the UDO was adopted, the county placed notices in the local newspaper, sent notices to property owners, and held numerous public meetings. The property owners alleged that they were denied due process because conspicuous notice was not posted adjacent to the property affected. On appeal, the court held that such conspicuous notice was not required by S.C. Code Ann. § 6-29-760 (2004) because the UDO did not involve rezoning. Because the adoption of the UDO was merely the enactment of a zoning ordinance, notice in the newspaper was sufficient. The court further determined that the property owners' actual notice of the proposed UDO was sufficient notice. Respondents were entitled to summary judgment on the property owners' takings claim because the owners did not demonstrate that they were, even temporarily, denied all economically viable use of their land. The complaint merely alleged that the owners could not sell their property for fair market value and that the UDO severely restricted subdivision. By not asserting before the circuit county that the UDO resulted in the division of citizens into unlawful racial groups, the owners failed to preserve this argument for review.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the circuit court's judgment.

State v. Downs


Opinion No. 25884


SUPREME COURT OF SOUTH CAROLINA


361 S.C. 141; 604 S.E.2d 377; 2004 S.C. LEXIS 246


September 21, 2004, Heard  
October 25, 2004, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Rehearing denied by State v. Downs, 2004 S.C. LEXIS 305 (S.C., Dec. 14, 2004)
Motion denied by Downs v. South Carolina, 544 U.S. 972, 125 S. Ct. 1829, 161 L. Ed. 2d 721, 2005 U.S. LEXIS 3317 (2005)
Subsequent appeal at State v. Downs, 2006 S.C. LEXIS 186 (S.C., May 30, 2006)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal From Aiken County. L. Casey Manning, Circuit Court Judge.



DISPOSITION: AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant pled guilty to murder, kidnapping, and first-degree criminal sexual conduct with a minor. The Aiken County Circuit Court (South Carolina) sentenced him to death. Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: Defendant kidnapped a six-year-old boy, raped him, and strangled him to death. Upon the court's inquiry he said he understood that by pleading guilty he waived his right to a jury trial on both guilt and sentencing. He presented evidence that he was mentally ill, which the State countered; the trial court ruled he was not "guilty but mentally ill." He claimed his plea was conditional because he pled guilty while deferring the decision whether to present evidence of mental illness. The appellate court disagreed, noting that he never attempted to reserve the right to later deny his guilt, but only to present evidence that he committed the crime while mentally ill. That his death sentence was imposed by a judge rather than a jury did not violate Ring because Ring was not implicated when a defendant pled guilty. As S.C. Code Ann. § 17-19-30 (2003) did not require the indictment to list aggravating circumstances, the trial court had subject matter jurisdiction. The sentence was not the result of passion or other arbitrary factors. The evidence supported the findings of aggravation; and in relation to sentences in similar cases, it was neither excessive nor disproportionate to the crime.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

 

123 of 275 DOCUMENTS


State v. Mills


Opinion No. 25864


SUPREME COURT OF SOUTH CAROLINA


360 S.C. 621; 602 S.E.2d 750; 2004 S.C. LEXIS 213


April 21, 2004, Heard  
September 7, 2004, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from Spartanburg County Gary E. Clary, Circuit Court Judge.



DISPOSITION: AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant pleaded guilty to distribution of crack cocaine, second offense. He was sentenced to six months of imprisonment. Following his release into a community supervision program (CSP), defendant's supervising agent swore out a warrant for CSP violations. The Spartanburg County Circuit Court (South Carolina) revoked defendant's CSP and sentenced him to five months and seven days of imprisonment. Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: Defendant argued that § 24-21-560(D) (Supp. 2003) limited his sentence for revocation to the remaining time left on his original sentence for the substantive crime. The appellate court held that a plain reading of the statute provided that for a successive revocation, the prisoner might be sentenced "as provided in subsection (C)" i.e., for up to one year, with a limitation that the total time imposed "for successive revocations" i.e., all revocations, could not exceed the length of time of the prisoner's original sentence. Section 24-21-560(D) did not provide that the sentence for any successive revocation was limited to the amount of time remaining on the prisoner's original sentence, nor did the statute inevitably result in the "doubling" of a prisoner's sentence. Further, the only issue before the appellate court was the construction of the statute and not the wisdom of the CSP statutory scheme. Since defendant served three weeks on his prior revocation and his time for all revocations could not exceed six months, the trial judge properly sentenced defendant to five months and seven days of imprisonment.


OUTCOME: The order was affirmed.

Brackenbrook N. Charleston, LP v. County of Charleston


Opinion No. 25855


SUPREME COURT OF SOUTH CAROLINA


360 S.C. 390; 602 S.E.2d 39; 2004 S.C. LEXIS 194


April 20, 2004, Heard  
August 16, 2004, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Corrected August 24, 2004.
Companion case at Hoefer Family L.P. v. County of Charleston, 360 S.C. 403, 602 S.E.2d 47, 2004 S.C. LEXIS 195 (S.C., 2004)
Rehearing denied by Brackenbrook North Charleston, L.P. v. County of Charleston, 2004 S.C. LEXIS 234 (S.C., Sept. 27, 2004)
Appeal after remand at Brackenbrook N. Charleston, LP v. County of Charleston, 2005 S.C. LEXIS 356 (S.C., Nov. 28, 2005)


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal From Charleston County A. Victor Rawl, Circuit Court Judge.
Riverwoods, LLC v. County of Charleston, 349 S.C. 378, 563 S.E.2d 651, 2002 S.C. LEXIS 81 (S.C., 2002)



DISPOSITION: Reversed and remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, a county, its treasurer, its auditor, and its assessor, appealed from an order entered by the Charleston County Circuit Court (South Carolina) certifying a class of taxpayers and ordering refunds for property taxes paid under Charleston, S.C., Ordinance 1163, which had been declared invalid. Respondent taxpayers cross-appealed.


OVERVIEW: The county argued that the suit should have been dismissed, as the taxpayers failed to exhaust their administrative remedies under the South Carolina Revenue Procedures Act. The appellate court held that the taxpayers failed to exhaust their administrative remedies and that the suit should have been dismissed without prejudice. The taxpayers did not dispute any component of their property tax assessments, but argued that their tax bills were inflated as the consequence of the unlawful ordinance. However, the appellate court instructed the county to provide eligible taxpayers within the class with notice of their right to file an administrative refund action within 120 days. The appellate court strongly disapproved of the county's failure to refund the illegally collected taxes promptly and of its attempts to block the refund of those taxes. Further, the county's suggestion that a writ of mandamus be issued requiring the county to rebill all county taxpayers who benefited from the unlawful ordinance was described as audacious.


OUTCOME: The trial court's orders were reversed. The case was remanded to the trial court with instructions to dismiss the suit without prejudice. The county was instructed to give written notice to the eligible taxpayers within the class of their right to seek an administrative refund, and of the date by which the refund request had to be initiated. Finally, the taxpayers were awarded their appellate costs and a reasonable attorney's fees.

White v. J.M. Brown Amusement Co.


Opinion No. 25849


SUPREME COURT OF SOUTH CAROLINA


360 S.C. 366; 601 S.E.2d 342; 2004 S.C. LEXIS 188


June 22, 2004, Heard  
August 9, 2004, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from Anderson County. James W. Johnson, Jr., Circuit Court Judge.



DISPOSITION: Reversed and remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioners, a store and its owner (owner), obtained a writ of certiorari for review of the South Carolina Court of Appeals' reversal of the entry of summary judgment for the owner as to respondent video poker machine provider's counterclaims for breach of contract and breach of contract accompanied by a fraudulent act in the owner's suit seeking to have the parties' contract declared unenforceable.


OVERVIEW: The owner argued that the contract was unenforceable as a local referendum banned the video machines that formed the subject matter of the contract. The state's highest court noted that the parties were performing under the contract until performance became legally impossible because the subject matter of the contract became illegal and, as mandated by the local option law, the South Carolina Department of Revenue revoked the licenses required to legally possess and use the machines. Consequently, the parties' duties to perform under the contract were discharged and the parties were free to enter a new contract involving legal machines or seek other contractual relationships. The contract was not revived when the local option provision of the South Carolina Video Game Machines Act, former S.C. Code Ann. §§ 12-21-2806 and -2808, was declared unconstitutional. The case presented a special circumstance in which the validity of transactions that occurred before the statute was declared unconstitutional had to be upheld. Neither party breached the contract. Thus, neither party was entitled to damages.


OUTCOME: The intermediate court's decision was reversed. The case was remanded for the entry of summary judgment in favor of the owner on the provider's breach of contract counterclaims.

 

126 of 275 DOCUMENTS


Kizer v. Clark


Opinion No. 25846


SUPREME COURT OF SOUTH CAROLINA


360 S.C. 86; 600 S.E.2d 529; 2004 S.C. LEXIS 179


March 2, 2004, Heard  
July 26, 2004, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Corrected July 28, 2004.
Rehearing denied by Kizer v. Clark, 2004 S.C. LEXIS 250 (S.C., Oct. 18, 2004)
Related proceeding at City of Charleston v. Masi, 2005 S.C. LEXIS 42 (S.C., Feb. 14, 2005)


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from Charleston County. Thomas L. Hughston, Jr., Circuit Court Judge.



DISPOSITION: AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Respondent city brought an action challenging the incorporation of appellant town. The Charleston County, South Carolina, trial court found that S.C. Code Ann. § 5-1-30(A)(4) (2003), by which the town established the contiguity necessary for incorporation, was unconstitutional as special legislation. The town appealed.


OVERVIEW: Section 5-1-30(A)(4) was enacted in response to lobbying by the town after the supreme court had ruled that marshlands and creeks had previously been annexed by other cities and therefore could not be used to establish the town's contiguity. Section 5-1-30(A)(4) provided that contiguity was not destroyed by an intervening marshland whether or not the marshland had been previously incorporated or annexed by another municipality. By establishing contiguity using marshland and waterways that had already been annexed by the city, the town was able to amass a population of 15,000, allowing it to bypass a request for annexation before incorporating. The supreme court held that the classification created by § 5-1-30(A)(4) was arbitrary because there was no rational reason to allow only certain geographic areas to use territory belonging to a neighboring municipality to enable incorporation. Section § 5-1-30(A)(4) created an unconstitutional diversity in municipal incorporation laws, which were constitutionally required to be uniform, by allowing only certain areas to incorporate using territory belonging to another municipality, and was therefore unconstitutional special legislation.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

Bergstrom v. Palmetto Health Alliance


Opinion No. 25807


SUPREME COURT OF SOUTH CAROLINA


358 S.C. 388; 596 S.E.2d 42; 2004 S.C. LEXIS 94


January 22, 2004, Heard  
April 19, 2004, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1]
Rehearing denied by Bergstrom v. Palmetto Health, 2004 S.C. LEXIS 140 (S.C., May 25, 2004)


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal From Richland County. Costa M. Pleicones, Circuit Court Judge. Alison R. Lee, Circuit Court Judge.
Bergstrom v. Palmetto Health Alliance, 352 S.C. 221, 573 S.E.2d 805, 2002 S.C. App. LEXIS 151 (S.C. Ct. App., 2002)



DISPOSITION: VACATED IN PART; AFFIRMED IN PART.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner daughter sought review of a decision of the Court of Appeals (South Carolina), which affirmed a circuit court's orders dismissing her intentional infliction of emotional distress claim against respondent charitable hospital and granting the hospital's motion for a directed verdict on her negligence claim. The daughters claims were based on the hospital's alleged failure to follow its adoption policies.


OVERVIEW: The mother intended to place the daughter for adoption, but, in violation of hospital policy, the mother testified that she had no recollection of signing any documents concerning the adoption and that she was prevented from seeing the daughter after the birth. The daughter was reunited with her mother after her putative adopted parents were taken into custody for taking nude photos of her when she was eleven. The daughter subsequently brought the action against the hospital. Affirming the result, the court held that (1) the daughter's cause of action accrued at her birth in 1979 when the hospital allegedly failed to follow its adoption policies; (2) the court's declaration in 1992 that S.C. Code Ann. § 44-7-50 was unconstitutional meant the statute was unconstitutional from the date of its enactment in 1977 and thus void ab initio; (3) based on the law in effect in 1979, the daughter was required to prove the hospital's acts were reckless, not simply negligent, which she failed to do; (4) the failure to prove recklessness also defeated the intentional infliction of emotional distress claim; and (5) it was unnecessary to address the issues of duty or proximate cause.


OUTCOME: The court vacated the appellate court's discussion on the issues of duty and proximate cause, but it affirmed the result of dismissal.

Sunset Cay, LLC v. City of Folly Beach


Opinion No. 25774


SUPREME COURT OF SOUTH CAROLINA


357 S.C. 414; 593 S.E.2d 462; 2004 S.C. LEXIS 29


December 4, 2003, Heard  
January 27, 2004, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1]
Rehearing denied by Sunset Cay v. City of Folly Beach, 2004 S.C. LEXIS 72 (S.C., Mar. 17, 2004)


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal From Charleston County. A. Victor Rawl, Circuit Court Judge.



DISPOSITION: REVERSED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant developer filed a declaratory judgment action against respondent city in the Charleston County Circuit Court (South Carolina), seeking a declaration that ordinances were contrary to state law and unconstitutional, and an order enjoining enforcement of the ordinances. The circuit court dismissed the case. The circuit court then denied the developer's S.C. R. Civ. P. 59(e) motion. The developer appealed.


OVERVIEW: The city built a sewer system serving the central commercial district and adopted ordinances, which governed the operation and expansion of the sewer system. The developer proposed to build eight residential duplexes on a property, but needed an expansion of the sewer service to build the duplexes. The city denied the expansion of the sewer system to the developer's property. The developer challenged the validity of an ordinance on statutory and constitutional grounds. Therefore, the developer was not entitled to a declaratory judgment, or to an injunction.


OUTCOME: The judgment was reversed.

Stanley v. Kirkpatrick


Opinion No. 25769


SUPREME COURT OF SOUTH CAROLINA


357 S.C. 169; 592 S.E.2d 296; 2004 S.C. LEXIS 15


October 21, 2003, Heard  
January 12, 2004, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1]
Rehearing denied by Stanley v. Kirkpatrick, 2004 S.C. LEXIS 51 (S.C., Feb. 18, 2004)


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal From Richland County. James R. Barber, Circuit Court Judge.

CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The South Carolina Court of Appeals reversed a trial court's judgment denying respondent dog owner's motion to amend her complaint to add state law tort claims after she filed a 42 U.S.C.S. § 1983 claim following petitioner city's euthanization of her dogs. It also reversed the trial court's order granting the city's summary judgment motion on her § 1983 action against it. The state supreme court granted the city's petition for certiorari.


OVERVIEW: The dog owner bred dogs. A police officer who had issued some citations to her received another complaint about her dogs. He visited her house and saw 15 dogs. He left, but later returned. He saw five dogs running around the street and captured them. He later told the dog owner that he had taken the captured animals to the city's animal shelter. The city and the dog owner disputed whether she was informed she only had five days to claim the dogs; in any event, when they went unclaimed, they were euthanized. The dog owner filed a claim pursuant to § 1983 against the city. The city sought summary judgment. The dog owner filed a motion to amend to add state law claims. The state supreme court granted certiorari and found that the dog owner should have been allowed to amend since her claims arose out of the timely-filed § 1983 action. However, it also found she had not shown she had a viable § 1983 claim.


OUTCOME: The appellate court's reversal of the trial court's denial of the dog owner's motion to amend her complaint was affirmed. However, the appellate court's reversal of trial court's decision to grant the city's motion for summary judgment as to the dog owner's § 1983 claim was reversed.

State v. Curtis


Opinion No. 25762


SUPREME COURT OF SOUTH CAROLINA


356 S.C. 622; 591 S.E.2d 600; 2004 S.C. LEXIS 1


October 21, 2003, Heard  
January 5, 2004, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1]
Rehearing denied by State v. Curtis, 2004 S.C. LEXIS 38 (S.C., Feb. 4, 2004)


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal From Greenville County. John C. Few, Circuit Court Judge.
Curtis v. State, 345 S.C. 557, 549 S.E.2d 591, 2001 S.C. LEXIS 133 (S.C., 2001)



DISPOSITION: AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: After the Greenville County Circuit Court (South Carolina) denied defendant's motion for a directed verdict, he was convicted of two counts of the sale of urine with the intent to defraud a drug or alcohol test, in violation of S.C. Code Ann. § 16-13-470 (2003). He appealed, arguing, inter alia, that § 16-13-470 was unconstitutionally vague, the indictment was defective, and trial court erred in denying his directed verdict motion.


OVERVIEW: Defendant's business sold urine substitution kits that contained urine, a pouch, a tube, a hand-warmer device, duct tape, and instructions for use. The instructions advised how to use the heat pack to maintain proper temperature and how to affix the kit to the body for maximum concealment. The supreme court held that § 16-13-470 was not unconstitutionally vague for failing to define the term "drug test," as that term had a sufficiently common meaning to put one on notice of the conduct proscribed. Section 16-13-470 prohibited the sale with the intent to defraud any drug test, not merely tests for illegal drugs. That the indictment did not allege that defendant personally sold urine with the intent to defraud was not fatal. Defendant opened the door to cross-examination about pornographic links on his business's website, and was not prejudiced by this inquiry. In view of business cards enclosed with the kits that stated, "Pass Any Drug Test," and the claim on defendant's website that the kits allowed anyone to pass any urinalysis, the evidence was sufficient to justify submission of the case to the jury; the trial court properly denied defendant's motion for a directed verdict.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.
