Ward v. State


Opinion No. 25758


SUPREME COURT OF SOUTH CAROLINA


356 S.C. 449; 590 S.E.2d 30; 2003 S.C. LEXIS 298; 32 Employee Benefits Cas. (BNA) 1732


September 25, 2003, Heard  
December 8, 2003, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Later proceeding at Ward v. South Carolina, 158 L. Ed. 2d 728, 124 S. Ct. 2198, 2004 U.S. LEXIS 3394 (U.S., 2004)
US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Ward v. South Carolina, 2004 U.S. LEXIS 5518 (U.S., Oct. 4, 2004)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal From Richland County. L. Casey Manning, Circuit Court Judge.
Ward v. State, 343 S.C. 14, 538 S.E.2d 245, 2000 S.C. LEXIS 213 (S.C., 2000)



DISPOSITION: AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Richland County Circuit Court (South Carolina) upheld the constitutionality of 1989 S.C. Acts 623 (Act 189). Appellant, a federal retiree, challenged the decision on behalf of a class of federal retirees.


OVERVIEW: The federal retirees claimed that South Carolina discriminated in taxation between state and federal retirees in violation of the federal constitutional and statutory intergovernmental tax immunity doctrine. The issue on appeal was whether the circuit court erred in holding that Act 189 did not violate the intergovernmental tax immunity doctrine codified in 4 U.S.C.S. § 111, and was therefore constitutional. The appellate court concluded that Act 189 was not, in effect, a tax rebate that implicated the intergovernmental tax immunity doctrine. The power to compensate state retirees was clearly not delegated to the United States by the Constitution. As long as federal and state retirees were taxed equally, any restriction on this authority would violate U.S. Const. amend. X. Even if the elimination of the tax exemption for state retirees and the simultaneous increase in benefits were read together, the doctrine of intergovernmental tax immunity was not violated. South Carolina has specifically followed the remedy prescribed by Davis; therefore, the federal retirees' argument was unavailing.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

 

132 of 275 DOCUMENTS


State v. Hook


Opinion No. 25752


SUPREME COURT OF SOUTH CAROLINA


356 S.C. 421; 590 S.E.2d 25; 2003 S.C. LEXIS 286


October 8, 2003, Heard  
November 24, 2003, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Rehearing denied by State v. Hook, 2004 S.C. LEXIS 12 (S.C., Jan. 7, 2004)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from Barnwell County Thomas W. Cooper, Jr., Circuit Court Judge.
State v. Hook, 348 S.C. 401, 559 S.E.2d 856, 2001 S.C. App. LEXIS 167 (S.C. Ct. App., 2001)



DISPOSITION: AFFIRMED AS MODIFIED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The State appealed, by writ of certiorari, a decision by the Court of Appeals (South Carolina), reversing the trial court's decision after it admitted defendant's statement to his probation officer for impeachment purposes, holding that defendant's statement to his probation officer was inadmissible at defendant's trial for driving under the influence (DUI), third offense, and remanding the matter for a new trial.


OVERVIEW: The trial judge ruled that defendant's statement to his probation officer was admissible for impeachment only. The intermediate court held that the statement was inadmissible, even for impeachment purposes, as it was involuntary. The state's highest court held that the phrase "unless ordered by the court" in S.C. Code Ann. § 24-21-290 (Supp. 2002) modified only the third phrase, which related narrowly to disclosure, and did not modify the information's receipt as evidence in the trial court, which was specifically prohibited under the second phrase. By its terms, § 24-21-290 made information given by a probationer to his probation agent inadmissible as evidence with no exception. A plain reading of the statute left no room for grafting onto it an exception to exclusion when the evidence was used for impeachment purposes. Whether the statement was voluntary or involuntary was irrelevant. Under § 24-21-290, a probationer's statement to his probation agent was inadmissible for impeachment regardless of whether it was voluntarily or involuntarily given.


OUTCOME: The intermediate court's decision was affirmed as modified.

 

133 of 275 DOCUMENTS


Ed Robinson Laundry & Dry Cleaning, Inc. v. S.C. Dep't of Revenue


Opinion No. 25731


SUPREME COURT OF SOUTH CAROLINA


356 S.C. 120; 588 S.E.2d 97; 2003 S.C. LEXIS 240


May 14, 2003, Heard  
October 13, 2003, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1]
Rehearing denied by Ed Robinson Laundry v. S.C. Dep't of Revenue, 2003 S.C. LEXIS 288 (S.C., Nov. 20, 2003)


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal From Richland County. G. Thomas Cooper, Jr., Circuit Court Judge.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant taxpayer sought review of the judgment of the Richland County Circuit Court (South Carolina) finding that the South Carolina Sales and Use Tax Act, S.C. Code Ann. § 12-36-910 et seq., did not violate equal protection under U.S. Const. amend. XIV and S.C. Const. art. I, § 3.


OVERVIEW: S.C. Code Ann. § 12-36-2120(24) of the Act provided dry cleaners with a tax exemption for supplies and machinery used to perform their services. The Act imposed a sales tax on dry cleaners based on later-earned receipts. The supreme court held that this did not constitute a violation of equal protection. The taxpayer argued that the sales tax violated equal protection, because it was not imposed upon all service providers, only dry cleaners. The supreme court found that a class could be constitutionally confined to a particular trade, like dry cleaners. The taxpayer's claim failed, as it did not claim that the State taxed some dry cleaners differently from others. Furthermore, the State had a rational basis for treating dry cleaners differently from other service providers. The legislature achieved its goal of encouraging the establishment of dry cleaning business by exempting taxation on expensive startup costs in return for allowing the payment of sales taxes based on later-earned receipts.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

 

134 of 275 DOCUMENTS


Camburn v. Smith


Opinion No. 25701


SUPREME COURT OF SOUTH CAROLINA


355 S.C. 574; 586 S.E.2d 565; 2003 S.C. LEXIS 195


June 12, 2003, Heard  
August 18, 2003, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Rehearing denied by Camburn v. Smith, 2003 S.C. LEXIS 248 (S.C., Oct. 8, 2003)
Rehearing denied by Camburn v. Smith, 2003 S.C. LEXIS 249 (S.C., Oct. 8, 2003)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from Horry County. H.T. Abbott, III, Family Court Judge.



DISPOSITION: REVERSED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, a mother and her husband (parents), appealed an order by the Horry County Family Court (South Carolina) that, inter alia, awarded visitation with the mother's three children to respondent grandparents pursuant to S.C. Code Ann. § 20-7-420(33) (Supp. 2002).


OVERVIEW: The grandparents sought visitation with the mother's three children, who had different fathers. (One father's location was unknown, one was incarcerated, and the third was the mother's current husband.) The children resided with the parents. The parents and the incarcerated father objected to the visitation. The family court found there was no evidence to "justify" the parents' decision denying visitation and that visitation was in the children's best interest. The state supreme court held that there had been no finding that the parents were unfit. The family court's application of § 20-7-420(33) unduly interfered with the parents' constitutional rights in the care, custody, and control of their children. Because the visitation order had to be reversed, the parents did not have to pay the attorney and guardian ad litem fees.


OUTCOME: The order was reversed.

 

135 of 275 DOCUMENTS


Ex parte Condon


Opinion No. 25670


SUPREME COURT OF SOUTH CAROLINA


354 S.C. 634; 583 S.E.2d 430; 2003 S.C. LEXIS 150


April 2, 2003, Heard  
June 30, 2003, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal From Richland County. G. Thomas Cooper, Jr., Circuit Court Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The parties in a class action suit reached a settlement. The Richland County Circuit Court (South Carolina) found that an award of 28 percent of the common fund, plus costs, was a fair and reasonable attorneys' fee. Appellant South Carolina Attorney General filed a notice of appeal from the order awarding attorneys' fees and costs. The state supreme court certified the case for review pursuant to S.C. App. Ct. R. 204(b).


OVERVIEW: Respondent citizen filed a class action suit on behalf of himself and those similarly situated against respondents, the State of South Carolina and its Department of Revenue (DOR), alleging that he and other South Carolina citizens 85 years of age and older had failed to receive the one percent sales tax exemption provided for in S.C. Code Ann. §§ 12-36-2620, 12-36-2630. On appeal, the issues were: (1) whether the Attorney General's appeal from the circuit court's award of attorneys' fees was properly before the state supreme court, and (2) if so, whether the circuit court's award of attorneys' fees was reasonable. The state supreme court concluded that the Attorney General's appeal should be dismissed because it was undisputed that the Attorney General was not a party to the action originally, and never intervened to become a party as required by S.C. R. Civ. P. 24. The Attorney General was required, like everyone else, to formally intervene and become a named party before he could file an appeal.


OUTCOME: The appeal was dismissed.

 

136 of 275 DOCUMENTS


State v. Michau


Opinion No. 25679


SUPREME COURT OF SOUTH CAROLINA


355 S.C. 73; 583 S.E.2d 756; 2003 S.C. LEXIS 161


May 28, 2003, Heard  
July 21, 2003, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1]
Rehearing denied by State v. Michau, 2003 S.C. LEXIS 191 (S.C., Aug. 6, 2003)
Related proceeding at, Remanded by Michau v. Cannon, 2006 S.C. App. Unpub. LEXIS 198 (S.C. Ct. App., June 30, 2006)


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal From Charleston County Paula H. Thomas, Circuit Court Judge.



DISPOSITION: AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was convicted of contributing to the delinquency of a minor and participating in prostitution of a minor in the Charleston County Circuit Court (South Carolina). Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: Defendant argued the trial court erred by denying his motion to quash the indictment for contributing to the delinquency of a minor on the basis that the indictment was void for vagueness. He specifically claimed the phrase "endanger the morals or health" was unconstitutionally overbroad and vague. The appellate court found that the phrase "endanger the morals or health" in the indictment derived directly from the language of the statute defendant was accused of violating, S.C. Code Ann. § 16-17-490(10) (2003). The indictment was sufficient. Defendant's act of encouraging a minor to drink beer and smoke marijuana, conduct which was illegal, clearly endangered the morals, if not the health, of the minor. Accordingly, defendant lacked standing to challenge the constitutionality of S.C. Code Ann. § 16-17-490(10) (2003). In any event, the statute was not unconstitutionally vague. Three sentences in defendant's written statement to police did not constitute propensity evidence under S.C. R. Evid. 404(b). The sentences at issue did not refer to any "crimes, wrongs, or acts."


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

 

137 of 275 DOCUMENTS


Campbell v. Hilton Head No. 1 Pub. Serv. Dist.


Opinion No. 25635


SUPREME COURT OF SOUTH CAROLINA


354 S.C. 190; 580 S.E.2d 137; 2003 S.C. LEXIS 93


February 20, 2003, Heard  
April 28, 2003, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Campbell v. Hilton Head No. 1 Public Serv. Dist., 157 L. Ed. 2d 277, 124 S. Ct. 388, 2003 U.S. LEXIS 7453 (U.S., Oct. 14, 2003)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal From Beaufort County. Perry M. Buckner, Circuit Court Judge.
Lawyer v. Hilton Head Pub. Serv. Dist. No. 1, 220 F.3d 298, 2000 U.S. App. LEXIS 17547 (4th Cir. S.C., 2000)



DISPOSITION: REVERSED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Respondent property owners sued appellants special purpose district and county, alleging, inter alia, a 42 U.S.C.S. § 1983 claim based on an alleged violation of the United States Constitution. The Beaufort County Circuit Court (South Carolina) granted summary judgment in favor of the property owners. The special purpose district and the county appealed.


OVERVIEW: The special purpose district was made up of a commission whose members were appointed by the governor. At relevant times, the special purpose district levied taxes on property within its service area. The county collected the taxes. The property owners paid taxes on their property in the service area of the special purpose district, but did not receive water or sewer service from the special purpose district. The trial court found that this was taxation without representation, which violated the Republican Guarantee Clause, U.S. Const. art. IV, § 4. Only the property owners' 42 U.S.C.S. § 1983 claim remained. The appellate court ruled that U.S. Const. art. IV, § 4 did not guarantee against taxation without representation. The United States Supreme Court refused to transmute the revolutionary slogan "no taxation without representation" into a constitutional principle and repeatedly rejected the proposition that the Federal Constitution guaranteed no taxation without representation. Because there was no independent, federal right found in U.S. Const. art. IV, § 4 prohibiting taxation without representation, the property owners had no 42 U.S.C.S. § 1983 action.


OUTCOME: The judgment was reversed.

 

138 of 275 DOCUMENTS


State v. Peake


Opinion No. 25614


SUPREME COURT OF SOUTH CAROLINA


353 S.C. 499; 579 S.E.2d 297; 2003 S.C. LEXIS 62


October 23, 2002, Heard  
March 31, 2003, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1]
Rehearing denied by State v. Peake, 2003 S.C. LEXIS 103 (S.C., Apr. 23, 2003)


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal From Greenwood County. Wyatt T. Saunders, Jr., Circuit Court Judge.
State v. Peake, 345 S.C. 72, 545 S.E.2d 840, 2001 S.C. App. LEXIS 53 (S.C. Ct. App., 2001)



DISPOSITION: AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of the judgment of the Court of Appeals of South Carolina reversing the trial court judgment quashing an indictment charging defendant with violating the Pollution Control Act, S.C. Code Ann. § 48-1-10 et seq.


OVERVIEW: Defendant owned a private water treatment plant. The department of health and environmental control informed defendant that his plant violated Pollution Control Act, S.C. Code Ann. § 48-1-10 et seq., and the department suggested that he pay a substantial fine. The department and defendant settled the civil matter by having defendant deed the waste treatment plant to a town. Defendant was indicted shortly thereafter for violating the act. Defendant and his attorney testified that they assumed that the settlement covered everything. A department employee never affirmatively represented that the settlement covered civil liability issues and criminal charges. On appeal, defendant asserted that S.C. Code Ann. § 48-1-210 (1996 & Supp. 2001) vested prosecutorial authority with the department. S.C. Code Ann. § 48-1-220 (1996 & Supp. 2001) vested the authority for civil prosecutions with the department, and it, along with S.C. Code Ann. § 48-1-210, vested criminal prosecutions with the attorney general. Defendant did not reasonably assume that the settlement dealt with civil and criminal claims under the act. The department had no authority under the act to settle criminal claims.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

 

139 of 275 DOCUMENTS


Beaufort County v. State


Opinion No. 25601


SUPREME COURT OF SOUTH CAROLINA


353 S.C. 240; 577 S.E.2d 457; 2003 S.C. LEXIS 43


January 8, 2003, Heard  
March 3, 2003, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal From Beaufort County Thomas Kemmerlin, Circuit Court Judge.



DISPOSITION: AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant Beaufort County sought review of the order of the Beaufort County Circuit Court (South Carolina) granting summary judgment in favor of appellee State of South Carolina, finding that S.C. Code Ann. § 27-32-240 (Supp. 2000) did not violate S.C. Const. art. X.


OVERVIEW: The county asserted that S.C. Code Ann. § 27-32-240 (Supp. 2000) violated S.C. Const. art. X, §§ 1, 2. The county construed S.C. Const. art. X, § 1 as mandating that the legislature value property in each subset of the section uniformly. The county asserted that S.C. Code Ann. § 27-32-240 (Supp. 2000) could not require a local assessor to value similar property differently. S.C. Const. art. X, § 2 conferred upon the legislature the power to define the classes of property and values for property tax purposes of the classes of property set forth in S.C. Const. art. X, § 1. S.C. Const. art. X, § 2 was logical only if read to allow the legislature to define sub-classes of property for those classes enumerated in S.C. Const. art. X, § 1 and to determine how each sub-class was valued for tax purposes. Under S.C. Const. art. X, § 1, the methodology to determine the value of the property remained a matter for the legislature. S.C. Code Ann. § 27-32-240 did not violate S.C. Const. art. X. The legislature was empowered to determine that a vacation time sharing ownership plan was valued differently from a vacation time sharing lease plan under S.C. Const. art. X, § 2.


OUTCOME: The order was affirmed.

Beaufort County Bd. of Educ. v. Lighthouse Charter Sch. Comm.


Opinion No. 25583


SUPREME COURT OF SOUTH CAROLINA


353 S.C. 24; 576 S.E.2d 180; 2003 S.C. LEXIS 22


April 16, 2002, Heard; December 4, 2002, Reheard  
January 27, 2003, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from Beaufort County. Jackson V. Gregory, Circuit Court Judge.



DISPOSITION: Vacated and appeal dismissed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant charter school committee sued respondent Beaufort County (South Carolina) Board of Education after the Board denied the committee's application to establish a charter school. On remand from the state supreme court, the Circuit Court, Beaufort County (South Carolina), held that S.C. Code Ann. § 59-40-50(B)(6) (Supp. 2001) was unconstitutional. The committee, the State of South Carolina, and the Board filed cross appeals.


OVERVIEW: A charter school committee sued the Beaufort County (South Carolina) Board of Education after the Board denied the committee's application to establish a charter school. The trial court upheld the Board's decision, and the state supreme court affirmed the trial court's decision but remanded the case for a determination of whether S.C. Code Ann. § 59-40-50(B)(6) (Supp. 2001) was constitutional. On remand, the trial court found that the racial composition requirement of § 59-40-50(B)(6) violated equal protection and that § 59-40-50(B)(6) was not severable from the remaining parts of the South Carolina Charter Schools Act of 1996. Accordingly, the trial court declared that the Charter Schools Act was unconstitutional in its entirety. On appeal after remand, the state supreme court held that because § 59-40-50(B)(6) had been replaced in its entirety when the governor signed 2002 S.C. Acts 341 on July 1, 2002, and was no longer applicable, the court would vacate the trial court's judgment holding the Charter Schools Act unconstitutional and dismiss the appeal as moot.


OUTCOME: The state supreme court vacated the trial court's judgment as moot, and dismissed the appeal.

Greenville County v. Kenwood Enters.


Opinion No. 25584


SUPREME COURT OF SOUTH CAROLINA


353 S.C. 157; 577 S.E.2d 428; 2003 S.C. LEXIS 16


November 19, 2002, Heard  
January 27, 2003, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal From Greenville County Wyatt T. Saunders, Jr, Circuit Court Judge.



DISPOSITION: AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant businesses appealed a grant of summary judgment in favor of respondent county by the Greenville County Circuit Court (South Carolina); the businesses claimed, inter alia, that a county ordinance regulating the location of sexually oriented businesses was unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: After the county enacted a zoning ordinance that established a licensing scheme and location restrictions for sexually oriented businesses, the licensing portion was declared unconstitutional. Two of the businesses claimed that the ordinance was unconstitutional and that the county was estopped from enforcing the ordinance against them. The state supreme court held that because the ordinance was enacted pursuant to the county's police powers under S.C. Code Ann. § 4-9-25 (Law. Co-Op. Supp. 2001), it was not part of the zoning ordinance and did not have to be enacted pursuant to the South Carolina Local Government Comprehensive Planning Enabling Act of 1994, S.C. Code Ann. § 6-29-310 et seq. (Supp. 2001). It was not preempted by the Act. The ordinance was content-neutral and merely regulated the businesses; therefore, it was constitutional. The county's representation that the ordinance was unconstitutional was a statement of law that could not be a basis for estoppel. Where a third business had a nonconforming use, no taking occurred since that business could continue in its existing location, but not as an adult use.


OUTCOME: The grant of summary judgment was affirmed on all claims.

State v. McKnight


Opinion No. 25585


SUPREME COURT OF SOUTH CAROLINA


352 S.C. 635; 576 S.E.2d 168; 2003 S.C. LEXIS 23


November 6, 2002, Heard  
January 27, 2003, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Subsequent appeal at State v. McKnight, 353 S.C. 238, 577 S.E.2d 456, 2003 S.C. LEXIS 37 (S.C., 2003)
US Supreme Court certiorari denied by McKnight v. South Carolina, 540 U.S. 819, 124 S. Ct. 101, 157 L. Ed. 2d 36, 2003 U.S. LEXIS 5604 (2003)
Post-conviction relief granted at McKnight v. State, 2008 S.C. LEXIS 142 (S.C., May 12, 2008)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal From Horry County James E. Brogdon, Jr., Circuit Court Judge.



DISPOSITION: AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed a judgment of the Horry County Circuit Court (South Carolina), which, after a jury trial, convicted her of homicide by child abuse in violation of S.C. Code Ann. § 16-3-85(A).


OVERVIEW: Defendant gave birth to a nearly full-term stillborn child. An autopsy showed a substance metabolized by cocaine in the infant. Defendant was convicted of homicide by child abuse and on her appeal, the court affirmed. The court held that defendant was not entitled to a directed verdict because the State sufficiently demonstrated that the cocaine caused the stillbirth. Defendant's act of using cocaine, knowing that she was pregnant, was sufficient to go to the jury on whether she acted with extreme indifference. The legislature intended § 16-3-85(A) to apply to the stillbirth of a fetus given the legislature's inaction after the court previously held that the term "child" included a viable fetus. Defendant had ample notice that her conduct was proscribed and therefore there was no due process violation. Defendant's use of cocaine while pregnant did not trigger a right to privacy. There was no Eighth Amendment violation because the gravity of the offense was severe, the penalty was proportionate to other murder offenses, and other states imposed similarly severe sentences. Defendant's urine test after the delivery was properly admitted because she voluntarily consented to the test.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the decision of the trial court.

Gardner v. S.C. Dep't of Revenue


Opinion No. 25587


SUPREME COURT OF SOUTH CAROLINA


353 S.C. 1; 577 S.E.2d 190; 2003 S.C. LEXIS 20


November 19, 2002, Heard  
January 27, 2003, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Corrected.
Rehearing denied by Gardner v. S.C. Dep't of Revenue, 2003 S.C. LEXIS 50 (S.C., Mar. 7, 2003)


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal From Spartanburg County. Gary E. Clary, Circuit Court Judge.



DISPOSITION: AFFIRMED IN PART, REVERSED IN PART, AND REMANDED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff taxpayers brought a declaratory judgment action against defendant agencies, asserting the improper seizure of their income tax refunds. The taxpayers sought injunctive relief, return of seized refunds, and damages. In addition, they sought to be certified as class representatives. The Spartanburg County Circuit Court (South Carolina) granted summary judgment and issued a series of orders in favor of the taxpayers. The agencies appealed.


OVERVIEW: This appeal concerned the Setoff Debt Collection Act, S.C. Code Ann. § 12-56-10 et seq., and the 1999 amendments thereto, 1999 S.C. Acts 1177, No. 1144. The trial court found that the notices sent by the claimant agencies did not adequately set out the review procedure, as contemplated by the 1995 provisions of the Act. The appellate court agreed. The instant case involved, in part, the years of 1996, 1997, and 1998. Therefore, the 1995 provisions were applicable. Despite the inadequate notice, the appellate court found that each taxpayer had to establish prejudice. Although the trial court did not specifically address the issue, it appeared that it improperly determined that violation of the notice provision constituted prejudice in and of itself. The taxpayers needed to establish that their debts were not due and owing, which they had not done. If there were a refund due, it would be from the agency that collected the setoff. The taxpayers did not show commonality, and the trial court erred by certifying both a plaintiff and defendant class. Each case had individual facts with regard to prejudice, which needed to be investigated, and which precluded a class action.


OUTCOME: The orders of the trial judge were affirmed in part, reversed in part, and remanded for further action.

 

144 of 275 DOCUMENTS


Fop v. S.C. Dep't of Revenue


Opinion No. 25566


SUPREME COURT OF SOUTH CAROLINA


352 S.C. 420; 574 S.E.2d 717; 2002 S.C. LEXIS 240


October 8, 2002, Heard  
December 9, 2002, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal From Charleston County. H. Dean Hall, Circuit Court Judge.



DISPOSITION: AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant taxpayers challenged the constitutionality of the Bingo Act of 1989, S.C. Code Ann. § 12-21-3310 et seq. (Supp. 1994), and two additional bingo statutes, S.C. Code Ann. §§ 12-21-3441, 12-21-3610 (Supp. 1995) in an effort to recover taxes paid to respondent South Carolina Department of Revenue. The taxpayers appealed the decision of the Charleston County Circuit Court (South Carolina), which ruled in the Department's favor.


OVERVIEW: On appeal, the parties raised numerous constitutional issues. The state supreme court concluded that the plain meaning of S.C. Const. art. XVII, § 7 was that bingo remained a lottery, but was a lottery that was not prohibited by the state constitution. The taxpayers' argument that they had a constitutional right to conduct bingo was flawed because bingo was a lottery and was gambling, and there was no right to gamble under the state constitution. In addition, the regulation of bingo had a legitimate state purpose (preventing the proliferation of crime), the regulations were rationally related to that purpose, and all charities that conducted bingo were treated alike. The taxpayers cited no authority for the proposition that conducting bingo was a fundamental right. The First Amendment challenge was not pleaded, discussed extensively at trial, or ruled upon by the trial judge; thus, it was not preserved for review. The taxpayers' argument that an implied exemption arose out of the State's historical tax treatment of charities was insufficient to show an implied exemption. Finally, the taxpayers' claims were not barred by res judicata.


OUTCOME: The decision of the trial court was affirmed.

 

145 of 275 DOCUMENTS


State v. Rothschild


Opinion No. 25518


SUPREME COURT OF SOUTH CAROLINA


351 S.C. 238; 569 S.E.2d 346; 2002 S.C. LEXIS 145


June 25, 2002, Heard  
August 26, 2002, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal From Richland County. William P. Keesley, Circuit Court Judge.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed a judgment by the Richland County Circuit Court (South Carolina) that convicted him of possession of adulterants intended to defeat a urine drug test under S.C. Code Ann. § 16-13-470(A)(4) (Supp. 2001) and denied his motion for a directed verdict.


OVERVIEW: Defendant owned two stores in which he sold products that were advertised as bodily system cleansing agents for drug tests. Undercover police purchased some of the products and arrested defendant for violating § 16-13-470(A)(4). The state supreme court held that adulterating a urine sample to defeat a drug or alcohol test was an illegal activity. Commercial speech promoting an illegal activity was not protected and evidence of such speech could be used to prove intent under § 16-13-470(A)(4) without violating the First Amendment. Defendant's advertisement and the other adulterants seized from his store evincing defendant's mental culpability were relevant and properly admitted. Further, it could be inferred from the advertisements that defendant possessed the adulterants with the mens rea that they be used to defeat drug or alcohol tests.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

 

146 of 275 DOCUMENTS


State v. Standard


Opinion No. 25514


SUPREME COURT OF SOUTH CAROLINA


351 S.C. 199; 569 S.E.2d 325; 2002 S.C. LEXIS 138


June 26, 2002, Heard  
August 12, 2002, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Writ of certiorari denied: Standard v. South Carolina, 2003 U.S. LEXIS 1218 (U.S. Feb. 24, 2003).
Writ of certiorari denied Standard v. South Carolina, 537 U.S. 1195, 123 S. Ct. 1255, 154 L. Ed. 2d 1032, 2003 U.S. LEXIS 1218 (2003)
Post-conviction relief denied at Standard v. State, 2007 S.C. Unpub. LEXIS 6 (2007)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal From Anderson County. Alexander S. Macaulay, Circuit Court Judge.



DISPOSITION: AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed the judgment of the Anderson County Circuit Court (South Carolina) convicting him of first degree burglary and grand larceny and sentencing him to life imprisonment without parole pursuant to S.C. Code Ann. § 17-25-45 (2001).


OVERVIEW: In 1995, when defendant was 15 years old, he committed armed robbery. He was convicted as an adult of armed robbery and given a youthful offender sentence. Shortly after his release, defendant and two others broke into a mobile home, vandalized the residence, and stole cash, jewelry, and other items. The victim testified that her home was destroyed and that her animals were beaten and killed. Based upon defendant's most serious armed robbery conviction, the trial court sentenced him under the Two-Strikes Law. The appellate court found that S.C. Code Ann. § 17-25-45 did not violate the separation of powers doctrine by removing discretion from the judiciary, as the penalty assessed for a particular offense was, except in the rarest of cases, purely a matter of legislative prerogative. The sentence of life imprisonment without parole did not constitute cruel and unusual punishment despite defendant's age at the time he committed the armed robbery. Such a sentence did not offend evolving standards of decency, as a large number of states permitted severe punishments for youthful offenders.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

In re Allen


Opinion No. 25504


SUPREME COURT OF SOUTH CAROLINA


351 S.C. 153; 568 S.E.2d 354; 2002 S.C. LEXIS 123


February 6, 2002, Heard  
July 22, 2002, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal From Greenville County. John C. Hayes, III, Circuit Court Judge.



DISPOSITION: AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed the order of the Greenville County Circuit Court (South Carolina), which confined him to the Department of Mental Health, pursuant to the South Carolina Sexually Violent Predator Act (Act), S.C. Code Ann. § 44-48-10, et seq.


OVERVIEW: Defendant argued on appeal the Act was not civil in nature and therefore violated the Double Jeopardy and Ex Post Facto Clauses of the United States Constitution. Specifically, defendant argued the trial court erred in relying on the United States Supreme Court's decision in Kansas to hold the Act did not violate the constitution, as Kansas' Sexually Violent Predator Act was "markedly different" from South Carolina's. The supreme court advised that it had already directly addressed the issues raised by defendant and found them to be without merit. With regard to defendant's argument that those committed under the Act were not treated as mentally ill persons, but as criminals, as those committed under the Act could be housed in ordinary jails and prisons, the supreme court advised that it previously held that housing sexually dangerous persons within a maximum-security prison did not transform the State's intent to treat into an intent to punish.


OUTCOME: The supreme court affirmed the order.

In re Treatment & Care of Luckabaugh


Opinion No. 25503


SUPREME COURT OF SOUTH CAROLINA


351 S.C. 122; 568 S.E.2d 338; 2002 S.C. LEXIS 126


January 8, 2002, Heard  
July 22, 2002, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal From Charleston County. Daniel E. Martin, Sr., Circuit Court Judge.



DISPOSITION: VACATED, REVERSED, AND REMANDED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: A commitment hearing was conducted in the Charleston County Circuit Court (South Carolina) to determine whether appellant inmate was a sexually violent predator. The trial court found that the State failed to prove that the inmate was a sexually violent predator. The trial court also ruled that the South Carolina Sexually Violent Predator Act, S.C. Code Ann. §§ 44-48-10 to 44-48-170 (Supp. 2000), was unconstitutional. The State appealed.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was an inmate serving a sentence for sexual assault. Three medical experts testified at a commitment hearing that the inmate suffered from sexual sadism, was a risk to re-offend, and needed aggressive medical treatment. The inmate disagreed with the diagnosis. The inmate's medical expert differed with the State as to whether the inmate could successfully complete treatment on an outpatient basis. The lower court found that the State failed to meet its burden of proof and that the South Carolina Sexually Violent Predator Act violated the ex post facto clause of the South Carolina Constitution. On appeal, the court found that (1) the lower court failed to provide adequate findings of fact as to whether the State failed to meet its burden of proof as to the inmate being a sexually violent predator; (2) the Act did not violate the ex post facto provision of the South Carolina Constitution; (3) the Act did not violate the Substantive Due Process Clause of the United States and South Carolina Constitutions; and (4) the Act did not violate the Equal Protection Clause of the United States and South Carolina Constitutions.


OUTCOME: The portions of the lower court's order finding that the State did not meet its burden of proving the inmate was a sexually violent predator and that the South Carolina Sexually Violent Predator Act violated the ex post facto clause of the South Carolina Constitution were reversed. The case was remanded for further proceedings.

Johnson v. Collins Entm't Co.


Opinion No. 25474


SUPREME COURT OF SOUTH CAROLINA


349 S.C. 613; 564 S.E.2d 653; 2002 S.C. LEXIS 93


December 11, 2001, Heard  
May 28, 2002, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Related proceeding at Collins Holding Corp. v. Wausau Underwriters Ins. Co., 2008 S.C. LEXIS 280 (S.C., Sept. 8, 2008)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On Certification from the United States District Court for the District of South Carolina. Joseph F. Anderson, Jr., United States District Judge.
Johnson v. Collins Entertainment Co., 199 F.3d 710, 1999 U.S. App. LEXIS 33892 (4th Cir. S.C., 1999)



DISPOSITION: CERTIFIED QUESTIONS ANSWERED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff habitual gamblers sued defendant video poker machine operators, alleging violations of the Racketeer Influenced Corrupt Organizations Act (RICO), the South Carolina Unfair Trade Practices Act (SCUTPA), S.C. Code Ann. § 39-5-10 et seq., and S.C. Code Ann. §§ 32-1-10, 32-1-20. The case was removed to the United States District Court for the District of South Carolina, which later certified questions.


OVERVIEW: The habitual gamblers argued that the video poker machine operators' payout offers and jackpot displays violated S.C. Code Ann. § 12-21-2804(B) (repealed 2000) (Act). The appellate court had earlier held unconstitutional the part of the Act which banned advertising for playing video poker machines, but held that the Act's unconstitutional part was severable from the ban on specified payouts. For commercial speech to come within the First Amendment, it needed to concern lawful activity and not be misleading. Since, under the Act, certain payouts were illegal, the advertisement of such payouts was not protected speech. The Act was not unconstitutionally vague. Payouts in violation of the Act were prohibited under 27 S.C. Code Ann. Regs. 117-190.1. Lessors were banned from making or allowing to be made special inducements, even if they did not physically maintain or own the premises where the machines were located. Violations of the Act served as predicates in a RICO cause of action. S.C. Code Ann. §§ 32-1-10, -20 did not have any preclusive effect relating to SCUPTA and the video poker machine operators had no protection in the "regulated activity" exemption of SCUTPA.


OUTCOME: The certified questions were answered.

 

150 of 275 DOCUMENTS


Morris v. Anderson County


Opinion No. 25473


SUPREME COURT OF SOUTH CAROLINA


349 S.C. 607; 564 S.E.2d 649; 2002 S.C. LEXIS 94


January 8, 2002, Heard  
May 28, 2002, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Corrected June 28, 2002. Rehearing Denied June 26, 2002.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal From Anderson County. J. C. Nicholson, Jr., Circuit Court Judge.



DISPOSITION: AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant administrators of deceased victims' estates brought a negligence action against respondent county and sheriff's department alleging gross negligence. The Anderson County Circuit Court, South Carolina, granted summary judgment in favor of the county and sheriff's department. The administrators appealed.


OVERVIEW: Police officers were authorized to pick up a person who was alleged to be mentally ill. Three weeks later, he was driving a vehicle when he collided with the victims' vehicle causing their death. The administrators had alleged that the county and the sheriff's department were grossly negligent in failing to pick up the mentally ill person on the detention order. On appeal, the administrators argued, inter alia, that summary judgment was improperly granted to the county and sheriff's department on the basis of the public duty rule. The administrators further argued that the protected class was all persons traveling on the highways of the county. The supreme court held that such class was not found in the statutes but rather was the post hoc class created in light of the accident. Where the statutory duty was owed to the public as a whole, then the public duty rule barred the administrators from maintaining a negligence action against the governmental entity. Because the statutes created no special duty running to the administrators, the trial court properly granted the county and the sheriff's department summary judgment on the statutory duty claims.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the trial court was affirmed.

 

151 of 275 DOCUMENTS


State v. Gaster


Opinion No. 25469


SUPREME COURT OF SOUTH CAROLINA


349 S.C. 545; 564 S.E.2d 87; 2002 S.C. LEXIS 85


December 11, 2001, Heard  
May 20, 2002, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal From Georgetown County L. Henry McKellar, Circuit Court Judge.



DISPOSITION: AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was convicted of second degree criminal sexual assault with a minor. When he was scheduled for release from prison, the State filed an action to have him designated a sexual predator, under the Sexually Violent Predator Act, S.C. Code Ann. § 44-48-10 et seq. (2000). A jury found him to be a sexually violent predator. He was committed for treatment, by order of the Georgetown County Circuit Court (South Carolina). Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: Defendant argued that the Sexually Violent Predator Act (Act), S.C. Code Ann. § 44-48-10 et seq. (2000), violated constitutional double jeopardy, ex post facto, and due process provisions. In rejecting this argument, the appellate court found that the Act was civil in nature, not criminal. The Act properly permitted confinement upon a determination that an offender currently suffered from both a mental abnormality or personality disorder and was likely to engage in acts of sexual violence. S.C. Code Ann. § 44-48-30(1) (2000). After commitment, defendant's custody was transferred from the department of mental health to the department of corrections. Although defendant argued that by being transferred he was subjected to conditions identical to those of prisoners, the appellate court found that there was no information in the record indicating that persons committed pursuant to the Act were being treated as if they were prisoners instead of as civilly committed persons. There was expert testimony that defendant suffered from two major mental illnesses: sadism and paraphilia, both sexual disorders. The evidence showed he was likely to engage in future acts of sexual violence.


OUTCOME: The order was affirmed.

 

152 of 275 DOCUMENTS


State v. Thompson


Opinion No. 25459


SUPREME COURT OF SOUTH CAROLINA


349 S.C. 346; 563 S.E.2d 325; 2002 S.C. LEXIS 76


March 21, 2002, Submitted  
May 6, 2002, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal From Laurens County Gary E. Clary, Circuit Court Judge.



DISPOSITION: AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed from the affirmance of his conviction by a magistrate of trapping beaver out of season by the Laurens County Circuit Court (South Carolina) pursuant to S.C. Code Ann. § 50-11-2540 (Supp. 2001).


OVERVIEW: Defendant had been issued a depredation permit, but had allowed it to expire. The appellate court held that S.C. Code Ann. §§ 50-11-2540, -2570 contained a reasonable limitation on the right to protect one's property. The protection of game and fish was a matter of public interest. The statutes represented a balanced approach, allowing a property owner to trap without a permit within 100 yards of her home, and there was no fee for a depredation permit. The statutes satisfied the rational basis test for equal protection purposes. The purpose of the statutes was to preserve wildlife, and they reasonably related to that goal. The statutes protected the furbearing animals in a reasonable manner, while also balancing a property owner's right to protect property from destruction. Members of the class were treated alike under similar circumstances. Finally, the classification based on the distance from the owner's home was rationally based. It was reasonable to allow a property owner to capture a damage-causing animal when it was close to the residence without first requiring the owner to secure a permit.


OUTCOME: The conviction was affirmed.

 

153 of 275 DOCUMENTS


State v. Wright


Opinion No. 25455


SUPREME COURT OF SOUTH CAROLINA


349 S.C. 310; 563 S.E.2d 311; 2002 S.C. LEXIS 70


May 24, 2001, Heard  
April 29, 2002, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal From Union County. J. Nicholson, Jr., Circuit Court Judge.



DISPOSITION: AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Union County trial court (South Carolina) entered a final judgment of conviction after a jury found defendant guilty of criminal domestic violence of a high and aggravated nature in violation of S.C. Code Ann. § 16-25-65(C). Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was six feet tall and weighed 216 pounds. He beat and kicked his wife so severely that she had to obtain medical assistance. Defendant was indicted for criminal domestic violence of a high and aggravated nature. The aggravating factors alleged in the indictment were "a difference in the sexes of the victim and the defendant" and/or that "the defendant did inflict serious bodily harm upon the victim by kicking her in the mid-section requiring her to seek medical attention." Defendant objected to the judge's charge on the aggravating circumstance of "a difference of the sexes," contending it violated equal protection. The objection was overruled. Defendant was found guilty as charged. After judgment was entered, the state supreme court found the aggravating circumstance of a "difference in the sexes" did not violate defendant's equal protection rights under the Fourteenth Amendment. It further found a state was not required to pretend differences did not exist between men and women and that the aggravating circumstance of which defendant complained realistically reflected the fact that men were generally stronger than women.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

 

154 of 275 DOCUMENTS


State ex rel. Condon v. Hodges


Opinion No. 25451


SUPREME COURT OF SOUTH CAROLINA


349 S.C. 232; 562 S.E.2d 623; 2002 S.C. LEXIS 65


February 21, 2002, Heard  
April 18, 2002, Filed



DISPOSITION:  [***1] The Attorney General is not prohibited from bringing a legal action against the Governor. The executive branch's actions violate separation of powers. The Governor is not required to return a balanced budget to the General Assembly.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff state attorney general brought suit against defendant state governor, seeking a determination as to whether there was authority to sue the governor, whether a separation of powers violation occurred, and whether the governor was required to return a balanced budget to the general assembly. Intervention was allowed, as was the filing of an amicus curiae brief.


OVERVIEW: The issue in the instant case involved the governor's re-direction of funds appropriated by the legislature. In his veto statement, the governor stated he was not vetoing supplemental appropriations to institutions of higher education because they agreed to return certain funds to the general fund. In the instant action, the governor argued it was impermissible for the attorney general (AG) to sue him, since he also represented him in other matters. In rejecting this argument, the highest court found that the AG had a dual role of being an attorney for the governor and an attorney for vindicating wrongs against the collective citizens of the state. Therefore, if the governor acted improperly the AG could sue him. The highest court found that the transfer of appropriated funds was for the legislative branch, not the executive branch. The concerted effort of the governor, comptroller, and treasurer, in transferring appropriated funds in the instant action, was improper. Finally, although the legislature was required to submit a balanced budget, there was no constitutional mandate for the governor, after exercising his veto power, to return a balanced budget to the legislature.


OUTCOME: The state's highest court concluded the attorney general was not prohibited from bringing a legal action against the governor, the executive branch's actions in the instant case resulted in a separation of powers violation, and the governor was not required to return a balanced budget to the general assembly.

 

155 of 275 DOCUMENTS


State v. White


Opinion No. 25421


SUPREME COURT OF SOUTH CAROLINA


348 S.C. 532; 560 S.E.2d 420; 2002 S.C. LEXIS 34


November 14, 2001, Heard  
March 4, 2002, Filed  [***1] 7400:::count###1:::


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Rehearing Denied March 21, 2002. Writ of certiorari denied: White v. South Carolina, 2002 U.S. LEXIS 6060 (U.S. Oct. 7, 2002).


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal From Florence County Hicks B. Harwell, Circuit Court Judge.



DISPOSITION: AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Florence County Circuit Court, South Carolina, convicted defendant for violation of S.C. Code Ann. § 16-17-700 (Supp. 2000), prohibiting the tattooing of another person except by a licensed physician for cosmetic or reconstructive purposes. Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: Defendant argued that the trial court erred in finding S.C. Code Ann. § 16-17-700 (Supp. 2000) did not violate defendant's freedom of speech as protected by the First Amendment of the United States Constitution and S.C. Const. art. I, § 2. The appellate court agreed with the State's position in that there were inherent risks to tattooing which gave the legislature wide latitude to determine how to best protect the general welfare of the state's inhabitants. Defendant did not make any showing that the process of tattooing was communicative enough to automatically fall within First Amendment protection. Defendant did not rebut the presumption of validity by showing the statute was arbitrary and unreasonable, with no relation to a legitimate governmental interest. Defendant put forth no evidence other than his own testimony and the testimony of a licensed tattoo artist, regarding the safety or danger of tattooing. Neither defendant nor the artist had any medical training and both admitted there were risks to tattooing if the proper precautions were not taken. Defendant as much as conceded a rational relationship between tattooing and public health.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

 

156 of 275 DOCUMENTS


Linder v. Ins. Claims Consultants, Inc.


Opinion No. 25417


SUPREME COURT OF SOUTH CAROLINA


348 S.C. 477; 560 S.E.2d 612; 2002 S.C. LEXIS 32


September 25, 2001, Heard  
February 25, 2002, Filed  [***1] 7400:::count###1:::



DISPOSITION: Business activities of first-party public adjusters was declared not to constitute the practice of law, subject to restrictions. However, because respondents engaged in prohibited acts, circuit court was directed to determine the value of respondents' authorized work. Finally, it was held there is no private right of action for the unauthorized practice of law.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Respondent public insurance adjusters sued petitioner insureds for breach of contract. The insureds filed a counterclaim, and asserted that the adjusters engaged in the unauthorized practice of law and therefore the contract between them was void ab initio. While that suit was pending, the insureds sought a declaratory judgment from the supreme court that the actions of the adjusters constituted the unauthorized practice of law.


OVERVIEW: The insureds hired the adjusters to resolve a coverage dispute with their homeowners insurer. A settlement was reached, but the insurer delayed payment. The adjusters sued the insureds for their contractual commission. The supreme court held that the business of first-party public insurance adjusting did not, per se, embody the practice of law; therefore, the contract was not void ab initio. Public adjusters could perform purely appraisal-oriented activities, submit claims to the insurer, and even negotiate with the insurer, as long as the discussions involved only property-damage valuations. However, if public adjusters (1) held themselves out as having legal skill, (2) advised clients of their rights under a policy regarding matters requiring legal skill, or (3) became involved in coverage disputes between the client and the insurer, they were engaging in the unauthorized practice of law. The adjusters violated (2) and (3), and were not entitled to commissions to the extent they did so. The adjusters' use of scales in its adverting was not equivalent to claiming to have legal skill. There was no private right of action for the unauthorized practice of law.


OUTCOME: A judgment was declared that the business activities of first-party public adjusters did not per se constitute the practice of law, subject to the restrictions outlined in the opinion. However, because the adjusters engaged in prohibited acts, the circuit court was directed to determine the value of the adjusters' authorized work. Finally, the supreme court held there was no private right of action for the unauthorized practice of law.

 

157 of 275 DOCUMENTS


State v. Walls


Opinion No. 25396


SUPREME COURT OF SOUTH CAROLINA


348 S.C. 26; 558 S.E.2d 524; 2002 S.C. LEXIS 2


October 24, 2001, Heard  
January 14, 2002, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:   [***1] Appeal From York County. Lee S. Alford, Circuit Court Judge.



DISPOSITION: AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was convicted by the York County Circuit Court, South Carolina, for failing to register as a sex offender under the South Carolina Sex Offender Registry Act (Act), S.C. Code Ann. § 23-3- 400 to -530 (Supp. 2000). Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: The main issue before the supreme court was whether the Act violated the ex post facto clause. The supreme court held that the Act met the first prong of determining whether it fell within ex post facto prohibitions; that is, the Act was retroactive because it applied to events which occurred before its enactment. In particular, it applied to defendant, whose offense was committed in 1973, prior to the enactment of the Act. Since it was clear that the general assembly did not intend to punish sex offenders, but instead intended to protect the public from those sex offenders who might re-offend and to aid law enforcement in solving sex crimes, the language indicated the intention to create a non-punitive law. The Act was not so punitive in purpose or effect as to constitute a criminal penalty. Thus, the Act did not violate the ex post facto clauses of the state or federal Constitutions.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the trial court was affirmed.

Knotts v. S.C. Dep't of Natural Res.


Opinion No. 25395


SUPREME COURT OF SOUTH CAROLINA


348 S.C. 1; 558 S.E.2d 511; 2002 S.C. LEXIS 1


November 14, 2001, Heard  
January 7, 2002, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:   [***1] Appeal From Lexington County. Marc H. Westbrook, Circuit Court Judge.



DISPOSITION: REVERSED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellee county legislative delegation sued appellant state natural resources department in the Lexington County Circuit Court (South Carolina), over allocating money from the South Carolina Water Recreational Resources Fund (W.R.R.F.). The trial court issued a writ of mandamus ordering the department to process the delegation's funding approval. The department appealed.


OVERVIEW:  S.C. Code Ann. § 12-28-2730 (Supp. 2000) provided for the funding of the W.R.R.F. The department oversaw the allocation of funds from the W.R.R.F. The delegation forwarded its approval to the department to disperse W.R.R.F. funds to constituent organizations. The department informed the delegation that it would not process the request until it ascertained what W.R.R.F. funds it would use to comply with provisos in the South Carolina 2001-2002 Appropriations Act. The delegation then sought to compel the department to disperse the funds as it had approved. On appeal, the supreme court found that S.C. Code Ann. § 12-28-2730 unconstitutionally usurped executive powers for the benefit of legislative delegations, because the South Carolina Legislature was constitutionally forbidden from undertaking to pass laws and then to execute them by bestowing upon its own members powers belonging to the executive branch. Further, § 12-28-2730 could not survive the severing of the unconstitutional requirement of legislative delegation approval.


OUTCOME: The judgment was reversed, and the writ of mandamus was vacated.

 

159 of 275 DOCUMENTS


Sloan v. City of Conway


Opinion No. 25374


SUPREME COURT OF SOUTH CAROLINA


347 S.C. 324; 555 S.E.2d 684; 2001 S.C. LEXIS 184


September 25, 2001, Heard  
November 13, 2001, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal From Horry County. Howard P. King, Circuit Court Judge.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Horry County Circuit Court, South Carolina, granted summary judgment in favor of appellees, city and water authority, in appellant customers' action challenging the city's 1996 ordinance raising water rates for all nonresident customers. The customers appealed.


OVERVIEW: The customers argued that the trial court erred. Under S.C. Code Ann. § 5-31-1910 (1976), the city's contract for its allocated capacity in the plant was sufficient ownership to pass muster. The ownership requirement of the statute simply ensured a municipality's ability to provide water to the persons with whom it contracts. The city owned an allocated capacity of water produced. It had a proprietary interest in the capacity as evidenced by its unilateral ability to sell or lease it. S.C. Code Ann. § 5-31-670 did not expressly apply to nonresident municipal water customers. There was no reasonable rate requirement for water service to nonresidents. The customers were charged the same rates as all other out-of-city customers and the city therefore met its contractual obligation to charge reasonable rates. The city thus breached no fiduciary duty. Even if the customers qualified as third-party beneficiaries of an earlier federal court order, they were deprived of no benefit bestowed by that order. The contractual provision requiring annexation was valid as a means of broadening the city's tax base.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

Rick's Amusement, Inc. v. State


Opinion No. 25359


SUPREME COURT OF SOUTH CAROLINA


351 S.C. 352; 570 S.E.2d 155; 2001 S.C. LEXIS 182


June 6, 2001, Heard  
November 5, 2001, Refiled


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Writ of certiorari denied Rick's Amusement, Inc. v. South Carolina, 535 U.S. 1053, 122 S. Ct. 1909, 152 L. Ed. 2d 819, 2002 U.S. LEXIS 3234 (2002)


PRIOR HISTORY:   [***1] Appeal From Richland County. Kenneth G. Goode, Circuit Court Judge.

 Rick's Amusement, Inc. v. State, 2001 S.C. LEXIS 166 (S.C. Sept. 10, 2001) 
Rick's Amusement, Inc. v. State, 2001 S.C. LEXIS 166 (S.C., Sept. 10, 2001)



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs, video gaming entrepreneurs, sued the state in the Richland County Circuit Court (South Carolina) to recoup losses incurred after 12 counties voted in favor of making payouts illegal pursuant to S.C. Code Ann. § 12-21-2806 (2000) (local option law), which was later found to be unconstitutional. The trial court granted the state's motion to dismiss. The entrepreneurs appealed.


OVERVIEW: The entrepreneurs argued they entered into contracts for placement of video gaming machines before enactment of the local option law and the law illegally impaired the contracts and constituted a taking without compensation. The trial judge held that because future regulations were foreseeable in the highly regulated video poker industry, the entrepreneurs failed to state a takings claim or contract impairment claim. The entrepreneurs argued the trial judge should have evaluated their takings claim under standard takings analysis rather than simply ruling that highly regulated industries were precluded from establishing a takings claim. The supreme court disagreed because the entrepreneurs' rights to cash payouts were dependent upon regulatory licensing and not inherent in the right to own or possess gaming machines. The entrepreneurs argued the trial judge erred by dismissing their contract clause claim. The supreme court disagreed and held that the trial judge properly dismissed the contract clause claim because the entrepreneurs could not have reasonably expected cash payouts for coin-operated video gaming machines to remain legal when they entered into the contracts.


OUTCOME: The supreme court affirmed the decision of the trial court.

 

161 of 275 DOCUMENTS


Horry County Sch. Dist. v. Horry County


Opinion No. 25355


SUPREME COURT OF SOUTH CAROLINA


346 S.C. 621; 552 S.E.2d 737; 2001 S.C. LEXIS 160


January 11, 2001, Heard  
September 4, 2001, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:   [***1] Appeal From Horry County Howard P. King, Circuit Court Judge.



DISPOSITION: AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant school district sued respondents, city and county, challenging their right to allocate certain revenue received in lieu of taxes. The Horry County Circuit Court (South Carolina) granted summary judgment to the city and county. The district appealed.


OVERVIEW: The county initiated creation of a multi-county business park (MCBP) partially within the limits of the city. The school district was coterminous with the county, and the city was located entirely within the county and the school district. Property within the MCBP was constitutionally exempt from ad valorem taxation, but the owners had to pay an amount equal to the taxes otherwise payable, fees in lieu of taxes. The agreement creating the MCBP at issue allocated revenue from MCBP in a lower proportion than the district would have received had the MCBP not been tax exempt. The district argued that S.C. Code §§ 4-1-170, -175, should not have been construed to allow the result. The appellate court, though finding flaws in these statutes, agreed with the trial court's interpretation, and that the statutes as interpreted were constitutional. Reading §§ 4-1-170 and -175 together, there was no requirement that revenues from the fees in lieu of taxes from an MCBP be distributed in the same proportion that it would be if the property were taxable. Further, the MCBP statutes were consistent with the Education Finance Act, S.C. Code Ann. §§ 59-20-10 through -80 (1990 & Supp. 2000).


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

 

162 of 275 DOCUMENTS


Franklin v. Catoe


Opinion No. 25353


SUPREME COURT OF SOUTH CAROLINA


346 S.C. 563; 552 S.E.2d 718; 2001 S.C. LEXIS 159


November 14, 2000, Heard  
August 27, 2001, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Rehearing Denied September 28, 2001. Writ of certiorari denied: Franklin v. Maynard, 2002 U.S. LEXIS 4083 (U.S. June 3, 2002).


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal From Williamsburg County. M. Duane Shuler, Trial Judge, Thomas W. Cooper, Jr., Post-Conviction Judge.



DISPOSITION: REVERSED IN PART.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was found guilty of murder, burglary in the first degree, grand larceny, and criminal sexual conduct in the first degree. He was sentenced to death. The Williamsburg County, South Carolina, trial court granted defendant post-conviction relief and granted the State's request to limit relief to defendant's murder conviction. Both parties appealed.


OVERVIEW: On review, the supreme court found that the trial record, post-conviction relief transcript, affidavits, and depositions supported the trial court's finding that defendant did not knowingly and intelligently waive his statutory right to make a personal closing statement during the guilt phase of his capital murder trial. However, it also found that the trial court erred in granting defendant a new trial because defendant did not show he was prejudiced by the lack of waiver. The fact that defendant appeared before the jury during the guilt phase, even if his appearance was not during the closing statement, demonstrated due process and fundamental fairness were not offended by the error. Considering the evidence of guilt and malice, defendant's testimony, and his attorney's closing argument, there was no reasonable possibility defendant's failure to make a personal closing argument to the jury during the guilt phase of his trial contributed in any way to his convictions. Because there was no reasonable probability that but for counsel's deficient performance, the result of defendant's trial would have been different, defendant suffered no prejudice.


OUTCOME: The trial court's order granting defendant a new trial was reversed and defendant's murder conviction was reinstated.

 

163 of 275 DOCUMENTS


Rollison v. State


Opinion No. 25343


SUPREME COURT OF SOUTH CAROLINA


346 S.C. 506; 552 S.E.2d 290; 2001 S.C. LEXIS 149


April 24, 2001, Submitted  
August 20, 2001, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:   [***1] Appeal From Greenville County. John W. Kittredge, Trial Judge, Larry R. Patterson, Post-Conviction Judge.



DISPOSITION: REVERSED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The State of South Carolina petition for a writ of certiorari to review the Greenville County Circuit Court's order granting defendant post-conviction relief.


OVERVIEW: The post-conviction relief (PCR) judge concluded trial counsel rendered defendant ineffective assistance when she failed to challenge a search and when she allowed him to plead guilty, pursuant to a negotiated agreement, to both first and second offense drug charges in the same proceeding. A reading of the arrest warrant affidavit raised a question regarding the legality of the weapons frisk of defendant, but defendant could not rely upon this document alone to establish his ineffective assistance claim. Defendant also had to show prejudice, that had his trial attorney challenged the frisk, there was reasonable probability that she would have prevailed in a suppression hearing. Assuming that trial counsel's failure to investigate the circumstances surrounding the search fell below an objective standard of reasonableness, defendant did not meet his burden of showing resulting prejudice. Defendant knowingly, intelligently, and voluntarily agreed to plead guilty to both offenses as part of a plea bargain in which the State agreed to drop three other drug charges.


OUTCOME: The judgment was reversed.

 

164 of 275 DOCUMENTS


Tucker v. Catoe


Opinion No. 25332


SUPREME COURT OF SOUTH CAROLINA


346 S.C. 483; 552 S.E.2d 712; 2001 S.C. LEXIS 124


June 7, 2001, Heard  
July 23, 2001, Filed



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Writ of Habeas Corpus Granted.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant filed a writ of habeas corpus after he was convicted of murder and sentenced to death by a jury. The South Carolina Supreme Court granted review of the writ.


OVERVIEW: During jury deliberations during defendant's sentencing, the jurors sent the trial court a note saying they were hopelessly deadlocked at 10-2 for the death sentence. Later, they sent the trial court a note saying they were locked at 11-1 for the death sentence. The trial court did not tell the lawyers the jurors were hopelessly deadlocked or the numbers of the jury split. The trial court returned the jurors to the courtroom and gave them an Allen charge. The jurors soon thereafter returned a death sentence for defendant. On appeal, the supreme court found the Allen charge violated defendant's due process rights. Further, the withholding of pertinent information from the parties, thereby depriving them of the facts necessary to make informed decisions; failing to instruct the jury to omit from its future communication any reference its division; and giving an unconstitutionally coercive Allen charge, with its emphasis on a collective result, shocked the universal sense of justice.


OUTCOME: Writ of habeas corpus was granted and defendant's case was remanded for new sentencing proceeding.

 

165 of 275 DOCUMENTS


State v. Peppers


Opinion No. 25330


SUPREME COURT OF SOUTH CAROLINA


346 S.C. 502; 552 S.E.2d 288; 2001 S.C. LEXIS 128


June 20, 2001, Heard  
July 23, 2001, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:   [***1] Appeal From Laurens County. Larry R. Patterson, Circuit Court Judge.



DISPOSITION: VACATED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant pleaded guilty in the Laurens County Circuit Court (South Carolina) to a violation of S.C. Code Ann. § 20-7-50 (Supp. 1995), prohibiting the neglect of a child or helpless person, with the understanding she could raise various constitutional arguments on appeal. She appealed.


OVERVIEW: Prior to entering a guilty plea, defendant moved to quash the indictment, arguing various constitutional violations. She also moved to quash the arrest warrant on the grounds S.C. Code Ann. § 20-7-50 did not provide adequate notice of the conduct it prohibited. The trial court denied defendant's motions, while assuring defense counsel defendant was "protected" on the record, and accepted defendant's guilty plea. The trial court erred in accepting a conditional guilty plea from defendant. Such a plea could only be unconditional, and when defendant attempted to attach the condition that she would be allowed to raise her constitutional arguments on appeal, the trial court was required to direct a plea of not guilty. Defendant's guilty plea was, therefore, a nullity.


OUTCOME: Defendant's guilty plea and sentence were vacated.

 

166 of 275 DOCUMENTS


In re McCracken


Opinion No. 25323


SUPREME COURT OF SOUTH CAROLINA


346 S.C. 87; 551 S.E.2d 235; 2001 S.C. LEXIS 135


March 7, 2001, Heard  
July 23, 2001, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Rehearing Denied September 12, 2001. As Corrected September 24, 2001.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal From Lexington County. Marc H. Westbrook, Circuit Court Judge.



DISPOSITION: AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Lexington County Circuit Court, South Carolina, found appellant patient to be a sexually violent predator and committed him to the department of mental health. The patient appealed.


OVERVIEW: On review, the supreme court found that the patient failed to meet his burden of proof showing the Sexually Violent Predator Act, S.C. Code Ann. §§ 44-48-10 to -170 (Supp. 2000), on its face was punitive rather than civil in nature. It also found that any alleged unconstitutional confinement would be by writ of habeas corpus; however, since he had been released by the department of mental health, the claim was moot. The supreme court also ruled that there was no error in the general charge, which gave the jury the background of the issue they were being asked to decide, followed by a charge defining the terms which the State had to prove beyond a reasonable doubt. In the recharge, the trial judge did not improperly emphasize any one part of the statute at the expense of another; he simply charged the statute, and where a specific term had a specific definition, charged that definition. Because the supreme court determined that the patient failed to show that his was a criminal proceeding, his effective assistance of counsel claims failed. Further, he failed to show any prejudice, much less a constitutional violation, in the denial of funds to depose unidentified individuals.


OUTCOME: The trial court's judgment was affirmed.

 

167 of 275 DOCUMENTS


Curtis v. State


Opinion No. 25319


SUPREME COURT OF SOUTH CAROLINA


345 S.C. 557; 549 S.E.2d 591; 2001 S.C. LEXIS 133


October 4, 2000, Heard  
July 17, 2001, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Rehearing Denied August 10, 2001. Certiorari Denied March 18, 2002, Reported at: 2002 U.S. LEXIS 1539.
Rehearing denied by, 08/10/2001
Writ of certiorari denied Curtis v. South Carolina, 535 U.S. 926, 152 L. Ed. 2d 208, 122 S. Ct. 1295, 2002 U.S. LEXIS 1539 (2002)
Rehearing denied by Curtis v. South Carolina, 535 U.S. 1074, 152 L. Ed. 2d 856, 122 S. Ct. 1954, 2002 U.S. LEXIS 3536 (2002)
Related proceeding at State v. Curtis, 2004 S.C. LEXIS 1 (S.C., Jan. 5, 2004)


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal From Greenville County. Thomas J. Ervin, Special Circuit Court Judge.



DISPOSITION: AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Greenville County Circuit Court, South Carolina, denied appellant business owner's motion for a temporary injunction concerning the enforcement of S.C. Code Ann. § 16-13-470 (Supp. 2000), which prohibited the selling of urine with the intent to defraud a drug screening test. The business owner appealed.


OVERVIEW: After temporary injunction was denied, the trial judge entered an order holding that S.C. Code Ann. § 16-13-470 was constitutional. Respondent attorney general filed a motion to dismiss the business owner's appeal as moot. The supreme court agreed that the temporary injunction issue was moot but decided to address the merits of the case. It found that § 16-13-470 unconstitutionally shifted the burden of proof on the issue of criminal intent to the defendant and that the unconstitutional intent language was severable from the remainder of the section. The supreme court also ruled that § 16-13-470 was not unconstitutionally vague or overbroad, that it served a legitimate public purpose of creating safety in the workplace, and that it did not prohibit the business owner from dispensing literature regarding his political beliefs on urine testing. The business owner's equal protection claims failed because all individuals who engaged in conduct prohibited by § 16-13-470 were treated alike, regardless of whether they adulterated urine samples with herbal supplements or chemicals. The business owner did not have standing to assert the constitutional rights of his customers.


OUTCOME: The unconstitutional presumption language was severed from the statute. The trial court's order upholding the constitutionality of the remainder of the statute was affirmed.

 

168 of 275 DOCUMENTS


Sea Cabins on the Ocean IV Homeowners Ass'n v. City of N. Myrtle Beach


Opinion No. 25307


SUPREME COURT OF SOUTH CAROLINA


345 S.C. 418; 548 S.E.2d 595; 2001 S.C. LEXIS 106


March 6, 2001, Heard  
June 11, 2001, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Petition for Rehearing Denied July 18, 2001.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal From Horry County. J. Stanton Cross, Jr., Master-in-Equity.



DISPOSITION: AFFIRMED IN RESULT.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioners homeowners' inverse condemnation proceeding alleged respondent city's temporary taking of their private pier for public use without compensation violated U.S. Const. amend. V and S.C. Const. art. I, § 13. The trial court awarded them compensation but the South Carolina Court of Appeals reversed. Petitioners were granted a writ of certiorari.


OVERVIEW: After a hurricane damaged homeowners' pier, they sought a permit to rebuild it. The city eventually issued a permit. Homeowners alleged the loss of use of their property during the years until a permit was issued was an inverse condemnation, entitling them to compensation. During this interim, city passed an ordinance conditioning a permit to rebuild a pier on making the pier publicly accessible, to which homeowners objected. City never enforced the ordinance against homeowners, so it did not create a temporary taking without compensation. The city's initial denial of homeowners' permit application, based on provisions of its zoning ordinance, ultimately reversed by the court of appeals, was not a temporary taking, as homeowners never claimed the ordinance's preclusion of the reestablishment of a legal nonconforming use under certain circumstances was unconstitutional in that it either failed to advance a legitimate governmental interest or denied all economically viable use of the property as a whole. No temporary taking occurred during homeowners' appeal of the city's adverse zoning ruling. Regulatory delay did not create a temporary taking.


OUTCOME: The result of the court of appeals' judgment reversing the trial court's award of compensation was affirmed.
