Ford v. State Ethics Comm'n


Opinion No. 25286


SUPREME COURT OF SOUTH CAROLINA


344 S.C. 642; 545 S.E.2d 821; 2001 S.C. LEXIS 78


March 20, 2001, Heard  
April 23, 2001, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal From Charleston County. Edward B. Cottingham, Circuit Court Judge.



DISPOSITION: AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant, The South Carolina Ethics Commission, appealed the order of the Charleston County Circuit Court, finding that jurisdiction over appellee senator's conduct was vested in the Senate Ethics Committee.


OVERVIEW: Respondent was a sitting member of the South Carolina Senate, and was a member of the Senate in 1998, when the alleged improper conduct occurred. Respondent also ran an activity known as the Black Community Development Committee (BCD). A complaint was lodged with appellant, alleging that respondent and the BCD, a non-candidate, ballot measure committee, had committed various campaign law violations. Thereafter, appellant began an investigation of respondent and then filed a complaint against respondent. Respondent filed a summons and complaint in the trial court, asserting appellant did not have jurisdiction over respondent. The trial court held a hearing and found appellant had jurisdiction over the BCD, but not over respondent's conduct. Since appellant clearly had no jurisdiction over respondent, and the complaint was filed against respondent, the appellate court found the trial court correctly held appellant did not have jurisdiction.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

 

170 of 275 DOCUMENTS


Pinckney v. Warren


Opinion No. 25271


SUPREME COURT OF SOUTH CAROLINA


344 S.C. 382; 544 S.E.2d 620; 2001 S.C. LEXIS 59


January 23, 2001, Heard  
March 26, 2001, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal From Charleston County. Roger M. Young, Master-in-Equity.



DISPOSITION: AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Charleston County Court (South Carolina) held that appellant did not have an interest in the property sold to it by the decedent's son because he did not have a valid interest in the property as he was illegitimate and therefore was not his father's heir at law. Appellant challenged the judgment.


OVERVIEW: The decedent died intestate, leaving an estate including 10 acres of land. The decedent's illegitimate son sold his partial interest in the land to appellant. In respondent's action to quiet title to the 10 acres, the trial court held that the illegitimate son had no valid interest in the property he sold to appellant because he was not an heir at law. On appeal, the instant court affirmed. Even though respondent did not dispute that the son was the decedent's son, his aunt's testimony, along with the absence of a marriage license and his mother's obituary indicated that the decedent was not married to his son's mother when he was born. The son had no right to inherit from the decedent's estate because there was no evidence of a court order establishing paternity or of an instrument signed by the decedent acknowledging his son's paternity prior to his death.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed because the son had no right to inherit from the decedent's estate because his parents were not married when he was born and because there was no court order or an instrument signed by the decedent establishing paternity.

 

171 of 275 DOCUMENTS


State v. Jones


Opinion No. 25257


SUPREME COURT OF SOUTH CAROLINA


344 S.C. 48; 543 S.E.2d 541; 2001 S.C. LEXIS 41


January 11, 2001, Heard  
March 12, 2001, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal From Richland County. H. Dean Hall, Circuit Court Judge.



DISPOSITION: AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant challenged a judgment of the Richland County Circuit Court (South Carolina) convicting him of three counts of armed robbery and possession of a firearm during the commission of a violent crime. He was sentenced to life imprisonment without parole pursuant to S.C. Code Ann. § 17-25-45 (Supp. 2000), commonly known as the Two-Strikes law.


OVERVIEW: The three victims were robbed at gunpoint by an unknown assailant. Three weeks after the robbery, police showed each victim, independently, a photographic line-up of six individuals; all three victims identified appellant as the person who robbed them. Appellant was later convicted of three counts of armed robbery and possession of a firearm and sentenced to life imprisonment without parole. On appeal, appellant contended the three counts of armed robbery should have been rolled into a single count, as the goods were taken from three victims simultaneously. The court held that because there was a threat of bodily injury to each victim from whom property was stolen, appellant could be charged with separate offenses. The court also found no constitutional violation in application of the state's Two-Strikes law to appellant. The sentence appellant received pursuant to the law was proportionate to the sentences imposed on other criminals in South Carolina and on criminals in other jurisdictions for the same offense. There was absolutely nothing in the record supporting appellant's assertion that minorities were affected most by the Two-Strikes law.


OUTCOME: Judgment was affirmed; appellant was properly charged with three separate counts of armed robbery, as though property was taken from the three victims simultaneously, there was a threat of bodily injury to each victim from whom property was stolen. Further, the Two-Strikes law did not violate the state constitution as applied to appellant, as life sentence did not constitute cruel and unusual punishment.

 

172 of 275 DOCUMENTS


Charleston County Parents for Pub. Schs., Inc. v. Moseley


Opinion No. 25231


SUPREME COURT OF SOUTH CAROLINA


343 S.C. 509; 541 S.E.2d 533; 2001 S.C. LEXIS 7


November 1, 2000, Heard  
January 16, 2001, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Counsel and Opinion Amended by the Court January 17, 2001.



DISPOSITION: Directed the County Auditor to levy the millage sufficient to generate $ 86,571,171.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In a proceeding invoking the court's original jurisdiction, plaintiffs asked the court to direct defendant to levy the amount of taxes certified by the Charleston County School District's Board of Trustees pursuant to Act No. 1602, § 11, 1972 S.C. Acts 3131, 3134, even though the tax levy would exceed 90 mills.


OVERVIEW: After giving notice and conducting a public hearing as required by Act No. 1602, § 11, 1972 S.C. Acts 3131, 3134, the Charleston County School District's Board of Trustees (school board) certified to defendant, the county auditor, a tax levy of approximately 99 4/10 (99.4) mills for school district operations. Defendant indicated that she would not levee more than 90 mills without a directive from the instant court. Plaintiffs then filed the instant action asking the court to direct defendant to levy the taxes as certified by the school board. The court concluded: (1) the Charleston County Legislative Delegation was not an indispensable party; (2) the plain language of § 11 was direct and mandatory and required defendant to levy the taxes once the school board had duly complied with the other portions of the act; and (3) nothing in the plain language of § 11 required the legislative delegation's approval before the millage rate could be increased.


OUTCOME: The court granted plaintiffs' request, directing defendant to levy taxes in the amount certified by the school board. State statute delegated to the school board, not the legislative delegation, authority to increase the tax levy. The board complied with the procedural requirements applicable whenever the tax levy would exceed 90 mills.

State v. Aleksey


Opinion No. 25212


SUPREME COURT OF SOUTH CAROLINA


343 S.C. 20; 538 S.E.2d 248; 2000 S.C. LEXIS 219


September 19, 2000, Heard  
November 13, 2000, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Rehearing Denied December 6, 2000. Certiorari Denied May 14, 2001, Reported at: 2001 U.S. LEXIS 3707.
Rehearing denied by, 12/06/2000
Writ of certiorari denied Aleksey v. South Carolina, 532 U.S. 1027, 121 S. Ct. 1974, 149 L. Ed. 2d 766, 2001 U.S. LEXIS 3707 (2001)
Habeas corpus proceeding at, Stay granted by, Motion granted by Aleksey v. Stirling, 2014 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 102891 (D.S.C., July 28, 2014)
Related proceeding at Aleksey v. South Carolina, 135 S. Ct. 1407, 191 L. Ed. 2d 376, 2015 U.S. LEXIS 980 (U.S., 2015)
Habeas corpus proceeding at, Stay granted by Aleksey v. Stirling, 2015 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 109253 (D.S.C., Aug. 19, 2015)


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal From Orangeburg County. Edward B. Cottingham, Circuit Court Judge.



DISPOSITION: AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Criminal appellant challenged a jury's finding of guilt and his sentence of death, entered by the Orangeburg County (South Carolina), in a case where appellant shot a state trooper to death after the trooper had pulled his vehicle over for speeding.


OVERVIEW: Criminal appellant was charged with first-degree murder. His vehicle was pulled over for speeding. A shot was fired, killing the arresting trooper. In custody, appellant spoke with law enforcement officers after being informed of and waiving his Miranda rights; he told the officers that his passenger had shot the trooper. Later, appellant said he wanted to talk further. He implicated himself. The trial court gave a jury instruction on witnesses' credibility which stated the jurors' objective was to seek the truth. Appellant was convicted and sentenced to death. He appealed. The court affirmed. The court held the instruction was not a violation of appellant's due process rights as it did not shift the burden of proof or dilute the reasonable doubt standard. The court also ruled appellant's confession was voluntarily given, the court did not err in refusing appellant the right to cross-examine a witness regarding dismissed narcotics charges having no bearing on truthfulness, and no error occurred in not redacting part of appellant's statement about a contract on his life. Finally, the court held the sentence was not excessive.


OUTCOME: Judgment affirmed because jury instruction on witnesses' credibility did not violate appellant's due process rights, trial court did not err in refusing to suppress appellant's confession, trial court did not err in refusing to allow appellant to cross-examine witness about dismissed indictments involving narcotics charges, and trial court did not err in refusing to redact portions of original statement appellant gave to police.

 

174 of 275 DOCUMENTS


Ward v. State


Opinion No. 25209


SUPREME COURT OF SOUTH CAROLINA


343 S.C. 14; 538 S.E.2d 245; 2000 S.C. LEXIS 213


October 4, 2000, Heard  
November 6, 2000, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Rehearing Denied December 7, 2000.
Related proceeding at Ward v. State, 2003 S.C. LEXIS 298 (S.C., Dec. 8, 2003)


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal From Richland County. Alison Renee Lee, Circuit Court Judge.



DISPOSITION: REVERSED AND REMANDED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff federal retirees appealed dismissal, for failure to exhaust administrative remedies under the South Carolina Revenues Procedures Act, S.C. Code Ann. § 12-60-10 et seq., by Richland County Court (South Carolina), of their declaratory judgment challenge to constitutionality of 1989 S.C. Acts 189, which affected state retirees' tax benefits.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff federal retirees, as a class, sought a declaratory judgment on the constitutionality of 1989 S.C. Acts 189, which abolished state retirees' income tax exemption (to comply with federal law) and simultaneously increased their pension benefits, while doing nothing for federal retirees. Although parties challenging state taxes were normally required to pursue their administrative remedies in compliance with the South Carolina Revenues Procedures Act, S.C. Code Ann. § 12-60-10 et seq., the exhaustion rule was not jurisdictional, and it would not be applied to compel plaintiffs to engage in a futile act. Since only a court could determine the constitutionality of a statute, pursuit of an administrative appeal would have been futile. Therefore plaintiffs were entitled to have their action heard.


OUTCOME: The court reversed and remanded. Exhaustion of remedies did not apply to an action to determine whether a statute was constitutional, because the administrative process would not provide an answer to that issue.

 

175 of 275 DOCUMENTS


Main v. Thomason


Opinion No. 25182


SUPREME COURT OF SOUTH CAROLINA


342 S.C. 79; 535 S.E.2d 918; 2000 S.C. LEXIS 181


March 7, 2000, Heard  
August 14, 2000, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Rehearing Denied September 7, 2000.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal From Charleston County. R. Markley Dennis, Jr., Circuit Court Judge.



DISPOSITION: AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant landowners challenged the judgment of the Charleston County Circuit Court (South Carolina), granting respondent neighbor a temporary license, allowing him access to appellants' property in order to perform repairs on his own property in exchange for paying appellants $ 1,500, arguing that S.C. Code Ann. § 15-67-270 was unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: Respondent requested and was denied permission by appellants for access to appellants' property for the purpose of repairing his home. Respondent sought and was granted a license under S.C. Code Ann. § 15-67-270, giving him access to appellants' property by the trial court. The court affirmed. The court held that § 15-67-270 was constitutional. Section 15-67-270 did not authorize a taking of private property because the temporary license allowed respondent to enter appellants' property only for a temporary period after fully compensating appellants, and because appellants did not lose all economic value of the property because they were still able to use their home despite the license. Section 15-67-270 was a valid exercise of the state's police powers to preserve property. The court also held that § 15-67-270 was not unconstitutionally vague because it provided a general context from which reasonableness of entry could have been determined.


OUTCOME: Judgment affirmed because S.C. Code Ann. § 15-67-270 did not constitute a taking of private property because the statute allowed respondent to enter appellants' property for only a temporary period and because § 15-67-270 was not unconstitutionally vague.

Video Gaming Consultants, Inc. v. South Carolina Dep't of Revenue


Opinion No. 25177


SUPREME COURT OF SOUTH CAROLINA


342 S.C. 34; 535 S.E.2d 642; 2000 S.C. LEXIS 174


June 20, 2000, Heard  
July 31, 2000, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Corrected August 1, 2000.
Costs and fees proceeding at Video Gaming Consultants, Inc. v. S.C. Dep't of Revenue, 2004 S.C. App. LEXIS 117 (S.C. Ct. App., Apr. 26, 2004)


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal From Horry County. J. Stanton Cross, Special Circuit Court Judge.



DISPOSITION: REVERSED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant video game parlor challenged a judgment from the Horry County circuit court's decision holding a ban on advertising in S.C. Code Ann. § 12-21-2804(b)(Supp. 1999) constitutional.


OVERVIEW: Respondent South Carolina Department of Revenue (DOR) issued citations to appellant for violating S.C. Code Ann. § 12-21-2804(b)(Supp. 1999) which banned advertising the playing of gambling and video machines. The DOR could not demonstrate this restriction on commercial speech recited real harms that were alleviated to a material degree. The DOR failed to show the ban would promote its goal of decreasing gambling activity. The court held the statute did not meet the last two prongs in the Central Hudson test--the regulation did not advance the governmental interest asserted, and was more extensive than was necessary to serve that interest, thus the statute was unconstitutional. Administrative law judges were an agency of the executive branch of government and must follow the law as written until its constitutionally was judicially determined. They had no authority to pass upon the constitutionality of a statute or regulation.


OUTCOME: Judgment reversed because the regulation banning advertising of gambling machines did not advance the governmental interest of reducing gambling, and was more extensive than was necessary to serve that interest, and was thus unconstitutional.

WDW Props. v. City of Sumter


Opinion No. 25174


SUPREME COURT OF SOUTH CAROLINA


342 S.C. 6; 535 S.E.2d 631; 2000 S.C. LEXIS 171


June 8, 2000, Heard  
July 24, 2000, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal From Sumter County. Linwood S. Evans, Jr., Master-in-Equity.



DISPOSITION: AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant partnership sought review of a decision of the court of Sumter County (South Carolina), which rejected appellant's claims in a declaratory judgment action challenging the legitimacy of respondent loan program in which the proceeds of tax-exempt bonds would be loaned to respondent developer for renovating retail and commercial properties in a blighted area of respondent city.


OVERVIEW: Respondent city declared its downtown, a slum area, an empowerment zone and designated it as a redevelopment project area eligible for federally tax-exempt local government bonds to finance business enterprises under 26 U.S.C.S. § 1391 et seq. Appellant partnership, which was not eligible for government-sponsored financing and would be in competition for respondent developer's tenants and patrons, asserted respondent loan program did not serve a public purpose as required by the state constitution. The court affirmed the opinion of the lower court, holding that the determination of public purpose was one for the legislative branch and that legislation was not for a private purpose merely because some individual made a profit as a result of the enactment. Elimination of decaying and unhealthy areas within a city directly benefited the public, although private parties within the area might also benefit incidentally. The public would be the primary beneficiary although developers within the empowerment zone would benefit more from the favorable loan rate and the new businesses attracted than those outside the zone.


OUTCOME: The decision of the lower court, which rejected appellant partnership's claims that respondent loan program did not serve a public purpose, was affirmed because respondent loan program, although incidentally benefiting developers through a lower loan rate, ultimately benefited the public by increasing jobs, improving blighted areas, and attracting new businesses to economically distressed areas of respondent city.

 

178 of 275 DOCUMENTS


Westside Quik Shop, Inc. v. Stewart


Opinion No. 25158


SUPREME COURT OF SOUTH CAROLINA


341 S.C. 297; 534 S.E.2d 270; 2000 S.C. LEXIS 151


June 20, 2000, Submitted  
June 21, 2000, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: As Corrected July 11, 2000. Certiorari Denied November 27, 2000, Reported at: 2000 U.S. LEXIS 8074.
Writ of certiorari denied, Motion granted by Westside Quik Shop, Inc. v. Stewart, 531 U.S. 1029, 121 S. Ct. 606, 148 L. Ed. 2d 518, 2000 U.S. LEXIS 8074 (2000)
Related proceeding at Holliday Amusement Co. of Charleston, Inc. v. South Carolina, 2006 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 30244 (D.S.C., May 5, 2006)



DISPOSITION: INJUNCTION DENIED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs, the owners and lessees of video gaming machines used for profit in their places of business, sought to enjoin the enforcement of part I of 1999 S.C. Act No. 125, which made possession of the machines illegal as of July 1, 2000.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiffs contended enforcement of 1999 S.C. Act No. 125 (Act) should have been enjoined because it effected a taking of their video gaming machines without compensation. The machines at issue provided for nonmachine cash payouts; prior to the legislation at issue, the machines were legal. The Act would have amended S.C. Code Ann. § 12-21-2710 (2000) to remove the exemption for video gaming machines, thereby rendering the possession or operation of the machines illegal. Further, under S.C. Code Ann. § 12-21-2712 (2000), the machines would have then been subject to forfeiture and destruction by the state. Under its original jurisdiction, the court denied the injunction, finding that plaintiffs were entitled to no compensation because their machines were lawfully subject to forfeiture as contraband. During the eight months between enactment of the law and its enforceability, plaintiffs had a reasonable period of time to avoid any forfeiture of their property and could not have claimed a compensable taking. Further, there was no taking of plaintiffs' businesses or their real property under either the federal or state constitutions.


OUTCOME: Injunction denied. Plaintiffs were entitled to no compensation for their video gaming machines because their machines were lawfully subject to forfeiture as contraband, and plaintiffs had reasonable time to profitably dispose of the machines out-of-state. There was no taking of either plaintiffs' businesses or real property under either federal or state constitutions.

Hodges v. Rainey


Opinion No. 25149


SUPREME COURT OF SOUTH CAROLINA


341 S.C. 79; 533 S.E.2d 578; 2000 S.C. LEXIS 132


February 15, 2000, Heard  
June 12, 2000, Filed


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner governor requested clarification as to ability to remove respondent board member pursuant to S.C. Code Ann. § 1-3-240(B) (Supp. 1998).


OVERVIEW: Petitioner governor initiated this action to clarify his authority to remove respondent as a member of the Board of Directors of the South Carolina Public Service Authority (board) pursuant to S.C. Code Ann. § 1-3-240(B) (Supp. 1998). Petitioner argued that the court should declare that the members of the board are subject to the petitioner's discretionary removal power under the provisions of the 1993 Restructuring Act and, thus, the executive order removing respondent was effective. The court agreed. S.C. Code Ann. § 1-3-240(B), (C) could be reconciled with S.C. Code Ann. § 58-31-20. Petitioner argued that S.C. Code Ann. § 1-3-240(B) applies to the board because the board is a state agency and, therefore, the office of its board members is a "state office" for purposes of the statute. The court agreed and found that the members of the board were subject to the petitioner's discretionary removal power under S.C. Code Ann. § 1-3-240(B).


OUTCOME: Petitioner's executive order removing respondent was effective. The court found that the members of board of directors were are subject to petitioner's discretionary removal power under S.C. Code Ann. § 1-3-240(B).

 

180 of 275 DOCUMENTS


Harkins v. Greenville County


Opinion No. 25112


SUPREME COURT OF SOUTH CAROLINA


340 S.C. 606; 533 S.E.2d 886; 2000 S.C. LEXIS 147


September 21, 1999, Heard  
June 12, 2000, Refiled


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Petition for Rehearing Denied June 12, 2000.
Certiorari Denied January 22, 2001, Reported at: 2001 U.S. LEXIS 797.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal From Greenville County. Thomas J. Ervin, Circuit Court Judge.

This Opinion Substituted on Denial of Rehearing for Withdrawn Opinion of April 24, 2000, Previously Reported at: 2000 S.C. LEXIS 92.



DISPOSITION: REVERSED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, adult businesses, sought review of a judgment from the Greenville County Court, South Carolina, holding that Greenville Co., S. C., Zoning Ordinance No. 2673, licensing and controlling the location of appellants' businesses, was constitutional. Appellants urged that the ordinance was an unconstitutional prior restraint on speech and that it impermissibly zoned them out of the county.


OVERVIEW: The court reversed a judgment finding Greenville Co., S. C., Zoning Ordinance No. 2673 constitutional and held it to be an unconstitutional prior restraint on speech. The ordinance required appellants, sexually oriented businesses, to obtain a permit from the county zoning administrator in order to operate and controlled where they could locate. Businesses providing non-obscene, sexually explicit material were entitled to protection by the U.S. Const. amend. XIV and I. Applying a two-prong test, the court affirmed the lower court's finding that the licensing decision under the ordinance was made within a reasonable time, and the restriction on location was valid. Construing the ordinance as a whole, the contention that not enough property was available for relocation was unsupported. Furthermore, the trial court's finding that nine relocation sites existed was reasonable and thus could not be disturbed. Nevertheless, the requirement for prompt judicial review of adverse licensing decisions was lacking. There was no guarantee that a judicial determination, meaning a judicial denial of a license, would be rendered within a prescribed period of time.


OUTCOME: The court reversed a judgment holding that an ordinance which licensed and restricted the location of appellants, adult businesses, was constitutional. The challenged ordinance failed to provide for prompt judicial review of adverse licensing decisions. A constitutionally proper scheme required that the licensor make the decision within a reasonable time and that prompt judicial review was available.

Lee v. South Carolina Dep't of Natural Resources


Opinion No. 25095


SUPREME COURT OF SOUTH CAROLINA


339 S.C. 463; 530 S.E.2d 112; 2000 S.C. LEXIS 76


October 7, 1999, Heard  
March 27, 2000, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Petition for Rehearing Denied April 20, 2000.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal From Richland County. James R. Barber, III, Circuit Court Judge.



DISPOSITION: REVERSED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant State challenged a decision by the Richland County Circuit Court (South Carolina) granting summary judgment to appellees in a declaratory judgment action brought by appellees challenging the validity of statutes prohibiting the hunting of "big game" on Sunday in eighteen upstate counties.


OVERVIEW: Appellees, big game hunters, brought a declaratory judgment action against appellant state department of natural resources challenging the validity of statutes prohibiting the hunting of "big game" (turkey, deer, and bear) on Sunday in 18 upstate counties. The circuit court granted summary judgment to appellees on the issues relating to deer and turkey hunting. On appeal, the court reversed, holding that the statutes did not violate the equal protection clause. The court held that the Sunday hunting ban had a reasonable relation to the legislative purpose of the enforcement of big game hunting laws.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the circuit court. The circuit court erred in finding an equal protection violation. The ban was rationally related to the protection of game.

 

182 of 275 DOCUMENTS


Hagy v. Pruitt(In re Pruitt)


Opinion No. 25091


SUPREME COURT OF SOUTH CAROLINA


339 S.C. 425; 529 S.E.2d 714; 2000 S.C. LEXIS 69


February 15, 2000, Heard  
March 20, 2000, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal From Horry County. Clyde K. Laney, Jr., Family Court Judge.



DISPOSITION: AFFIRMED AS MODIFIED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs, mother and father of child, commenced action to set aside adoption of their daughter by defendants, who were plaintiff mother's father and stepmother. Family court overturned the adoption. Court of Appeals (South Carolina) reversed, holding action was time barred under S.C. Code Ann. § 20-7-1800 (Supp. 1998). Plaintiffs appealed.


OVERVIEW: In action to set aside adoption of daughter, plaintiffs mother and father commenced action against defendants, who were plaintiff mother's father and stepmother. Family court overturned the adoption. Court of appeals reversed, holding action was time barred under S.C. Code Ann. § 20-7-1800 (Supp. 1998). Plaintiffs appealed, asserting consent to adoption was obtained by fraud. The court held § 20-7-1800 (Supp. 1998), which provided decree could not be set aside for any reason after one year, did not preclude setting aside adoption if extrinsic fraud existed. However, fraud was not shown by clear and convincing evidence. Plaintiff mother signed consent after being advised by two attorneys that adoption was irrevocable and parental rights would be terminated. Consent was not revocable on ground she was a minor, S.C. Code Ann. § 20-7-1690(E) (Supp. 1999). The decision reversing the family court was affirmed, as modified.


OUTCOME: The decision of the court of appeals, reversing family court's order setting aside the adoption, was affirmed, as modified to reflect that the then statute of limitations did not preclude setting aside the adoption if extrinsic fraud was shown. Plaintiffs failed to show extrinsic fraud by clear and convincing evidence.

 

183 of 275 DOCUMENTS


Staubes v. City of Folly Beach


Opinion No. 25089


SUPREME COURT OF SOUTH CAROLINA


339 S.C. 406; 529 S.E.2d 543; 2000 S.C. LEXIS 67


January 20, 2000, Heard  
March 20, 2000, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal From Charleston County. James E. Lockemy, Circuit Court Judge.



DISPOSITION: AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner city challenged decision by the Court of Appeals (South Carolina), which reversed the grant of summary judgment in favor of respondent property owner on negligence claim, which had been granted by the circuit court.


OVERVIEW: Respondent owned a building in petitioner city, which was destroyed by a hurricane. He obtained a permit from petitioner city to repair his building. Petitioner revoked the permit and issued a condemnation order in an abuse of its discretion, and respondent alleged several claims against petitioner. Petitioner did not plead a negligence cause of action in his complaint, but it was preserved for review as it was clearly raised at the trial court and petitioner had impliedly consented to the presentation of the negligence claim for purposes of the summary judgment hearing. Petitioner was not prejudiced by treating the pleadings as amended to include the negligence claim where petitioner was the one who first raised the issue of negligence. Petitioner did not have absolute immunity under the South Carolina Tort Claims Act, S.C. Code Ann. § 15-78-10 to -200 (Supp. 1999).


OUTCOME: Decision to reverse summary judgment on the negligence cause of action affirmed; the issue was preserved for review because it was tried by implied consent, and claim was allowed under the general provisions of the South Carolina Tort Claims Act.

 

184 of 275 DOCUMENTS


Jernigan v. State


Opinion No. 25087


SUPREME COURT OF SOUTH CAROLINA


340 S.C. 256; 531 S.E.2d 507; 2000 S.C. LEXIS 94


December 15, 1999, Submitted  
March 13, 2000, Filed; May 1, 2000, Refiled


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal From Lexington County. Marc H. Westbrook, Post-Conviction Judge.

This Opinion Substituted on Denial of Rehearing for Withdrawn Opinion of March 13, 2000, Previously Reported at: 2000 S.C. LEXIS 63.



DISPOSITION: VACATED AND REMANDED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The court granted a writ of certiorari to review the summary dismissal of petitioner's post-conviction relief action by a Lexington County (South Carolina) court. The court granted the writ to determine whether a change from annual parole review to biannual review for violent offenders violated the Ex Post Facto Clause.


OVERVIEW: The court granted a petition for a writ of certiorari to determine whether a change from annual parole review to biannual review for violent offenders violated the Ex Post Facto Clause. There were two issues on review: (1) did the post-conviction relief court err in summarily dismissing petitioner's claim, and (2) did the change from annual parole review to biannual review violate the Ex Post Facto Clause? As to the first issue, the court held that, as a non-collateral matter, petitioner's ex post facto claim should be remanded to the appropriate agency to allow it to make a final decision on petitioner's claim. As to the second issue, the court agreed that the change in petitioner's parole review to a biannual review constituted an ex post facto violation. The court found that S.C. Code Ann. § 24-21-645 offended S.C. Const. art. I, § 4, even if the federal constitution was not offended. The court held that the change from annual parole eligibility review to biannual review produced a sufficient risk of increasing the measure of punishment attached to the covered crimes, and therefore, any retroactive application of the statute constituted an ex post facto violation.


OUTCOME: The post conviction relief court's decision was vacated and remanded because retroactive application of the statute changing parole review from an annual review to a biannual review constituted an ex post facto violation.

 

185 of 275 DOCUMENTS


R.L. Jordan Co. v. Boardman Petroleum, Inc.


Opinion No. 25067


SUPREME COURT OF SOUTH CAROLINA


338 S.C. 475; 527 S.E.2d 763; 2000 S.C. LEXIS 36


October 5, 1999, Heard  
February 14, 2000, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Henry M. Herlong, Jr., United States District Judge.



DISPOSITION: CERTIFIED QUESTION REMANDED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The United States District Court for the District of South Carolina certified the question regarding whether S.C. Code Ann. § 39-5-325(A) was unconstitutional because it violated the substantive due process guarantees of S.C. Const. art. I, § 3, in a case involving defendant's alleged violation of the statute.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff brought suit in district court, alleging defendant had violated the motor fuel pricing statute, S.C. Code Ann. § 39-5-325(A), at two of its gas stations, requiring plaintiff to reduce prices at its nearby station. Defendant moved for judgment on the pleadings, alleging the statute violated the state constitution's due process clause. In answering the certified question, regarding whether S.C. Code Ann. § 39-5-325(A) was unconstitutional, the court overruled its cases which applied the Lochner approach. The court adopted the modern standard, of upholding statutes that bear a reasonable relationship to any legitimate interest of government, for reviewing all substantive due process challenges to state statutes. The court declined to decide whether the motor fuel pricing statute was reasonably related to any legitimate state goal, and remanded the matter for further proceedings in light of the new standard it adopted.


OUTCOME: Certified question remanded for further proceedings in light of the new standard for reviewing all substantive due process challenges to state statutes that the court adopted, upholding statutes that bear a reasonable relationship to any legitimate interest of government.

 

186 of 275 DOCUMENTS


State v. 192 Coin-Operated Video Game Machs.


Opinion No. 25061


SUPREME COURT OF SOUTH CAROLINA


338 S.C. 176; 525 S.E.2d 872; 2000 S.C. LEXIS 30


October 7, 1999, Heard  
February 7, 2000, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Certiorari Denied October 2, 2000, Reported at: 2000 U.S. LEXIS 5115. Rehearing Denied March 9, 2000.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal From Greenville County. Thomas J. Ervin, Circuit Court Judge.



DISPOSITION: AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant challenged the Greenville County Circuit Court's (South Carolina) affirmation of destruction orders pursuant to S.C. Code Ann. § 12-21-2710 (Supp. 1998) for illegal gambling machines from appellant's warehouses.


OVERVIEW: Appellee state law enforcement division seized illegal gambling machines from warehouses belonging to appellant. Two magistrates found the machines to be illegal contraband under S.C. Code Ann. § 12-21-2710 (Supp. 1998) and ordered them destroyed. The circuit court affirmed both destruction orders. On appeal, appellant argued that possession of the machines was not unlawful because the federal Gambling Devices Transportation Act, 15 U.S.C.S. § 1172(a) (1997), preempts the state law, and because the machines must be operational to violate the law. The court affirmed the circuit court's affirmation of the magistrate's destruction orders. The court concluded that the Gambling Devices Transportation Act did not preempt state law because it regulated transportation of gambling equipment, not possession or operation. Furthermore, the plain language of the state law made clear that the legislature makes possession a violation in itself, separate from the crime of operation.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the circuit court's affirmation of the magistrate's destruction orders. The court concluded that possession of the machines was unlawful because the federal Gambling Devices Transportation Act did not preempt state law, and because gambling devices need not be operational before they are subject to seizure and destruction.

 

187 of 275 DOCUMENTS


State v. Benton


Opinion No. 25040


SUPREME COURT OF SOUTH CAROLINA


338 S.C. 151; 526 S.E.2d 228; 2000 S.C. LEXIS 4


November 16, 1999, Heard  
January 10, 2000, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Certiorari Denied May 30, 2000, Reported at: 2000 U.S. LEXIS 3736.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal From Charleston County. Costa M. Pleicones, Circuit Court Judge.



DISPOSITION: Appellant's conviction and sentence AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant challenged the judgment of the Charleston County Court (South Carolina), which convicted appellant of first-degree burglary under S.C. Code Ann. § 16-11-311(A) after refusing appellant's motion to require respondent to accept appellant's stipulation to prior burglary convictions.


OVERVIEW: On trial for first-degree burglary under S.C. Code Ann. § 16-11-311(A), which required a history of two or more burglary convictions as an element of the statute, appellant offered to stipulate that he had two prior burglary convictions in lieu of respondent introducing evidence of the convictions. Respondent refused to stipulate and the trial court declined to require respondent to accept. Subsequent to his conviction, appellant argued that admission of the prior crimes was highly prejudicial and deprived him of due process because it diluted respondent's burden of proof as to other statutory elements. The court disagreed, holding that respondent was required to prove all elements of the statute despite its ability to offer evidence of prior convictions. Additionally, the court held that the probative value of admitting the prior convictions outweighed its prejudicial effect, due to analogous U.S. Supreme Court case law allowing admission of other crimes to establish a material element of the offense charged.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed because allowing respondent to introduce evidence of appellant's prior burglary convictions did not dilute respondent's burden of proving the remaining elements under the burglary statute.

193 of 275 DOCUMENTS


Wilson v. Willis


Opinion No. 5387


COURT OF APPEALS OF SOUTH CAROLINA


416 S.C. 395; 786 S.E.2d 571; 2016 S.C. App. LEXIS 25


December 8, 2015, Heard 
March 2, 2016, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Rehearing denied by Wilson v. Willis, 2016 S.C. App. LEXIS 76 (S.C. Ct. App., June 24, 2016)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal From Abbeville County Eugene C. Griffith, Jr., Circuit Court Judge. Appellate Case No. 2014-000946.



DISPOSITION: REVERSED AND REMANDED.


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: HOLDINGS: [1]-In a multi-count action against insurers, arising from insurance agents' alleged illegal and improper tactics to destroy competition, a valid and binding contract containing an arbitration provision (AP) existed based on an agency agreement (AA) between the insurers and the insurance agency, as such AA did not have to be signed by all parties and the agency and its agents clearly accepted and acted upon the AA; [2]-Moreover, the statute of frauds, S.C. Code Ann. § 32-3-10 (2007), was inapplicable because performance was possible within a one-year period; [3]-The matter was subject to arbitration because the AP encompassed the claims, in that they were related to and in connection with the AA, the insureds and agents were equitably estopped from arguing that their status as nonsignatories precluded enforcement of the AP, and the insurers had not waived the right to arbitrate.


OUTCOME: Order reversed; matter remanded with instructions to grant insurers' motions to dismiss claims and compel arbitration.

Spur at Williams Brice Owners Ass'n, Inc. v. Lalla

PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal From Richland County. Clifton Newman, Circuit Court Judge. Appellate Case No. 2013-001479.



DISPOSITION: AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: ISSUE: Whether the trial court erred in granting declaratory relief to appellee owners association in its suit to enforce a restrictive covenant banning appellants from renting their unit to college students who were not related to them. HOLDINGS: [1]-The court did not err in ruling that when appellants purchased the unit, they voluntarily and intentionally bound themselves by the restrictive covenant and that its rental ban was binding on them; [2]-The restrictive covenant satisfied the state and federal Equal Protection Clauses because college students were not a suspect class since, inter alia, they had not faced a long history of discrimination, and there was a rational basis for the classification: to ensure the comfort and safety of the complex's residents and to protect property owners' investments by minimizing the risk of creating a dormitory-like atmosphere at the complex.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

Affirmed in part and modified in part by State v. Griffin, 416 S.C. 266, 785 S.E.2d 786, 2016 S.C. LEXIS 102 (S.C., 2016)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal From Greenwood County Thomas L. Hughston Jr., Circuit Court Judge. Appellate Case No. 2012-213602.



DISPOSITION: AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: ISSUE: Whether a circuit court erred in denying defendant's motion to dismiss, in which he asserted that he was unlawfully stopped, seized, detained, and arrested by deputies who had not been duly qualified to serve as deputy sheriffs. HOLDINGS: [1]-The county sheriff's office deputies were to be considered de facto deputies, despite the lack of compliance with the requirements of S.C. Code Ann. §§ 23-13-10 and 23-13-20 (2007), because all of the deputies who participated in defendant's capture and arrest had been employed with the sheriff's office for a significant number of years and stated that they were bonded and had taken an oath for every sheriff for whom they had worked. Moreover, at the time of defendant's capture and arrest, the deputies were performing duties consistent with their appointments as deputies and were identifiable to defendant as deputy sheriffs who had authority.


OUTCOME: Judgment affirmed.

State v. Johnson


Opinion No. 5246
